        Critical Approaches to Fieldwork

1011

2
3111    In Critical Approaches to Fieldwork Gavin Lucas provides a fundamental
4       examination of the historical and conceptual framework within which
5       archaeology is practised today. Drawing on the development of the disci-
6       pline since the nineteenth century, the relation between theoretical
7       paradigms and everyday archaeological practice is critically explored.
8          This work takes as its starting point the role of ﬁeldwork and how this
9       has changed over the past 150 years. The author argues against progressive
20111   accounts of ﬁeldwork and instead places it in its broader intellectual context.
1       From this, a number of key structural changes are identiﬁed in archaeo-
2       logical practice which correlate interestingly with the emergence of
3       sub-divisions within the discipline, such as ﬁnds specialisms, area/period
4       research and theoretical/methodological specialities. It is argued that such
5       structural divisions within archaeology have major theoretical consequences
6       which need to be addressed. This work contributes greatly to this emerging
7       discussion.
8          In providing a much-needed historical and critical evaluation of current
9       practice in archaeology, this book opens up a topic of debate which affects
30111   all archaeologists, whatever their particular interests. This will be essential
1       reading for all current and future archaeologists.
2
3       Gavin Lucas is currently working for the Cambridge Archaeological Unit
4       involved in directing excavation and post-excavation programmes.

        Chapter 1
1111
2
        Introduction
3       Archaeology and the field
4
5111
6
7
8
9
1011
1
2
3111    Not so long ago I was working on a small trial excavation in the village
4       of Castor in eastern England; it involved cutting a narrow trench into a
5       beautiful old orchard garden backing on to a churchyard in order to ﬁnd
6       evidence for a Roman palace which once occupied almost the whole village.
7       In the end, we did ﬁnd the remains of Roman buildings on a terrace, as
8       well as a great deal of subsequent occupation which ceased sometime in
9       the twelfth or thirteenth century when the area became part of the church-
20111   yard. The process of excavation involved using a machine to strip off the
1       garden soil, followed by hand digging with mattocks, spades, shovels and
2       trowels. In the process, we sought to identify separate deposits marked by
3       differences in their composition, deposits such as slopewash, ﬂoor layers,
4       pit ﬁlls, walls and so on. Each of these was described on separate record
5       sheets accompanied by measured drawings to scale, and identiﬁed by a
6       unique number; any artefacts or other remains such as animal bones or
7       shells were bagged and labelled according to the deposit they came from.
8       Critical to the whole process was understanding both what any deposit
9       represented and what its relationship was to other deposits, i.e. earlier, later
30111   or contemporary.
1          After excavation, all the ﬁnds and records were taken back, put in order
2       and checked through; the different ﬁnds – the pottery, the animal bones,
3       the coins, etc. – were sent for study to different specialists, each of whom
4       analysed the material in certain ways and produced a report. For example,
5       the ceramicist sorted out all the sherds into different types of vessel based
6       on their fabric and form, quantiﬁed this information and at the end was
7       able to say what kind of vessels were represented from the site, what period
8       they dated from, and where they were made. On this site, most of the
9       pottery came from local kilns, but some came from other places such as
40111   France, and most could be dated to the latter part of the Roman period.
1       This and the other specialist information was then integrated with the
2       records made on site to produce a narrative which aimed to establish the
3       sequence and nature of events which left their trace under that old orchard
44111   garden about 1,500 years ago.
        2   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111       I have just described very approximately what happens on innumerable
2       archaeological sites in Britain and all over the world; the precise proce-
3       dures might differ, but basically they share similar goals. How is it that we
4       use these procedures? Why do we do it in this way rather than in any
5       other? I ask these questions because I think there has been a notable lack
6       of concern for them in the recent wider theoretical debates about archae-
7       ology, in particular with the development of post-processual approaches in
8       the past ten to ﬁfteen years. While one can point to changes in practice
9       which New Archaeology effected – from ﬁeld survey and sampling tech-
1011    niques to statistical representations of data – can post-processualism be said
1       to have had such an impact on everyday practice? This may seem a harsh
2       statement, since post-processualism is certainly not a purely theoretical,
3       armchair exercise (as some might believe) and its studies are as data-driven
4       as any processual work. But this is not the point. How much has post-
5       processual theory actually altered the everyday practice of archaeology?
6       When I consider the investigation of the Roman palace described above,
7       I cannot think of any way in which post-processualism has effected the
8       process, yet there are numerous ways in which New Archaeology has. To
9       me, this points to a serious lapse in critical thinking about what we do.
20111   Why has this occurred? I am not the ﬁrst to raise this issue of course (Tilley
1       1989; Hodder 1989b, 1997; Richards 1995), but one of the aims of this
2       book is to bring the issue to greater prominence through an examination
3       of the historical and conceptual framework within which archaeology is
4       practised today.
5          I want to begin by asking, perhaps strangely, why do we even go into
6       the ﬁeld at all? This may seem rather paradoxical, but the very idea of
7       ﬁeldwork is not necessarily synonymous with archaeology as Chris Tilley
8       has argued – ‘digging as a pathology of archaeology’ (Tilley 1989: 275).
9       However, Tilley’s main point is not so much directed towards excavation
30111   itself as to the purpose behind it and he believes this should shift from
1       being ‘a process whereby the material traces of the past are recovered and
2       “rescued” to being an exercise in a very different kind of production: the
3       manner in which interpretive experience is produced’ (Tilley 1989: 278).
4       His work with Barbara Bender and Sue Hamilton on Bodmin Moor exem-
5       pliﬁes this (Bender et al. 1997), and recent work by Ian Hodder at
6       Çatalhöyük addresses similar issues in terms of a reﬂexive methodology
7       (Hodder 1997). Both these projects are innovative, positive moves towards
8       reconceiving ﬁeldwork and are discussed further below. But what is the
9       historical context of their reaction? Why do we go into the ﬁeld, how do
40111   we decide what constitutes ﬁeldwork and post-ﬁeldwork, and why do we
1       do things in certain ways in or out of the ﬁeld?
2
3
44111
                                            Introduction: archaeology and the field   3

1111    The ‘field’ in archaeology
2
3       The significance of the field
4       In the paper referred to above, Tilley remarked that much emphasis is given
5       to methods in textbooks, but less on what the end-product is supposed to
6       be (Tilley 1989: 275). What is the status of the ‘ﬁeld’ in archaeology? We
7       might say that without ﬁeldwork, there would be no material to work on,
8       and therefore no archaeology – it is the bread and butter of all archaeol-
9       ogists, even those who do not go into the ﬁeld themselves. Whether studying
1011    transistor radios or palaeolithic hand axes, archaeologists need ﬁeldwork to
1
        sustain what they do, to produce the very material or ‘data’ on which to
2
        work. And yet this is perhaps rather too simple. Fieldwork does not just
3111
        mean the recovery of objects; ﬁeldwork is, as Tilley is at pains to point
4
        out, an interpretive exercise, an experience. There is a whole unwritten
5
        mythology about the nature of ﬁeldwork.
6
           Fieldwork, as part of a professional practice performed by the scientists
7
        themselves, emerged in the later nineteenth century primarily among natu-
8
9       ralists and geologists but also among archaeologists and, slightly later,
20111   anthropologists (Kuklick 1997: 48). Earlier, most researchers had relied
1       heavily on material brought back by travellers or specially commissioned
2       collectors; going into the ﬁeld was not regarded as the proper activity of a
3       gentleman, aspects of both class and commodiﬁcation being implicated in
4       this. This, however, was determined by the proximity of the material. In
5       terms of exotica (whether plants, people or artefacts), reliance on others
6       was largely the case, but with specimens closer to home there was a great
7       tradition of gentleman scholars going into the ﬁeld. In Britain, for example
8       there was the antiquarian topographic work of William Camden, John
9       Aubrey and William Stukeley followed by that of Cunnington and Colt
30111   Hoare, for whom surveying was seen as part of the culture of a ‘gentleman’
1       (Piggott 1976: 111; also see Ashbee 1972).1 Different European countries
2       had their own differing emphases, but most (except France) had a history
3       of antiquarians who went into the ﬁeld (Malina and Vašíček 1990: 27;
4       Schnapp 1993: 154). However, it is questionable how far one can call the
5       work of these antiquarians ﬁeldwork in the sense of modern archaeology.
6          It is interesting to see that, particularly in the seventeenth and eighteenth
7       centuries, antiquarian researches were frequently part of the wider study of
8       natural history, and archaeological remains are discussed alongside ﬂora,
9       fauna, and other aspects of the environment (Schnapp 1993: 198). For
40111   example the antiquarian Robert Plot’s works were entitled The Natural
1       History of Oxford-shire (1677) and The Natural History of Stafford-shire (1686),
2       and many of the early local society journals were similarly ecumenical in
3       their coverage and entitlement. This idea of a regional approach, based on
44111   a topographic and environmental framework became particularly a hallmark
        4   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    of the British antiquarian tradition – the work of Cunnington and Colt
2       Hoare in the late eighteenth/early nineteenth centuries was founded on the
3       same basic approach as that of Stukeley: topographic survey. Excavation was
4       supplemental, merely an exercise in the recovery of artefacts, and ultimately,
5       without written records, such things were often considered mute (Trigger
6       1989: 70). There was no real consideration of how to engage with the mate-
7       rial – it was merely there, attesting to an incomprehensible antiquity. The
8       association between antiquarian studies and natural history, however, was
9       perhaps most marked with prehistoric remains; historical and classical anti-
1011    quarianism was much more closely linked to art history and was perhaps a
1       stronger theme in early ﬁeld research in mainland Europe than in Britain
2       (Malina and Va šíček 1990: 27). The eighteenth- and early nineteenth-
3       century excavations in Pompeii and Herculaneum or in Mesopotamia and
4       Egypt are obvious examples.
5          The real advances were made, not by those who did go into the ﬁeld, but
6       by those who largely stayed at home and examined material collected by
7       others, whether this came from abroad or closer to home. The major later
8       nineteenth-century archaeologists such as Daniel Wilson, John Lubbock and
9       John Evans did not really conduct ﬁeldwork but relied on collections,
20111   and when they did go into the ‘ﬁeld’ this was rarely to initiate controlled
1       excavation but rather to visit sites and monuments. Typology more than
2       ﬁeldwork characterises the emergence of archaeology as an academic disci-
3       pline. There are exceptions to this mode of work however – palaeolithic
4       archaeology on the one hand which, from being closely allied to geology, did
5       engage in ﬁeldwork to a greater degree, and, on the other, individuals such
6       as J. J. A. Worsaae in Denmark or those working in the Near East such as
7       Paul-Emile Botta and Austen Layard, or Heinrich Schliemann in Greece and
8       Turkey. However, these exceptions need to be qualiﬁed.
9          Thus palaeolithic archaeology either involved observations of sections
30111   already cut, as with Boucher de Perthes’ work in the Somme Valley, or
1       purposive investigation as with William Pengelly at Brixham Cave. Yet,
2       ironically, the sites in the Somme valley only gained academic credence
3       after they had been visited by a stream of famous British scientists including
4       John Evans, John Prestwich and Charles Lyell in 1859. The Académie des
5       Sciences, when ﬁrst presented with de Perthes’ results in 1846, rejected
6       them (Trigger 1989: 93–4; Schnapp 1993: 311–14). A remarkably similar
7       scenario occurred with the question of palaeolithic occupation in North
8       America, where investigations at the Trenton Gravels were subject to a
9       visit by most of the principal ﬁgures in American archaeology. However,
40111   in contrast to Europe, the ﬁndings were much more equivocal and, in
1       the end, were rejected (Meltzer 1985: 254–5). The situation with other
2       nineteenth-century ﬁeldworkers is more complex. I discuss this in more
3       detail in the next chapter, but Schliemann and Worsaae, despite their
44111   more careful techniques and records, have in common the central focus of
                                            Introduction: archaeology and the field   5

1111    artefacts and the retrieval of a ‘collection’ which places them in close align-
2       ment to that ‘father’ of ﬁeld archaeology, General Pitt Rivers.
3          What emerges from this, though, is a division between the ﬁeldworkers
4       and the intellectual elite, which more or less reproduces the same division
5       between the person who collected the material and the museum or indi-
6       vidual who commissioned the expedition. In Britain, much of the ﬁeldwork
7       in the latter part of the nineteenth century was conducted on prehistoric
8       burial mounds, and major ﬁeldworkers such as William Greenwell, Thomas
9       Bateman and John Mortimer are not known for their interpretive contri-
1011    bution to archaeology – indeed in most histories they may get only a passing
1       mention in comparison to ﬁgures such as Daniel Wilson and John Lubbock.
2       Indeed, both Greenwell and Bateman commissioned other people to exca-
3111    vate barrows for them (the most famous of whom was James Ruddock) as
4       well as paying locals to collect surface ﬂints. In the US, the American
5       Bureau of Ethnology and the Peabody Museum were the major sponsors of
6       ﬁeldwork. Although Putnam at the Peabody is renowned for his own ﬁeld-
7       work, both he and John Wesley Powell frequently commissioned others to
8       conduct ﬁeldwork for them (Hinsley 1974, 1985).
9          Part of this division relates to the fact that archaeology was part of the
20111   wider discipline of the science of man, or anthropology, not only in
1       the USA but also in Britain. The idea of a distinct profession of archae-
2       ologists was not fully developed, and ﬁeldwork was highly variable because
3       of the people doing it – all were effectively amateurs, though towards the
4       end of the century there was an increasing concern for professionalisation
5       (Hinsley 1974; Levine 1986: 31, 87). Fieldwork thus played an ambiguous
6       part in archaeology in the middle of the nineteenth century – if there was
7       anything in common in archaeological practice, it was the ‘collection’ rather
8       than the ‘ﬁeld’, and more particularly the association between the collec-
9       tions and museums and systems of classiﬁcation (Cole 1974; Chapman 1989;
30111   Jacknis 1985). The deﬁning feature of archaeological work before the 1870s
1       was this focus on collections rather than the observation of material in situ
2       (Kehoe 1998: 34), and, even when this did occur, it was, as with geology,
3       from a desire to answer questions posed by these collections. Most archae-
4       ologists, as well as naturalists, stayed at home or at the academy waiting
5       for the material to come to them.
6          It is even debatable how much the growth in ﬁeldwork after the 1870s
7       was not still very much in the same mode – I develop this point further
8       in the next chapter. Someone such as the so-called father of archaeolog-
9       ical ﬁeldwork, Pitt Rivers, may not have been quite as we paint him. It
40111   could be argued that ﬁeldwork as we understand it today has more in
1       common with the work of Mortimer Wheeler in Britain or Alfred Kidder
2       in the USA and with developments in the early decades of the twentieth
3       century, especially when we consider how it was only at this time that
44111   ethnographic ﬁeldwork took on its modern character through the mythic
        6   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    ﬁgure of Bronislaw Malinowski (Stocking 1983). One could argue that the
2       dominant concern with universalising classiﬁcation and evolution meant
3       that ﬁeldwork was still viewed primarily as a means of enhancing the collec-
4       tion rather than an interest in the site itself, and only when the idea of
5       culture as particularistic emerged – that is, culture history or culture
6       groups/areas – did ﬁeldwork become more relevant. The very concern for
7       particularity meant that presence on the site and the site’s own particu-
8       larity became important too. As long as one views culture as a universal
9       concept, the best place to appraise it is perhaps the armchair – going into
1011    the ﬁeld potentially achieves nothing (Gupta and Ferguson 1997: 8).
1          Franz Boas’s early involvement in museum work in the USA is a good
2       example of this shift in thinking – he attempted to restructure the way
3       collections were organised at the American Museum, an approach which
4       epitomises the Boasian revolution in American anthropology (Jacknis 1985:
5       77–83; also see McVicker 1992). In contrast to Otis Mason’s or William
6       Henry Holmes’s method of organisation which placed objects in typolog-
7       ical groups, Boas argued for objects to be arranged according to tribal or
8       cultural criteria; for example, instead of showing different examples of rattles
9       together, he suggested each should be placed alongside other objects from
20111   the same tribal group. It was not that Mason or Holmes did not see
1       the relevance of this approach, but rather that they gave precedence to the
2       typological one, arguing that it told much more about the objects that any
3       cultural grouping (Jacknis 1985). Boas’s approach eventually led him to see
4       museums and collections in general as of limited value, in that they restrict
5       the dimensions of cultural life available for study. It ultimately led to the
6       development of a new kind of ﬁeldwork, one based not on simply enhancing
7       collections but on a concern with a more contextual understanding of
8       cultural lifeways. One can easily see how this is translated into ﬁeldwork,
9       with a greater concern for the speciﬁc spatial and temporal contexts of
30111   objects. The primary locus of research necessarily shifts from museums and
1       studies to the ﬁeld (Boone 1993: 330).
2
3
        The politics of the field
4
5       When scientiﬁc ﬁeldwork ﬁrst developed in the later nineteenth century,
6       the broader cultural background certainly fostered its development. Owning
7       or studying collections was often a way of increasing one’s social status
8       (Levine 1986: 11) and, in terms of ﬁeldwork, the idea of travel as part of
9       personal – more speciﬁcally masculine – growth encouraged scientists to
40111   acquire their own collections (Kuklick 1997). Even for the archaeologist
1       who worked at home rather than abroad (never such a romantic ﬁgure),
2       the ‘Field’ remains an arena of personal development with deﬁnite machismo
3       undertones. Even if there are no dangerous animals or tribes, it is, at the
44111   very least, awful weather. Of course a touch of irony is intended here, but
                                            Introduction: archaeology and the field   7

1111    in the early days of archaeology the element of personality was prominent
2       in those texts dealing with ﬁeldwork, and the deﬁnition of masculinity came
3       to be implicated in ﬁeldwork practices. J. P. Droop’s small book Archaeological
4       Excavation (1915) contains the infamous epilogue on ‘women on site’, in
5       which he states that ‘mixed excavations’ are a bad idea, but that if women
6       have to be present they ought to be kept separate (Droop 1915: 63–4).
7          The issue of gender in ﬁeldwork is deeply implicated in the wider accep-
8       tance of women into archaeology. Before the issuing of formal degrees,
9       ﬁeldwork was the crucial step towards a career in archaeology, and there
1011    was much opposition to women going into ‘the ﬁeld’ (Díaz-Andreu and
1       Stig Sørensen 1997: 8; also see Dincauze 1992). There were, however,
2       women who did go – women such as the Swede Hanna Rydh or the
3111    American Harriet Boyd Hawkins early in the twentieth century and, later,
4       British Kathleen Kenyon. There were also the less visible wives (Hilda
5       Petrie, Tessa Wheeler and Madeleine Kidder) of famous male archaeolo-
6       gists. In fact, wives of archaeologists were often expected to accompany
7       their spouses into the ﬁeld, though often to deal with ﬁnds rather than
8       help with excavation (Reyman 1992: 74). The extension of the exclusion
9       of women from the ﬁeld was the rejection of any ‘feminine’ characteristics
20111   in those men intending to work in archaeology. Flinders Petrie’s Methods
1       and Aims in Archaeology (1904), perhaps the ﬁrst manual on ﬁeldwork in
2       English, begins with a chapter on the character of the archaeologist, with
3       attention to bodily appearance:
4
5           his readiness [to dig] should be shown by the shortness of his ﬁnger-
6           nails and the toughness of his skin . . . one might as well try to play
7           the violin in a pair of gloves as proffer to excavate with clean ﬁngers
8           and a pretty skin.
9                                                                (Petrie 1904: 6–7)
30111
1       This whole chapter of his book reads very much like a colonial handbook
2       on how to deal with ‘natives’, and while Petrie’s work was mostly in Egypt,
3       Wheeler makes similar allusions to the masculinity of the discipline half a
4       century later in his main book on ﬁeldwork which he regarded as ‘an earthly
5       book, inapt to clerkly hands’ (Wheeler 1954: v).
6          In the USA, John Rowe in his paper on ‘Archaeology as a career’ simi-
7       larly notes that archaeology requires special characteristics, such as the
8       ability ‘to withstand a considerable amount of physical discomfort without
9       it interfering with his work or making him excessively irritable . . .’ (Rowe
40111   1954, reprinted in Heizer’s Guide to Archaeological Field Methods (1958: 154).
1       One also recalls the phrase attributed to Alfred Kidder distinguishing archae-
2       ologists with hairy chests from those with hairy chins, assuming, of course,
3       that all archaeologists were male anyway. Perhaps the most outspoken
44111   example, though, comes from Noel Hume’s advice on women on site:
        8   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111        Digging is, after all, a masculine occupation, and while more women
2           than men are likely to do well in the pot-washing shed or in the labo-
3           ratory, shovel-wielding females are not everyday sights in Western
4           society. If they are to be useful on site (and the right women can be
5           splendid excavators), they must be prepared to be accepted as men,
6           eschewing the traditional rights of their sex. It is vastly time-consuming
7           for men working in one area to be constantly hopping up and down
8           to push barrows for women working in another.
9                                                              (Noel Hume 1969: 60)
1011
1       This is undoubtedly an extreme view (though not perhaps for its time),
2       and few could point to such blatant sexism today. Nevertheless, there is
3       still a tendency for male archaeologists to work in the ﬁeld and for female
4       archaeologists to work in the laboratory or ﬁnds room (Gero 1985); and it
5       is not extreme in the slightest to argue that the ﬁeld remains heavily
6       inscribed as a masculine space – something which has been raised before
7       in other disciplines (for example, see Sparke 1996).
8          More broadly, the striking feature of many ﬁeldwork texts up to the
9       middle of the twentieth century was their focus on the properly disciplined
20111   individual. In Britain, after the 1960s, there was a shift from concern with
1       the ‘character’ of the individual archaeologist to concern with site ‘disci-
2       pline’. Of course the focus on site discipline has always been there –
3       throughout the earlier part of the twentieth century there was frequent use
4       of military analogies, with references to ‘campaigns’ of ﬁeldwork (Wheeler
5       1954: 1; Webster 1965: 63; Noel Hume 1969: 54). However, the issue
6       became much more signiﬁcant as the workforce changed from primarily
7       unskilled labourers to students and skilled professionals. Fieldwork manuals
8       are no longer written for the site director but the excavator. The devel-
9       opment of new recording methods and excavation practices in the 1960s,
30111   required ‘great site discipline and well-trained workers’(Biddle and Kjølbye-
1       Biddle 1969: 213). The same point is made in what is perhaps the most
2       popular book on ﬁeldwork in Britain today, Barker’s Techniques of Archaeo-
3       logical Excavation, where a section on site discipline argues for ‘a careful
4       balance of that which is self-imposed and that imposed from above; where
5       all the members of the excavation do what they are told or required because
6       they understand what they are doing’ (Barker 1982: 110).
7          Currently, each excavator (or ‘site assistant’ as they are often called) can
8       be responsible for the whole process from digging to recording a feature on
9       a site and the director/supervisor merely co-ordinates; this gives the impres-
40111   sion of democratic digging – yet the excavator’s focus is very narrow and
1       the level of interpretation minimal (for example, ‘this is a pit’). This is not
2       to denigrate the skill involved, but it is an illusion to think that the average
3       excavator of today has necessarily any more power on a site than had the
44111   labourer of a century ago. In the end, the supervisor/director, the one who
                                             Introduction: archaeology and the field   9

1111    writes the report, determines the interpretation of the site not only by being
2       in possession of the ‘wider picture’, but by selecting where and what the
3       site assistant excavates. In many ways, site assistants are completely inter-
4       changeable – he or she is not a person but a digging machine and although
5       some assistants may be more efﬁcient than others, their ‘local knowledge’
6       or personality is often ignored and certainly never mentioned in any contem-
7       porary manuals on ﬁeldwork.
8          Although Barker reiterates the importance of a hierarchy and this indeed
9       remain a key element of the organisation of ﬁeldworkers, since the 1960s
1011    the channels of discipline and power on site have been much more concealed
1       and dispersed. Instead of simply having an overall director who monitors
2       and controls the workforce – which still of course occurs to some extent,
3111    especially in non-Western contexts – control is articulated through pro
4       forma context sheets and other records which can proliferate excessively,
5       and which are used to constantly check the quality and quantity of work.
6       It is critical that all records are signed and dated, and many records have
7       boxes headed ‘checked by . . .’, to be ﬁlled in by the supervisor. There is
8       a clear inscription of surveillance in modern record-keeping which is directly
9       linked to these hierarchies. It is argued, of course, that such a system is to
20111   provide a standardised and ‘objective’ record of the excavation, but in fact
1       it controls not only the record, but also the bodies who produce it.
2          Thus, while there may have been a shift from the disciplined individual
3       to the disciplined workforce, there is clearly a continuous sense in which
4       the ‘Field’ is an area in which proper discipline is essential, as part of scien-
5       tiﬁc rigour. These disciplines and controls were all linked in with the issue
6       of trust, which was such a major part of nineteenth-century ﬁeldwork. It
7       is as if the credibility and reliability of any material generated through ﬁeld-
8       work takes its validity directly from the command of self/personnel on site.
9       And this returns us again to the issue of gender politics, where ‘male’ prac-
30111   tices frequently have a higher value than ‘female’ practices, because they
1       generally exhibit those attributes of certainty, authority and dichotomous
2       thinking which science values (Gero 1996: 276). Moreover, the very fact
3       of who is credited as an archaeologist largely depends on other archaeolo-
4       gists and, as a result, many women in the history of archaeology have not
5       merely been silenced, they have not even been recognised as archaeolo-
6       gists because of a masculine valuation of what constitutes an archaeologist
7       (Mason 1992: 93). As Joan Gero says in her critique of gender bias in ﬁeld-
8       work, much of this links back into the current model employed in
9       archaeology about how knowledge is produced, exempliﬁed by that (by
40111   now) old diagram showing the relation between theory and data as a cycle
1       or feedback loop (Gero 1996: 254).
2
3
44111
        10   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    The place of the field
2       This often cited relation between theory and data has come to be almost
3       the standard position on the relation between archaeologists and the
4       phenomena they study. It states that the archaeologist will always have an
5       effect on the phenomena under study but that the phenomena still exist
6       in a real sense and are not a ﬁgment of the archaeologist’s mind. Thus, in
7       so far as archaeologists will select what aspects to investigate, based on what
8       at the time is considered relevant, they construct the data. This of course
9       changes as new questions, new theoretical perspectives guide the inves-
1011    tigative process – examples might include anything from the development
1       of new techniques (e.g. soil micromorphology) to new analytical questions
2       (e.g. structural oppositions in artefact patterning). Conversely, there must
3
        be something there to select from, something which is beyond/independent
4
        of our conception of it and which can accommodate or conﬂict with our
5
        expectations of it. Practice is, in effect, this process or relationship between
6
        theory and data.
7
           This sounds very reasonable and it seems to have become almost a point
8
        of agreement or convergence among many of the different theoretical
9
        schools, in particular the processualists and post-processualists (Wylie 1992).
20111
1       But, as Gero says of this theory–data interdependence model, ‘it misrepre-
2       sents the complex historical and sociopolitical interactions that condition
3       every stage of archaeological research, from the naming and classifying of
4       archaeological data through the writing of interpretive explanations’ (Gero
5       1996: 254). These are issues of critical importance, issues which highlight
6       the importance of archaeology as a practice, of the process of archaeology
7       rather than just the ﬁnal product. How might we re-envisage practice? I
8       think it helps to look a little deeper at how practice is articulated, because
9       in many ways the old model of theory/data referred to above masks a much
30111   wider politics of ﬁeldwork and archaeology, one which in some respects
1       contradicts the initial impetus for ﬁeldwork. When, by the 1870s, ﬁeldwork
2       had become an established component of archaeology, it presented expe-
3       rience, presence in the ﬁeld, as a critical guarantor of scientiﬁc validity. As
4       Kuklick says of the wider scientiﬁc establishment of ﬁeldwork, ‘when natu-
5       ralists made experience in the ﬁeld a deﬁning property of membership in
6       their disciplinary communities, they were setting fundamental standards of
7       scientiﬁc craft’ (Kuklick 1997: 48–9). Now while this may still be believed
8       to be the case, it can also be argued that ﬁeldwork has become quite widely
9       separated from broader interpretive practices. This is exempliﬁed by a
40111   paradox which has developed over the past thirty years and which closely
1       relates to the development of a group of professional ﬁeldworkers, archae-
2       ologists whose specialism is ﬁeldwork itself: contract archaeologists.
3          Before I address the paradox, it is useful to elaborate a little further on
44111   how this split of ﬁeldwork from interpretation has been conceived. A
                                            Introduction: archaeology and the field    11

1111    commonly held view of archaeology, chieﬂy in the USA and especially
2       during the earlier half of the twentieth century, was that it was primarily
3       a method of data retrieval. Walter Taylor, for example, wrote that
4       ‘Archaeology per se is no more than a method and a set of specialised tech-
5       niques for the gathering of cultural information. The archaeologist, as
6       archaeologist, is really nothing but a technician’ (Taylor 1948: 43). More
7       recently, the most vocal exponent of this view is Irving Rouse, who has
8       written in numerous places on what he sees as a basic distinction between
9       archaeology and prehistory: archaeology is essentially a method, a tech-
1011    nique for data retrieval, while the interpretation of that data belongs to
1       the prehistorian or any other interested academic, such as the historian,
2       the sociologist, or the anthropologist (Rouse 1968; 1972). Rouse is not
3111    saying that an archaeologist cannot also be a prehistorian, but the two roles
4       must be made quite distinct. Both Taylor and Rouse produced similar system-
5       atic outlines for archaeology’s position within the wider study of culture
6       which they call anthropology. In this scheme archaeology is quite emphat-
7       ically at the base (Taylor 1948: ch. 6; Rouse 1972).
8          Many have of course disagreed with his approach, including Willey and
9       Phillips (1958), Chang (1968), Deetz (1971) and Clarke (1973), and since
20111   the 1960s the status of archaeology as more than mere ﬁeldwork, or data
1       retrieval, has been widely accepted. This difference, however, may have
2       been more virtual than real – it was after all the same Gordon Willey and
3       Phillip Phillips who proclaimed that ‘American archaeology is anthropology
4       or it is nothing’ (Willey and Phillips 1958: 2). By this they meant that
5       archaeology, as much as any other branch of anthropology, should concern
6       itself with explanation and not be merely descriptive – and yet in their
7       scheme, steps similar to those given by Taylor and later Rouse are presented,
8       with clear divisions between ﬁeldwork (observation), integration (descrip-
9       tion) and explanation (interpretation) (ibid.: 4). The difference really seems
30111   to be that Taylor and Rouse reserved the term ‘archaeology’ for the data
1       collection and analysis stage of any investigation or research.
2          Now I want to quote a portentous statement made by Rouse in 1968,
3       who foresaw
4
5           that the people interested in archaeology as technology will eventually
6           become sufﬁciently numerous and specialised to be recognised as a
7           separate profession, bearing the same relationship to interpretive archae-
8           ologists that engineers and applied scientists bear to pure scientists.
9                                                                   (Rouse 1968: 12)
40111
1       This returns us to contract archaeologists, and indeed others who effec-
2       tively work full time in the ‘ﬁeld’, whether this is on site or in the laboratory.
3       From one perspective, such archaeologists might be viewed as specialists,
44111   akin to zooarchaeologists or micromorphologists; from another, however,
        12   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    they are merely technicians, collecting and processing data for the ‘real’
2       scientists. Thus to a large extent Rouse’s prediction has come true, for while
3       we are all still called archaeologists, this may serve only to mask the different
4       modes of scientiﬁc production in operation in archaeology today (Shanks
5       and McGuire 1996: 82). Shanks and McGuire indeed talk about ‘archae-
6       ological engineers’ and how a hierarchy is in place with ‘those of us who
7       manage and sit on committees, synthesise, generalise and theorise and those
8       of us who sort, dig and identify’ (ibid.). Indeed, since the rise of contract
9       archaeology and the amassing of large quantities of data, there have been
1011    cries for this material to be incorporated and integrated much more into
1       research agendas. But the question is, who will do this?
2          I suspect that in many cases it will not be the people who dig it up, but
3       the academics. Is there a danger that we may return to the pre-1870s situation
4       (with often the same class implications too) where one group of people go
5       out and do ﬁeldwork while the ‘real’ archaeologists stay in their armchairs?
6       Probably not, but there can be no question that there is a very large group
7       of anonymous and silent archaeologists engaging in ﬁeldwork in Britain and
8       elsewhere today, who have no voice. This is the result not only of a lack of
9       a platform but of an ideology founded on the assumption that data collec-
20111   tion is independent of interpretation; that excavation, as Tilley says, is
1       merely an exercise in retrieval rather than in interpretation. This is not to
2       suggest that anyone who claims to be an archaeologist should conduct their
3       own ﬁeldwork and only draw on the product of their own labour – no one
4       can conﬁne themselves solely to writing about what they themselves have
5       excavated, and a value which privileges ﬁrst-hand experience should be con-
6       tested. But this is not really the issue here – rather it is about a politics of
7       archaeological practice which separates ﬁeldwork from interpretation, which
8       sees the production of archaeological knowledge as akin to factory produc-
9       tion rather than, as Shanks and McGuire (1996) suggest, as a craft.
30111      The paradox is, then, that ﬁeldwork became established as the basis of
1       the discipline in the nineteenth century, as the guarantee of its scientiﬁc
2       validity, because it was done by the same people who interpreted the material
3       gathered from that ﬁeldwork. It was this fact which deﬁned archaeology
4       and a host of other disciplines as scientiﬁc enterprises. And yet, over the
5       course of a century, it is as if the whole situation has been turned upside
6       down – archaeologists are now no longer required to go into the ﬁeld them-
7       selves; indeed, a whole group of specialist ﬁeldworkers has emerged, albeit
8       under development-led conditions, who can collect much of the data archae-
9       ologists need. And yet perhaps this paradox was always there, for the very
40111   construction of the ‘ﬁeld’ was established in opposition to ‘home’, to the
1       library, study or academy (Gupta and Ferguson 1997: 12–15). Fieldwork in
2       archaeology, as with anthropology and other disciplines, always has had its
3       counterpart in the ‘writing up’ – post-excavation, post-survey, etc. Each
44111   practice has its own texts, its own tropes and materials (ibid.; Table 1).
                                           Introduction: archaeology and the field   13

1111    Table 1 Discourse and the field
2       Fieldwork                          Post-ﬁeldwork
3
4       field                              library, laboratory, academy
5       fieldnotes                         monograph, paper
        fragmentary                        coherent
6       raw data, description              analysis, interpretation

8
9
1011       One does wonder, however, how widely this opposition is experienced.
1       For many contract excavators, there is no post-excavation work – the ﬁeld
2       is all they know, the ﬁeld is work. Indeed, the issue of the invisibility of
3111    the bulk of an excavation team in post-excavation work has been sharply
4       highlighted in a joint project at a Bronze Age ring-ditch in Cambridgeshire
5       (McFadyen 1997; McFadyen et al. 1997):
6
7            In terms of the excavation work that we carry out, we cannot ﬁnd the
8            words, and this is literally speaking, to describe to you how painful the
9            process of cutting ourselves out of an archaeological imagination is? Or
20111        describe what a dangerous shattering of subjectivity there is in drawing
1            and interpreting where you and others made something, but without
2            you? And yet everything else is sectioned, planned and given a context.
3            Do you know what it is like to always focus the camera in the shadows
4            that reside after you deliberately push a colleague out of the frame,
5            making them wipe out their footprints and pick up their work tools in
6            the process of leaving. What kind of archaeology are these forced expe-
7            riences for and who is it for? Why are we so professional about creating
8            an archaeology devoid of us?
9                                                              (McFadyen et al. 1997)
30111
1       Archaeology is a practice we do with others, perhaps in ﬁeldwork particu-
2       larly, and there is a violence which accompanies this when people are
3       silenced in the name of representation, the production of knowledge.
4          The over-riding conception of the relation between ﬁeldwork and its
5       sequel is one of transformation, of ﬁeldnotes into the monograph, fragments
6       into a whole, raw data into interpretations – and, by implication, the change
7       from engineer to thinker. Who and what gets lost in this process is usually
8       neglected, however; indeed this neglect is probably compounded by the fact
9       that, as Clifford says of anthropological ﬁeldwork, the ﬁeldnotes serve to reify
40111   and naturalise a place called ‘the ﬁeld’, kept separate from the operations of
1       theorising and writing up (Clifford 1990: 66). And yet, this model is more
2       than just the single movement from ﬁeld to study. It should recognise feed-
3       back between these two poles and consequently a certain movement, a
44111   hermeneutic. In the next section, I want to look in a little more detail at
        14   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    some recent attempts to explore this relationship, which try to break down
2       the distance between the ﬁeld and the academy.
3
4
        Alternative fieldworks
5
6       More reﬂexive approaches to archaeological ﬁeldwork have been presented
7       recently, in particular the work directed by Ian Hodder at Çatalhöyük
8       (Hodder 1997) and Barbara Bender, Sue Hamilton and Chris Tilley on
9       Bodmin Moor (Bender et al. 1997). Hodder’s argument is in many ways
1011    merely an inevitable and long overdue extension of post-processual ideas
1       into the realm of ﬁeldwork (Hodder 1989b, 1997). He identiﬁes what he
2       calls a contradiction within British ﬁeldwork in that it recognises there is
3       interpretation prior to, or embedded in, the process of excavation and yet
4       in practice it continues to pretend that this primary data collection was
5       prior to interpretation; that description can be separated from interpreta-
6       tion. Hodder is probably correct in this observation and he argues that we
7       should take a more reﬂexive approach to ﬁeldwork methodology. This
8       involves, perhaps most critically, enabling the relationship between ﬁnds
9       analyses (e.g. faunal remains, lithics, pottery, seeds) and the determination
20111   of stratigraphic events/contexts to be more interactive, facilitated through
1       increased personal interaction between excavators and specialists and a rela-
2       tional computer database. The traditional approach is that such analyses
3       are done after the excavation, not only temporally but conceptually. It is
4       as if data collection was factory production (see Shanks and McGuire above),
5       the process marked by various stages: excavation → ﬁnds processing →
6       analysis → synthesis. This indeed is the premise of English Heritage’s
7       Management of Archaeological Projects (MAP 2) which is the model for
8       most contract ﬁeldwork today in Britain.
9          The general point is that this process is almost seen as uni-directional,
30111   whereas in fact a reﬂexive methodology encourages constant feedback. Of
1       course, in practice, ﬁnds analysis often might change the in-ﬁeld interpre-
2       tation of a site, but this is an ad hoc process which is not explicitly
3       encouraged in the literature or in general attitudes. Frequently, for example,
4       pottery specialists are not given stratigraphic information in case it biases
5       their dating (or is it a test of their ability?), the point being, I suppose, to
6       have independent checks between stratigraphy and dating. But this seriously
7       misunderstands the nature of pottery as a dating tool; it is not like having
8       a stopped clock. The deposition of pottery and the types of pottery which
9       get deposited are not necessarily independent from the formation of that
40111   deposit, and it is often crucial to know something about the context in
1       understanding the pottery. Object and context are not independent and
2       should not be treated as if they were. It remains to be seen how widely
3       Hodder’s approach at Çatalhöyük could be adopted, but the issue certainly
44111   becomes sharply focused through the relationships between the excavators
                                          Introduction: archaeology and the field   15

1111    and various specialists (Hamilton 1996) and, particularly in contract archae-
2       ology, this is a major obstacle.
3          Tilley’s work in many ways expresses the same concerns as those of
4       Hodder, although his approach is somewhat different (Tilley 1989; Bender
5       et al. 1997). Tilley, and later Bender and Hamilton, are perhaps less
6       concerned with fostering new methodologies as with focusing simply on
7       the process of ﬁeldwork, the movement of interpretation which is usually
8       left behind in the presentation of a ﬁnal or ﬁnished product. They see the
9       whole ﬁeldwork enterprise as part of the process of interpretation, not simply
1011    as primary data collection, and thus its reportage should be as much a part
1       of the published site report. The same general issues which concern Hodder
2       concern them: excavation as interpretation, reﬂexivity, multivocality – the
3111    ﬂuidity of interpretation, but in a much more informal way. They achieved
4       this in their ﬁrst publication of ﬁeldwork through a juxtaposition of feature
5       descriptions, diary entries and narrative discussion. What they have achieved
6       is questionable, because the context descriptions are still there, and there
7       is a sense that we have not necessarily learned anything by having the
8       process of the excavation charted and narrated. However, in attempting
9       alternative approaches, there is no doubt that the project represents an
20111   important innovation.
1          What seems to be missing from both the above projects is any concern
2       for reﬂecting on the actual nature of the encounter as an experience –
3       rather the authors appear more concerned with the process and the struc-
4       tures established in moving from ﬁeldwork to text or how data are created.
5       Michael Shanks has discussed the experiental aspect of archaeology, but
6       most of his work focuses on objects and monuments rather than the act of
7       excavation (Shanks 1992a; e.g. see pp. 68–9). Another recent paper has
8       focused more on this particular aspect, with a call to ‘re-discover the act
9       of discovery’ (Edgeworth 1990). But this in a way takes the reﬂexive method-
30111   ology almost back to its point of origin, the discussion presented very much
1       revolving around the same issues; while Edgeworth writes about the embod-
2       iment of the act, his concern is very much with the practical knowledge
3       archaeologists bring to ﬁeldwork in identifying and interpreting what they
4       ﬁnd. This kind of implicit, embodied knowledge is really only the other
5       side of the formal explicit structures which Hodder is concerned with and
6       is ultimately a call for a kind of ethnomethodology or anthropology of ﬁeld-
7       work. Not that this is uninteresting, but all the time we are reminded that
8       the encounter is almost one-sided, that it is a subject encounter with an
9       object. Even though Edgeworth argues that the objects are the products of
40111   other humans, this is precisely where the problem lies, I feel; by viewing
1       what we excavate as products, our relationship is put at a distance.
2          In 1997 Victor Buchli and I ‘excavated’ an abandoned English state
3       housing property (Buchli and Lucas, forthcoming). The project arose
44111   through an interest in exploring the dual themes of the alienation of late
        16   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    twentieth-century individuals and the way we, as archaeologists alienate
2       our material, our data through the act of ﬁeldwork. We spent two intense
3       days recording the contents of a house recently abandoned by a single
4       mother with two children, the results of which can be found in our published
5       paper. A version of the paper was also presented at a Heritage seminar in
6       Cambridge in 1998, causing distress among some of the audience who
7       thought we had gone too far in invading someone’s privacy. Needless to
8       say, there was some debate about how far this is ordinarily done, both in
9       archaeology and anthropology. I want to quote from a diary entry I made
1011    at the time of the ﬁeldwork, because both it and the response which the
1       paper received at the seminar, illustrate, I think, something deeply central
2       to the act of the archaeological encounter.
3
4            Many broader issues come out of this, particularly issues of privacy and
5            invasion; there were occasions when it did feel like we were invading
6            H—’s privacy, particularly in those things which were closer to the
7            body. The erotic lingerie to some extent, but no more than some of
8            the other items. In particular the things in the bin seemed most ‘of
9            the body’ – tampons, K-Y jelly, used tissues, cotton buds. It’s the things
20111        which are intentionally thrown away, which seem to bear the marks of
1            privacy the most – those things which are not meant to be seen but
2            to be destroyed, put out of sight. Of course not all the rubbish can be
3            characterised like this but there is an element which can. More gener-
4            ally, why is it that we feel we are invading someone’s privacy in doing
5            this but not when doing ‘normal’ archaeology? I think familiarity obvi-
6            ously plays a strong part – the more familiar, the closer one can draw
7            the boundaries over privacy, whereas in an unfamiliar context – archae-
8            ology, but more generally anthropology, the limits seem to be set much
9            further back in the name of understanding – making a connection with
30111        the Other. One could imagine limits in anthropology still, however,
1            but in archaeology? This is very rare indeed and usually it is still
2            when the archaeology is still ‘wet’ – e.g. graves of our or another’s
3            recent dead.
4                                                       (GML, 22 July 1997; extract)
5
6       From the response at the seminar, we had clearly found other limits. I think
7       this question of limits is critical because it tells us so much about the nature
8       of the archaeological encounter, things which we can perhaps only discover
9       at the limits and not in the safety of, say, a prehistoric settlement.
40111      It also highlights an important point about the nature of much archae-
1       ology – that it is an encounter, not just observation, albeit active or
2       interpretive observation. We are not simply observers of the traces left
3       behind by people. These traces are not merely impassive objects separated
44111   from us as subjects which we must interpret to get to the other subject
                                          Introduction: archaeology and the field   17

1111    behind, invisible, absent, gone. We encounter these traces materially and
2       physically, they are alive, they are real. A group of people excavating a site
3       are immersed in this material culture as well as bringing things with them
4       of their own time (trowels, pens, texts, cameras); these intermesh with
5       things of the past (ditches, dirt, pot sherds, bones) and an encounter is
6       created which is unique. But it is an encounter and one which is always
7       lost in the aftermath of excavation in the name of representation. The
8       twentieth-century objects are erased or backgrounded as are the people and
9       the relationships between people – the politics of ﬁeldwork (see above).
1011    Indeed, this is perhaps the crucial meaning of the term ‘trace’ – rather than
1       being an object of interpretation, a foil for the subject, it rather mediates
2       the hermeneutic, and provides the possibility of archaeological discourse
3111    through its materiality, its presence (see Ricoeur 1988: 119–26; also Levinas
4       1996: 59–64).
5          I will return to the issue of materiality in archaeological practice at the
6       end of this book, and in particular to how it might help us re-think the
7       nature of the archaeological process; for now, however, I would like to
8       establish the main themes I will be discussing. First let me say that this
9       book will cover primarily the European (with a bias towards Britain) and
20111   North American scene. One of my regrets is that it could not be wider in
1       its coverage – especially, for example, to include the way European archae-
2       ology was transplanted through colonialism but subsequently developed
3       differently in other parts of the world. I can only say that the topic is so
4       large that, had I done so, this book might have lost out in coherence and
5       perhaps persuasiveness. Initially, I will explore the ﬁrst side of Clifford’s
6       equation – development of ﬁeldwork in archaeology – in order to pull out
7       major shifts in the way it has been practised and how this relates to broader
8       conceptual changes within archaeology. These shifts produce foci for discus-
9       sion in the subsequent chapters, all of which in one way or another also
30111   relate to major disciplinary divisions which fall on the other side of the
1       equation, the post-ﬁeldwork realm. These divisions include the emergence
2       of ﬁnds specialists, area/period specialists and, ultimately, archaeology as a
3       specialism. By focusing on the issue of specialisms and how these are artic-
4       ulated historically and theoretically within the discipline, I hope to reveal
5       some major faultlines in archaeology, but, at the same time, areas of great
6       potential.

        Chapter 2

2
        Finding the past
3
4
5
6
7
8
9
1011
1
2
3       On 17 June 1873, in the third year of his excavations at Hissarlik, Heinrich
4       Schliemann discovered what he regarded as the ultimate proof that the site
5       he was digging was the famous Troy of Homer’s Iliad:
6
7           In excavating this [city] wall further and directly by the side of the
8           Palace of King Priam, I came upon a large copper article of the most
9           remarkable form, which attracted my attention all the more as I thought
20111       I saw gold behind it. . . . In order to withdraw the treasure from the
1           greed of my workmen, and to save it for archaeology, I had to be most
2           expeditious, and although it was not yet time for breakfast, I immedi-
3           ately had ‘paidos’ called. . . .
4              While the men were eating and resting, I cut out the treasure with
5           a large knife, which it was impossible to do without the very greatest
6           exertion and the most fearful risk of my life, for the great fortiﬁcation-
7           wall, beneath which I had to dig, threatened every moment to fall
8           down upon me. But the sight of so many objects, every one of which
9           is of inestimable value to archaeology, made me foolhardy, and I never
30111       thought of any danger. It would however, have been impossible for me
1           to have removed the treasure without the help of my dear wife, who
2           stood by me ready to pack the things which I cut out in her shawl and
3           to carry them away.
4                                                     (Quoted in Deuel 1978: 205)
5
6       Much of the public perception of archaeology lies in the excitement of
7       discovery, of buried treasure, of the ﬁnd (Biddle 1994). We tend to down-
8       play this aspect in academic archaeology, almost as an emotional reaction
9       to what should be a dispassionate, scientiﬁc exercise. However, there is
40111   clearly a tension involved here, of the thrill of discovery, a hasty scrab-
1       bling in the earth, and at the same time a certain pride in meticulous,
2       restrained excavation. It is not that the two are contradictory – we can all
3       unearth wonderful ﬁnds in what might appear a greedy way and yet main-
44111   tain the standards of observation and recording required by contemporary
                                                                 Finding the past   19

1111    practice. Yet in any text you might pick up on ﬁeld archaeology today,
2       while the emotive thrill may be alluded to, it is the stress on a controlled
3       encounter with the archaeological record that is maintained.
4          And yet in the quote above from Schliemann, he clearly describes the
5       moment of discovery and links it into the archaeological value of the
6       objects; in his narrative, the two aspects seamlessly interweave. We may
7       deride Schliemann as a treasure hunter, even see some hypocrisy in his
8       allusion to the greed of the workmen and his own secretive removal of the
9       ﬁnds, but he was working in a very different intellectual climate and it is
1011    too easy to dismiss his archaeology as a primitive version of today’s progres-
1       sive techniques. What did it mean at the end of the nineteenth century
2       to excavate – and how different was that from today? This is the question
3111    I want to address in this chapter – it is not simply a history of ﬁeld tech-
4       niques, but of how what we today call the archaeological record has been
5       conceived in the past century or so since the discipline became properly
6       established.
7
8
        Cultural evolution and the object (1880–1920)
9
20111
        Pitt Rivers and the total archaeological record
1
2       A few years before Schliemann began his Trojan excavations, Lieutenant-
3       General Pitt Rivers had begun his excavation career in Ireland and then
4       later in England (Bowden 1991). When we think of these two ﬁgures of
5       archaeology, they stand at quite opposite poles – Schliemann the romantic,
6       the treasure-hunter, and Pitt Rivers the ﬁeld archaeologist, the scientist.
7       But how differently did they see the archaeology they – or rather their
8       workmen – unearthed? Pitt Rivers has gone down in all the history books
9       as the father of British ﬁeld archaeology in that he introduced detailed and
30111   systematic excavation and recording techniques, perfected over many
1       seasons of ﬁeldwork on his estates at Cranborne Chase. But what exactly
2       did this involve, and how different was it from the work of his forebears
3       and contemporaries? Pitt Rivers himself summed it up thus in his preface
4       to the ﬁrst volume of Excavations in Cranborne Chase:
5
6           It will, perhaps, be thought by some that I have recorded the excava-
7           tions of this village and the ﬁnds that have been made in it with
8           unnecessary fulness, and I am aware that I have done it in greater detail
9           than has been customary, but my experience as an excavator has led
40111       me to think that investigations of this nature are not generally sufﬁ-
1           ciently searching and that much valuable evidence is lost by omitting
2           to record them carefully. That this has been so in the present instance
3           is proved by the fact that this village had before been examined and
44111       reported upon in the XXIVth volume of the Journal of the Anthropological
        20   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111         Institute, and not a single pit or skeleton had been found; whilst I have
2            discovered 95 pits and 15 skeletons.
3                                                         (Pitt Rivers 1887: xvi–xvii)
4
5       These comments on a previous investigator, Austin, highlight two key points
6       which Pitt Rivers saw as crucial to ﬁeldwork: a ‘sufﬁcient search’ and ‘care-
7       ful recording’. These are undoubtedly the aspects of his work which make
8       him stand out. His accounts in the four volumes of his excavations make it
9       quite clear that his investigations were extensive, frequently included open-
1011    area trenching and were detailed in the recording. For example, regarding
1       the ‘sufﬁcient search’, during the excavation of the Romano-British village
2       at Rushmore Park (to which the above quote also alludes), he not only
3       cleared out major sections of ditch circuits, but also opened up inside the
4       enclosure by
5
6            placing the men in line at working distance apart, digging down every-
7            where through the surface mould until the chalk beneath was reached,
8            and noticing carefully any irregularity in the latter. Where the mould
9            went deeper it was dug out, and in this way pits, hearths, ditches, and
20111        irregularities of all kinds were cleared out and exposed.
1                                                              (Pitt Rivers 1887: 10)
2
3       It is unlikely, however, that a whole area was stripped in one pull but rather
4       that he used the technique known as ‘strip-digging’, by which the ground
5       was cleared in a series of successive parallel trenches, the spoil from one
6       being used to backﬁll the last (Barrett et al. 1983: 196). This was a common
7       nineteenth- and early twentieth-century method in shallow quarrying and
8       therefore probably one the workmen were familiar with. The consequences
9       of this for not identifying features or ﬁnds has been remarked upon (Barrett
30111   et al. 1991: 13; Bowden 1991: 156). When not excavating like this, he
1       tended to run single trenches across earthworks such as dykes, or into the
2       centre of barrows. For example, at Wor Barrow he began in a fairly conven-
3       tional way, running a trench north to south through the mound, even
4       leaving standing blocks at certain points as references for ﬁnd location
5       (Figure 1; Pitt Rivers 1898: 64, and plate 255). In many ways this is similar
6       to what other mound diggers had done, including Canon Greenwell, under
7       whom Pitt Rivers had excavated and whom he acknowledges as his ﬁrst
8       teacher (Pitt Rivers 1887: xix). However, unlike Greenwell, Pitt Rivers
9       then removed the rest of the mound, exposing the whole plan of the barrow
40111   including the mortuary enclosure. It is this aspect of trying to achieve total
1       excavation which really separated him from others, something which H.
2       St George Gray highlights in his memoir, illustrated by the excavation of
3       South Lodge Camp where total excavation was carried on, even after
44111   nothing was found in the ﬁrst three sections (Gray 1905: xvii).
        Finding the past                                                  21

                       Figure 1 Excavation of Wor Barrow by Pitt Rivers

1
