44111
        22   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111       The point Gray makes is that Pitt Rivers was not after treasure but after
2       history. Gray was one of Pitt Rivers’ supervisors in the later years of the
3       Cranborne Chase excavations, and was trained by Pitt Rivers to conduct
4       most of the actual ﬁeldwork and post-excavation analysis. This second inno-
5       vation, ‘careful recording’, is enshrined in the oft-quoted paragraph of the
6       preface to Volume I of Excavations:
7
8            Excavators, as a rule, record only those things which appear to them
9            important at the time, but fresh problems in Archaeology and
1011         Anthropology are constantly arising, and it can hardly fail to have
1            escaped the notice of anthropologists, especially those who, like myself,
2            have been concerned with the morphology of art, that, on turning back
3            to old accounts in search of evidence, the points which would have
4            been most valuable have been passed over from being thought unin-
5            teresting at the time. Every detail should, therefore, be recorded in the
6            manner most conducive to facility of reference, and it ought at all times
7            to be the chief object of an excavator to reduce his own personal
8            equation to a minimum.
9                                                             (Pitt Rivers 1887: xvii)
20111
1       Pitt Rivers’ military background probably strongly inﬂuenced his ability and
2       desire for systematic recording (Bradley 1983: 3); he trained up assistants
3       in surveying and draughting who carried out the actual ﬁeldwork and,
4       increasingly, the post-excavation analysis for the Cranborne Chase exca-
5       vations. Of course Pitt Rivers had supervised ﬁeldwork himself and one
6       cannot accuse him of being an armchair archaeologist, although by the
7       time he began work at Cranborne Chase, this is what in effect he was:
8
9            The work of superintending the digging – though I never allowed it
30111        be carried on in my absence, always visiting the excavations at least
1            three times a day, and arranging to be sent for whenever anything of
2            importance was found – was more than I could undertake single-handed,
3            with the management of the property and other social duties to attend
4            to, and I had by ample experience been taught that no excavation
5            ought ever to be permitted except under the immediate eye of a respon-
6            sible and trustworthy superintendant.
7                                                          (Pitt Rivers 1887: xvii)
8
9       To this end, a whole string of archaeologists learnt their ﬁeldwork through
40111   him, and it is probably this as much as anything which helped perpetuate his
1       image as the father of British ﬁeld archaeology. Pitt Rivers’ concern for ‘total
2       excavation’ comes out in his reports where equal emphasis is placed on plans
3       and sections. Comparing Excavations in Cranborne Chase with his teacher
44111   Greenwell’s major work, British Barrows (published only ten years before
                                                                   Finding the past   23

1111    Volume I of Excavations), the difference in the graphic record is startling.
2       Greenwell mostly showed artefacts, site ﬁgures rarely, and when the barrows
3       were illustrated, it was usually as a section (Greenwell 1877). In contrast,
4       Pitt-Rivers’ volumes are in effect read through the illustrations, each portion
5       of text entitled as a description of plate such-and-such. The plans are mea-
6       sured, showing contours of the surface and the location of all features after
7       excavation, while the sections are schematic, although with some represen-
8       tational elements, but again measured. In addition, tabulated summaries (his
9       ‘Relic Tables’ which were ﬁrst used at Mount Caburn and Caesar’s Camp in
1011    the late 1870s) appear at the end and give details of each feature, including
1       dimensions and quantities and types of ﬁnds. In many respects, this structure
2       is still used today, with topographic introduction, presentation of excavated
3111    features, followed by ﬁnds reports which include not just artefacts but faunal
4       and botanical remains in many cases too.
5          Pitt Rivers’ actual reports, then, very much live up to the standards he
6       preached in his preface. In everything I have said here it sounds as if he
7       was indeed the revolutionary ﬁgure he has been painted. But I think we
8       need to qualify this and try and place his innovations in some kind of
9       context. In the ﬁrst place, we noted in his excavation of Wor Barrow how
20111   he started in an almost conventional manner with a linear trench and only
1       then opened up the rest. This is quite revealing, because his actual methods
2       of excavation were in many ways no different from those of Greenwell or
3       other contemporaries – he merely applied them on a more extensive scale
4       (Bowden 1991: 154–5). While he was not working on very deeply strati-
5       ﬁed sites – although in many cases they were certainly multi-phase – he
6       rarely mentions stratigraphy or raises issues of stratigraphically phasing a
7       site (e.g. looking at ditch junctions/cuts, etc.). Bowden, in his detailed study
8       of Pitt Rivers, sums this up very well:
9
30111       His recording was meticulous but his physical digging methods let him
1           down. Though recognising the importance of stratigraphy and the inter-
2           relationship of features he failed to excavate in such a way as to be
3           able to see them clearly. He dug in spits and not by layers, a tech-
4           nique which he was to retain until the end of his life.
5                                           (Bowden 1991: 94; also see pp. 155–6)
6
7       The same lack of originality could be said of his recording; the sections
8       look little different from those of the better excavators of the time, and
9       locating all ﬁnds by vertical position as well as contextual location was
40111   nothing new – he merely applied the same technique more exhaustively
1       than did others (see Bowden 1991: 154–5). More signiﬁcant, however,
2       is that towards the end of his Cranborne Chase excavations he gave up
3       even these sections in favour of ‘average sections’ which he continued
44111   to use thereafter (Bowden 1991: 128); these were simply generalised proﬁles
        24   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    with the ﬁnds location plotted on (e.g. see Pitt Rivers 1898). This suggests
2       very strongly that his main interest was not in stratigraphy but in sequencing
3       ﬁnds, and he clearly felt that a simple vertical position was sufﬁcient to do
4       this. This comes out very clearly in a paragraph on how to excavate a ditch
5       in Volume IV of Cranborne Chase (1898), where he argues for horizontal
6       excavation in spits, against the usual method of digging down to the bottom
7       in one spot and then working the face vertically along the length of the
8       ditch (Pitt Rivers 1898: 26). His reasoning for this is simply in terms of
9       ﬁnds control. In summary, to quote Bradley:
1011
1            Recent work in Cranborne Chase suggests that it is an over-simpliﬁ-
2            cation to see Pitt Rivers as the advocate of a purely empirical school
3            of archaeology. His reluctance to interpret the structural sequence on
4            his sites did not result entirely from scholarly restraint; the General
5            had quite different objectives.
6                                                                  (Bradley 1983: 8)
7
8       The key point, I believe, to Pitt Rivers’ innovation was not that he invented
9       ﬁeld archaeology or new methods of excavation and recording, but that he
20111   applied the standard techniques of the day in a much more totalising and
1       exhaustive manner – he wanted a total record of what was in the ground.
2       While he may have been among the ﬁrst to excavate non-barrow sites
3       (most of his contemporaries, such as Greenwell, were almost all barrow-
4       diggers – see Marsden 1974), his interest was in the recovery of a wider
5       range of everyday ﬁnds rather than in the site itself. I think this comes out
6       very clearly in the ﬁrst quote we gave above, where he opens with an
7       apology for the ‘unnecessary fulness’ of his account. We may still applaud
8       this as no mean achievement, but it is to be questioned whether such a
9       thing as a total record is possible. Carver discusses this aspect in relation
30111   to two schools of ﬁeld archaeology, one stemming from Pitt Rivers and the
1       idea of the total record, and the other from Petrie and Wheeler who argued
2       that one always selects what to record (Carver 1990: 45–7). Petrie, writing
3       four years after the death of Pitt Rivers, said:
4
5            In recording, the ﬁrst difﬁculty is to know what to record. To state
6            every fact about everything found would be useless, as no one could
7            wade through the mass of statements. . . . The old saying that a man
8            ﬁnds what he looks for in a subject is too true; or, if he has not enough
9            insight to ensure ﬁnding what he looks for, it is at least sadly true that
40111        he does not ﬁnd anything he does not look for.
1                                                                     (Petrie 1904: 49)
2
3       If we accept that the real force behind Pitt Rivers’ approach was the idea
44111   of a ‘total record’, it means that this is what we should focus on rather
                                                                Finding the past   25

1111    than any mythical revolution in ﬁeld methods or techniques. I think in
2       this respect it is extremely productive to mention Pitt Rivers’ other major
3       contribution to archaeology, evolutionary typology. It is possible to believe
4       that Pitt Rivers’ intellectual faculties were applied with much greater force
5       to classiﬁcation and an evolutionary conception of material culture than
6       they were to excavation (for example, see Pitt Rivers 1906; also Bradley
7       1983; Bowden 1991: 161). Moreover, one could argue that typology is in
8       its very nature, an exercise in totalising objects; it brings them under a
9       single system by placing them in relation to other objects and in this way,
1011    every object is connected in a structured chain – which in the case of
1       evolutionary typology, is ordered by the criterion of complexity. I will not
2       go into Pitt Rivers’ concept of evolutionary typology here as I touch on it
3111    in more detail later, but for the moment I think it is important to recog-
4       nise the link between a totalising classiﬁcation and a totalising excavation.
5       The reason Pitt Rivers pushed the conventional ﬁeld methodologies to a
6       much greater extent than anyone had done previously is because of his
7       desire for totalisation; it was his intellectual background in classiﬁcation
8       and desire for a total record which drove his innovations in the ﬁeld.
9          If there is a link at the conceptual level between Pitt Rivers’ excavation
20111   methods and evolutionary typology, is there a more practical link? This can
1       be answered by posing a further question: why, despite all his detailed
2       recording and the presentation of the site through plans, sections,
3       photographs, text and even model reconstructions, does Pitt Rivers never
4       really discuss the way a site ‘works’ or functions? What is really glaringly
5       marked by its absence in his accounts of Cranborne Chase, is any sense of
6       phasing (Barrett et al. 1991: 13). Why this lack? He clearly had a sense
7       of the importance of the site as opposed to just artefacts or objects – he
8       was after all the ﬁrst Inspector of Ancient Monuments. But what value did
9       he put on these sites? That is the question that surely needs answering. I
30111   think the answer relates to the observation we made earlier about his inter-
1       ests in evolutionary typology. What really matters to him in the
2       archaeological record are objects and how they reﬂect upon human history.
3       While his concern for detailed recording may have been motivated in
4       general by a desire for totalisation, his particular aims were dominated by
5       the need to provide a secure context for objects, to establish their proper
6       place in the evolutionary scheme of culture. Bradley has even suggested
7       that his main motivation in early excavations was to recover material purely
8       to illustrate his typological sequences – not in the sense of testing but
9       merely as a physical addition to a collection (Bradley 1983: 4; also Barrett
40111   et al. 1991: 13).
1          I think that this motivation perhaps never left him. Indeed, as Bradley
2       points out, at the end of Volume IV of Excavations there is an essay on
3       the evolution of chevron-decorated pottery. In concluding this section, Pitt
44111   Rivers re-asserts the importance of recording the proper levels of ﬁnds in
        26   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    order to help answer these questions (Pitt Rivers 1898: 238–9). Moreover,
2       in commenting elsewhere on the need to recover all artefacts, not just the
3       ‘nice’ pieces, his argument is based on the signiﬁcance that common (i.e.
4       frequently occurring) objects have for classiﬁcation (ibid.: 27). I think this
5       is crucial to appreciating how the archaeological record was conceived in
6       the late nineteenth century and to contextualising Pitt Rivers’ contribu-
7       tions to ﬁeld archaeology. While he gives every appearance of concern for
8       all those issues which are important today, such as the site plan and context,
9       in actuality it is very hard to ﬁnd in any of his writings, any real interest
1011    in interpreting the site, the meaning of features and so forth. This may
1       seem something of a contradiction, but this relation does not really surface
2       in Pitt Rivers’ work, partly because his texts on archaeological excavations
3       and those on anthropological issues of evolutionary typology, are quite sepa-
4       rate. The same cannot be said of another major British ﬁgure in ﬁeld
5       archaeology, Flinders Petrie. Petrie provides an interesting study of the
6       relation between the way in which the archaeological record was conceived
7       and the prevailing concern for the evolution of material culture.
8
9
        Flinders Petrie: the site and its finds
20111
1       Flinders Petrie’s Methods and Aims in Archaeology (1904) was perhaps the
2       ﬁrst archaeological ﬁeld manual in English; although largely based upon
3       and applied to work in Egypt, it can be read as a more general statement
4       on a ‘scientiﬁc archaeology’. In it he discusses a whole series of issues
5       surrounding excavation, from the personality of the archaeologist to methods
6       of recording and the ethics of ﬁeldwork. I shall return to this text on later
7       occasions but here I will focus on certain points only, chieﬂy the methods
8       of excavating and recording sites. In terms of excavation, Petrie recom-
9       mended two main types of trenching – open area and linear, depending on
30111   the nature of the site. The former was better for complex sites with deep
1       stratigraphy, while simpler sites were best tested by a series of parallel
2       trenches which could be extended according to the layout of structures
3       (Petrie 1904: 41–2).
4          The concern for the plan of a site is clearly paramount in Petrie’s discus-
5       sion and indeed in his recording he focuses on how to plan large areas by
6       means of a grid of squares identiﬁed by a letter (for the row) and number
7       (for the column) (Petrie 1904: 48–54). In this he is clearly following on
8       the innovation of Pitt Rivers who undoubtedly changed the graphic repre-
9       sentation of archaeology through the use of plans – a point acknowledged
40111   by O. G. S. Crawford as being the ‘one essential feature in every excavation’
1       (Crawford 1921: 208). Petrie’s method was still in use on some post-war
2       sites in this country (e.g. Clark on Starr Carr). Moreover, Petrie’s great
3       criticism of earlier excavation reports was their lack of plans – he singles
44111
                                                                   Finding the past   27

1111    out as an example Greenwell’s British Barrows (1877) which contained very
2       few illustrations, yet most of the text of which could have easily been
3       reduced to a series of plans (Petrie 1904: 114). Petrie’s vision of a good
4       archaeological report was clearly one dominated by its ﬁgures rather than
5       its words – ‘the text is to show the meaning and relation of the facts already
6       expressed by form’ (ibid.: 115). This visual sense of archaeological data was
7       increased by the importance he attached to photographs, and more gener-
8       ally his whole attitude can again be seen as partly derived from Pitt Rivers.
9       Indeed, Petrie had met Pitt Rivers in 1881 in rather comical circumstances
1011    when the latter was on a tour in Egypt (Burleigh and Clutton-Brock 1982).
1          The element of visual representation is one to which I shall return in
2       the ﬁnal chapter, but it is worth qualifying Petrie’s focus on plans by his
3111    almost total disregard of sections which gets, by comparison, very little
4       mention in the book. Why this should be so is curious, for Petrie was not
5       unconcerned with the chronological development of a site. Mortimer
6       Wheeler, who was a strong critic of Petrie, remarks on this: ‘The almost
7       complete absence of measured sections from Petrie’s reports is the inevitable
8       corollary of his “method”’ (Wheeler 1954: 15). What Wheeler is referring
9       to here is Petrie’s lack of concern for stratigraphic relationships; a closer
20111   look shows that it was chieﬂy the ﬁnds which provided the site’s chronology
1       rather than stratigraphic analysis; in the chapter entitled ‘Systematic archae-
2       ology’ (ch. XI), he identiﬁes two modes of work necessary for archaeology
3       to become properly scientiﬁc: the ‘complete deﬁnition of facts by means of
4       a corpus of all known variety of objects’, and the ‘arrangement of material
5       in its order of development by statistical methods and comparison, which
6       bring out the original sequence of construction’ (ibid.: 123).
7          Petrie’s interest in typology is well known, especially his work on seriation,
8       but we need to ask why typological sequences should be seen as deﬁning
9       the development of a site rather than stratigraphy. A few years later, his
30111   approach was to be criticised as relying on ‘evidence from style rather than
1       stratiﬁcation’, as being subjective rather than objective archaeology (Droop
2       1915: 8–10). One could put it down to ‘bad archaeology’ (as Droop and
3       later Wheeler did), but given the evolutionary conception of culture which
4       was dominant at the time, is it not surprising that sections at least should
5       not have been seen as a greater resource? Petrie makes an interesting
6       comment towards the end of the aforementioned chapter with regard to
7       displaying objects in museums; he states that they should be displayed so
8       as to demonstrate both their development and context (Petrie 1904: 132).
9       This idea of the twofold character of an object is derived from the
40111   Scandinavian innovations in museum display by Thomsen and later
1       Hildebrand, and it is worth exploring a little further as it helps to under-
2       stand Petrie’s focus on seriation and typology in providing the ‘original
3       sequence of construction’ of a site.
44111
        28   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    Antiquarianism and archaeology in Northern Europe:
2       redefining the object
3       It is all very well having stratigraphic sequences but the question arises
4       whether there is any archaeological site in the world which has a ‘full
5       sequence’ of deposits representing all prehistoric periods (forgetting for
6       a moment the absurdity of this very conception). More commonly one
7       has to link stratigraphically unrelated deposits or groups of stratiﬁed
8       deposits across unconnected sites. The greater the lack of stratigraphic in-
9       formation, the more reliant one is upon typological methods – indeed
1011    in Sweden where typological studies were effectively developed, prop-
1       erly contexted ﬁnds were rare compared to Denmark. Where in Sweden
2       most ﬁnds came as ‘strays’, in Denmark graves and hoards were far more
3       common, offering a better basis for using ﬁnd combination to construct
4       chronologies (Almgren 1995: 27). This is well illustrated by the exhibition
5       in the Museum of National Antiquities at Stockholm in 1866 which
6       was modelled upon that of Thomsen at Copenhagen in 1819, for ‘the
7       exhibitions were arranged in two parallel running series, one arranged
8       according to types, and the other according to ﬁnd location’ (ibid.: 27). The
9       lack of closed ﬁnds, (e.g. graves, hoards) meant that type series came to
20111   have greater importance. B. E. Hildebrand, who directed the exhibition,
1       employed an aesthetics of similarity, imitating an exhibition of modern
2       mass-produced items of the same year (ibid.). Helping out at the exhi-
3       bition was his son, Hans Hildebrand (who later published the ﬁrst evolu-
4       tionary typology, on Iron Age brooches), and the young Oscar Montelius
5       (ibid.: 28–31).
6          The development of typology might then be said to have arisen out of
7       a need to provide chronology in the face of poor stratigraphic information.
8       A debate later emerged in the 1880s between Montelius and another archae-
9       ologist Sophus Müller about the relative priority of ﬁnd combination
30111   (fundsammenængen) and typology in constructing prehistoric chronologies
1       (Klindt-Jensen 1975: 84–96; Gräslund 1987). The basis of the ﬁnd combi-
2       nation is that:
3
4            A ﬁnd consists of a number of artefacts within a common spatial frame-
5            work, which indicates that they got into the soil on one and the same
6            occasion or that they were accumulated during a deﬁnite and contin-
7            uous period of time. Thus, a ﬁnd indicates an absolutely simultaneous
8            deposit or a relatively simultaneous accumulation in the soil.
9                                                               (Gräslund 1976: 70)
40111
1       From this deﬁnition, it seems clear that the ‘ﬁnd’ is conceived much as a
2       ‘context’ or stratigraphic unit is, although in the same statement it is
3       described both as the deposit and the ﬁnds deﬁned by the deposit. Gräslund
44111
                                                                  Finding the past   29

1111    goes on to say that, in themselves, the deposit or associated ﬁnds offer no
2       means of relative dating – it is only when the deposits in a group are
3       compared in terms of the composition of their ﬁnds, e.g. through seriation,
4       that a chronology can be constructed. Fundamental to this process is clas-
5       siﬁcation, in so far as one analyses what type of ﬁnd one deposit has in
6       common with another. Of course Gräslund is not saying that stratigraphy
7       is irrelevant to the relative date of the deposits in a group but the chrono-
8       logical value of stratigraphy is conditioned by the type deﬁnability of the
9       artefacts (Gräslund 1976: 74). The crucial point in all this, however, is
1011    that the ﬁnd combination, whether it has any stratigraphic content or not
1       (i.e. is open or closed), is primarily seen in terms of its relative date, its
2       ‘epochal’ attribution (e.g. Iron Age). Stratigraphy as a concept is of little
3111    importance except in so far as it aids chronology via closed ﬁnd-combination
4       analogies. This is best demonstrated by the work of Worsaae, who succeeded
5       Thomsen at the National Museum and who was the principal ﬁgure in
6       Danish ﬁeld archaeology in the late nineteenth century.
7          Worsaae is often cited as another pioneer of scientiﬁc ﬁeld techniques,
8       but his methods leave a lot to be desired. In the appendix to Primeval
9       Antiquities of Denmark (1849), he discusses the methods used in excavating
20111   barrows: ﬁrst one should make a proper, measured description, preferably
1       with a drawing, and only then excavate (Worsaae 1849: 152–3). He recom-
2       mends two techniques – ﬁrst, trenching across the barrow, sometimes
3       supplemented by another trench at right angles, and the second technique,
4       digging a pit through the centre from above, again preferably supplemented
5       by a trench leading to the barrow edge. What is interesting about the
6       trenches is that he gives orientations – these are not arbitrary but run along
7       speciﬁc alignments, which correspond to where satellite burials are predom-
8       inantly located (ibid.). These trenches are not dug in order to understand
9       barrow construction, but are geared to the location of burials and the
30111   recovery of artefacts. Indeed for the same reasons as Pitt Rivers, he argues
1       for total recovery of all ﬁnds no matter how trivial, as it is usually the
2       common objects which provide the means for comparison (Worsaae 1849:
3       156). Sites, like barrows, are treated as ‘mines’ for artefacts, albeit in a
4       controlled manner, and he cites other good places where antiquities may
5       be found, including peat bogs and quarries (ibid.: 155). No mention is made
6       of stratigraphy beyond a passing mention of distinguishing interments at
7       the top and base of barrows and nowhere in the book is stratigraphy used
8       to argue for the dates of antiquities – he draws instead on general evolu-
9       tionary assumptions. Even his later work on shell middens, which exhibited
40111   greater concern for stratigraphy, does not alter the fact that his main concern
1       was the retrieval of artefacts.
2          Now perhaps we can appreciate why Petrie showed little concern for
3       sections and the stratigraphy of the site; in itself it was deemed of little
44111
        30   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    relevance to chronology except through seriation of closed ﬁnd groups, and
2       even then it was not strictly necessary as the same effect could be achieved
3       though seriation of purely typological elements, that is, evolutionary
4       typology. This attitude is strongly expressed in his paper ‘Sequences in
5       prehistoric remains’ (1899); Petrie complained that although broad period
6       divisions had been achieved, there was still a lack of ﬁner resolution. To
7       achieve this, he outlined ﬁve methods which he employed to resolve issues
8       of chronology:
9
1011         1st Actual superposition of graves and burials; but rarely found.
1            2nd Series of development or degradation of form; very valuable if
2            unimpeachable.
3            3rd Statistical grouping by proportionate resemblance; the basis for clas-
4            sifying large groups.
5            4th Grouping of similar types, and judgement by style; giving a more
6            detailed arrangement of the result of the 3rd method.
7            5th Minimum dispersion of each type, concentrating the extreme
8            examples.
9                                                                (Petrie 1899: 297)
20111
1       The ﬁrst, stratigraphic relationship, was for Petrie rarely of practical use
2       but he does afﬁrm its importance in the broadest periodisation and as a
3       framework for the more reﬁned sequences; moreover, it appears to be of
4       quite a different order from the other four in that it uses direct character-
5       istics of the contexts to sequence themselves rather than relying on
6       comparisons of their objects. Of the other four, each has different aspects
7       worthy of remark. The second method, essentially an evolutionary typology,
8       is comparable to Pitt Rivers’ method and, under one guise, that of Montelius.
9       The third method is closest to what we call seriation, though it seriates
30111   the presence/absence or frequency of different types of objects rather than
1       attributes of objects; this latter is closer to what Petrie describes as the
2       fourth method which looks at the similarity/dissimilarity between objects,
3       such as brooches, of a single type. The ﬁfth method is more of a supple-
4       ment to the others and is merely a kind of Occam’s razor or law of parsimony
5       which would see each different type in the sample as occupying the shortest
6       possible duration.
7          From this, it is fairly clear what Petrie’s priorities are; but more than
8       that, they reveal that while stratigraphy may be relevant in understanding
9       the constructional sequence of a site, neither it nor the sequence itself
40111   was the aim of ﬁeld archaeology; rather these were simply means to an end,
1       the end being understanding the evolution of material culture. A demon-
2       stration of this is given quite clearly in the opening paragraphs of the
3       same paper:
44111
                                                                 Finding the past   31

1111        But it may be said that in dealing with ages before any written record
2           of years no reference to time or dates is possible. In the narrowest sense
3           this may be true. Yet the main value of dates is to show the sequence
4           of events; and it would matter very little if the time from Augustus to
5           Constantine had occupied six centuries instead of three, or if Alexander
6           had lived only two centuries before Augustus. The order of events and
7           the relation of one country to another is the main essential in history.
8           Indeed, the tacit common-sense of historians agrees in treating the
9           periods of great activity and production more fully than the arid ages
1011        of barbarism, and so substituting practically a scale of activity as the
1           standard rather than a scale of years.
2              It would be, therefore, no fallacy to portion out the past by the ratio
3111        of events rather than by the seasons; and to measure history by the
4           stages of thought and action of man rather than by inanimate celestial
5           motions. In this truest sense, then, we may have a possibility of reducing
6           the prehistoric ages to a historical sequence, and deﬁning them as
7           readily as historic times. If some scale and ratio of human activities
8           can be adopted, we may measure the past by means of it deﬁnitely as
9           we do by years B.C.
20111                                                               (Petrie 1899: 295)
1
2       One could argue that Petrie, as much as Pitt Rivers or Worsaae, was not
3       really concerned to understand how a site worked or developed in itself –
4       the site was merely the context for the ﬁnds. The epochal concept of culture
5       provided the framework within which a context or stratigraphic event was
6       primarily understood – as denoting a particular phase or period (epoch)
7       within a relative chronology whose ultimate purpose was to place an object
8       in the correct point of a sequence. This was its primary cultural relevance.
9          Despite the so-called innovations of Pitt Rivers, the differences between
30111   him and Greenwell are not differences in kind, but only of degree. Indeed,
1       one might even suggest that Schliemann was as good as Pitt Rivers, espe-
2       cially when one considers the complexity of the sites he excavated,
3       identifying building levels/layers in a Tell. In 1881 he also brought in an
4       architect, Dörpfeld, to produce accurate plans (Cottrell 1953: 79, 85; Trigger
5       1989: 197). Similarly, Giuseppi Fiorelli at Pompeii in the later nineteenth
6       century excavated in wide horizontal layers rather than the usual ‘mine
7       shafts’, expressing a concern for controlled excavation (Malina and Va šíček
8       1990: 49). Petrie, who started excavating in Egypt around the same time,
9       also produced work of an equal standard and was followed by George Reisner
40111   at the turn of the century who improved techniques even more (Trigger
1       1989: 197). We have not discussed Reisner’s work here but it is signiﬁcant
2       to note that the American archaeologist Alfred Kidder, who is discussed
3       below, had studied under him at Harvard (ibid.: 188).
44111
        32   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111       What brings most of these archaeologists together is a similar conception
2       of archaeological ﬁeldwork as primarily one concerning the retrieval of arte-
3       facts. The actual precision used in such retrieval is in many ways irrelevant
4       since the signiﬁcance lies not in any stratigraphic context but in a ﬁnd’s
5       relationship to other ﬁnds. This is clear for example in Pitt Rivers’ adoption
6       of average sections and the concern for three-dimensional recording rather
7       than stratigraphic association. This is not to say that they were not inter-
8       ested in the features from which ﬁnds came, such as graves, buildings,
9       ditches – but rather that these took second place to the ﬁnds. Schliemann’s
1011    conception of the recovery of Priam’s treasure shares the very same back-
1       ground as Pitt Rivers’ recovery of Romano-British pottery from Rushmore
2       Park; both archaeologists wanted to be sure of the context from which the
3       ﬁnds came, but were not concerned for the context in itself, so much as
4       for the ﬁnd.
5          From this perspective, the proximity of late nineteenth-century archae-
6       ology to antiquarianism is much closer; what unites them is a focus on the
7       object rather than the site. What separates them is a concern to place
8       the ﬁnds in an evolutionary/historical rather than an aesthetic/speculative
9       context. Schliemann’s major intellectual concern was with the literal truth
20111   of Homer; Pitt Rivers’ preoccupation was with the evolution of material
1       culture. Both focused on the object to achieve this, and the methods they
2       actually used to retrieve objects were not that dissimilar. It is interesting
3       to turn and see how the same issue was dealt with in North America,
4       because, despite differences, the same conceptions lie at the root of ﬁeld-
5       work conducted there at the end of the nineteenth century.
6
7
        The development of field archaeology in North America
8
9       As in Europe, late nineteenth-century archaeological ﬁeldwork in North
30111   America was highly variable in quality, being conducted by a mixture of
1       peripatetic collectors such as Edward Palmer or people trained in other
2       disciplines such as William Henry Holmes (art), Cyrus Thomas (ento-
3       mology) and Frederic Ward Putnam (zoology) (Meltzer 1985: 251).
4       Archaeology in North America was very much dominated and forged at
5       this time by two institutions, the Bureau of American Ethnology (BAE) in
6       Washington and the Peabody Museum, Harvard, both of which were
7       concerned to develop a profession of ‘real archaeologists’ although with
8       some differences of opinion (Hinsley 1974, 1985). The BAE certainly
9       regarded itself as the leader of a new science, claiming it represented the
40111   ‘New Archaeology’ (Meltzer 1985: 252); on the other hand, it was perhaps
1       Putnam who did more than anyone to train groups of students in careful
2       excavation and recording and he was one of the few to talk publicly about
3       his techniques (Putnam 1973 [1886]).
44111
                                                                 Finding the past   33

1111      In an address to the Harvard University Archaeological Society in 1885,
2       Putnam outlined the methods of investigation for burial mounds, settle-
3       ments and cemeteries, all of which followed a similar procedure of trenching
4       or slicing:
5
6           For instance, in exploring a mound a trench is ﬁrst dug at the base of
7           the mound. A slight vertical wall is made thereby showing the contact
8           of the edge of the mound with the earth upon which it rests. . . . This
9           wall is the ﬁrst section of the exploration, and its outline should be
1011        drawn or photographed and its measurements noted. For the latter
1           purpose it is best to stretch two strings over the mound, one north and
2           south and the other east and west, and to take all measurements from
3111        those. After this ﬁrst section is made, the work is carried on by slicing;
4           or cutting down a foot at a time, always keeping a vertical wall in
5           front, the whole width of the mound. Each slice thus made is a section.
6           . . . This method is continued until the whole mound has been dug
7           away, and a thorough knowledge of its structure and contents obtained.
8                                                           (Putnam 1973 [1886]: 3)
9
20111   This slice method was basically a way of progressing by means of a contin-
1       uous vertical face, allowing constant check on the stratigraphy. It is
2       important to recognise that while it is not stratigraphic excavation, it is
3       still excavation which is aware of stratigraphy. This is an important point,
4       because there is a great deal of disagreement over who conducted the ﬁrst
5       stratigraphic excavations in North America, and misunderstanding of this
6       difference may lie at the base of many of these disputes (Browman and
7       Givens 1996: 81). The ‘stratigraphic revolution’ is discussed later in this
8       chapter, but it is worth looking at other ﬁeldworkers in the late nineteenth
9       century who used stratigraphy to interpret their sites.
30111      Browman and Givens analyse several early claimants for stratigraphic
1       excavation, including William Dall and Henry Holmes, both of whom, like
2       Putnam, clearly observed stratigraphy in cross-sections, but this is not the
3       same as excavating stratigraphically (ibid.: 82). However, others such as
4       G. Nordenskiöld, Richard Wetherill and George Pepper have also been
5       accredited with early stratigraphic excavation, and at least in the case of
6       Pepper this seems incontrovertible, thanks to Reyman’s study of his unpub-
7       lished ﬁeld notes (Reyman 1989). Pepper’s work in the 1890s was certainly
8       ahead of its time (if only by a decade or so) and not only because of his
9       ﬁeld methodology, but also because of his concern with chronological
40111   change – and the two are linked. His methods remained unpublished and
1       were not taken up, perhaps because the discipline was still not quite ready
2       for an interest in chronology.
3          Much the same point can be made of early stratigraphic excavations in
44111
        34   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    South America by Max Uhle which were more or less ignored in their own
2       day. Uhle was born and trained in Germany, and in particular his early
3       museum work in Europe would have made him attentive to issues of typo-
4       logical development. Moreover, he was familiar with Petrie’s studies. His
5       most inﬂuential work was conducted in Peru and Bolivia in the 1890s, and,
6       after Petrie, he used seriation techniques on grave assemblages to create a
7       highly successful chronology (Uhle 1902). Only rarely did he employ strati-
8       graphic means to verify these, but during later excavations on a shell-mound
9       in California he used the stratigraphy to suggest a sequence of cultural
1011    change. The reaction to this work is very interesting, because essentially
1       the work was disputed and ultimately ignored; and yet the man responsible
2       for this, Alfred Kroeber, was also responsible for later resurrecting Uhle’s
3       work and bringing the importance of seriation into archaeology (Willey
4       and Sabloff 1993: 63–4; Browman and Givens 1996: 82).
5          While it is important to recognise Uhle’s contribution to archaeological
6       ﬁeldwork, I think one must not lose sight of the fact that his use of stratig-
7       raphy was not common and, like Petrie, he preferred to use discrete graves
8       to work on rather than stratiﬁed deposits such as middens. Where he perhaps
9       differed from Petrie was that he was more concerned with speciﬁc
20111   regional/historical culture change than general evolutionary trends – a fact
1       which also caused him to clash with prevailing views at the turn of the
2       century in North America. The latter part of the nineteenth century in
3       North America has been characterised as a period of formalisation for
4       American archaeology, much as it was in Europe (Willey and Sabloff 1993).
5       It was a time when a great deal of ﬁeldwork was conducted, and sites
6       described and classiﬁed; however, unlike in Europe, there was comparatively
7       little concern for chronology which appears partly linked to the legacy of
8       Evolutionism which saw non-Europeans as ‘peoples without history’. This
9       may be an over-simpliﬁcation, however, especially if one looks at a work such
30111   as the Marquis de Nadaillac’s Pre-historic America (1885), which distinguishes
1       several periods ‘from the shell-heap to the mound, from the mound to the
2       pueblo, from the pueblo to the structures of Mexico, Central America and
3       Peru’ (de Nadaillac 1885: 534). However, it is telling that he was French,
4       not American, and his book was an explicit copy of his earlier work on pre-
5       historic Europe. Nevertheless, it might be noted that the book was edited
6       (and its themes endorsed) by William Dall, a prominent North American
7       archaeologist who made use of stratigraphic observation in his studies.
8          For North American archaeologists, the major issues in the latter part
9       of the nineteenth century revolved around the antiquity of human occu-
40111   pation and the origin of numerous mounds in the Ohio and Mississippi
1       valleys, which some claimed had been built by a ‘lost race’ rather than by
2       ancestors of present-day North American Indians (Dixon 1913: 549; Meltzer
3       1985: 252). Through the efforts of the Bureau of American Ethnology under
44111
                                                                      Finding the past   35

1111    the guidance of Wesley Powell and later Cyrus Thomas, both issues were
2       resolved, with most unanimity centred on the latter issue. Putnam, at the
3       Peabody Museum, also contributed greatly to destroying the ‘lost race’ theory
4       of the mound-builders but, in opposition to Powell, argued for a more
5       ancient origin for the North American Indians. The important point here
6       is that the only issue of chronological signiﬁcance was over the very long
7       term – between palaeolithic and present-day Indians – and when Powell’s
8       views eventually prevailed, North American prehistory became more or less
9       a single period discipline. Part of this undoubtedly relates to the close asso-
1011    ciation between archaeological and ethnographic work by the Bureau, and,
1       while destroying the lost race hypothesis of mound-builders was an impor-
2       tant development, the very premise of this association also underpinned
3111    the continuity perceived between contemporary and prehistoric North
4       American Indians (Meltzer 1985: 255).
5          It is this lack of concern for chronology which accounts for the relative
6       obscurity of those early stratigraphic excavations. Finding any published work
7       on ﬁeld methods for this period is hard – indeed much information undoubt-
8       edly lies in ﬁeldnotes and manuscripts (Reyman 1989: 41–2). Yet this fact
9       itself is instructive – in many ways, the issue of who conducted, and when, the
20111   ﬁrst stratigraphic excavation is a red herring. The larger point is that there was
1       no standardised or widely adopted approach to ﬁeldwork in North America
2       before the 1930s; indeed before 1900 there was little agreement over how to
3       evaluate archaeological discoveries, except on a personal basis (Hinsley 1976;
4       Meltzer 1985: 254). Putnam’s address from which I quoted above ended with
5       an appeal for all archaeologists to follow similar principles; at the heart of the
6       drive for the professionalisation of archaeology was ﬁeldwork which one could
7       trust. Hinsley raises this issue both for Putnam at the Peabody and Powell at
8       the BAE, in terms of their seeking people to conduct ﬁeldwork for their
9       respective institutions (Hinsley 1974: 41; 1985: 62). This essentially came
30111   down to the need for a full and proper record of the excavation, as Putnam’s
1       references to measured plans, sections and photographs intimate, and while
2       articulated differently from Pitt Rivers’ call for the total record, the basic issue
3       of trust, i.e. reliable data, was the same.
4          The period between 1870 and 1900 was a period of major transition in
5       many ways in North America, as in Europe, with, on the one hand, a ﬁeld-
6       work which was largely driven by the desire to build and enhance museum
7       collections, and, on the other, one which also clearly recognised the impor-
8       tance of context. But, unlike Europe, the meaning of this context was quite
9       different: in Europe, context was chronological due to the evolutionary
40111   interpretation of prehistory; in North America, context was far more ethno-
1       graphic due to the anthropological perspective. This view, however, was
2       soon to change, largely through the inﬂuential ﬁgure of Franz Boas. Within
3       the second decade of the twentieth century, culture change became the
44111
        36   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    issue, and it was largely articulated through a combination of stratigraphic
2       excavations and seriation. But it was also mediated by a very different
3       conception of culture which rejected evolutionism outright.
4
5
        Culture history and the assemblage (1920–1960)
6
7
        The Wheeler–Kenyon school and stratigraphy in Britain
8
9       It has been an often raised point that Pitt Rivers’ exhaustive approach to
1011    ﬁeldwork was almost ignored by his successors as few excavators reproduced
1       his standards, at least in Britain (Petrie’s ﬁeldwork was in Egypt). Mortimer
2       Wheeler clearly regarded Pitt Rivers as a model:
3
4            Between 1880 and 1900 General Pitt Rivers in Cranborne Chase had
5            brought archaeological digging and recording to a remarkable degree
6            of perfection, and had presented his methods and results meticulously
7            in several imposing volumes. Then what? Nothing. Nobody paid the
8            slightest attention to the old man. One of his assistants had even
9            proceeded to dig up a lake-village much as Schliemann had dug up
20111        Troy or St. John Hope Silchester: like potatoes.
1                               (Wheeler 1958: 55; also see Wheeler 1954: 13–14)
2
3       This assistant who dug a site ‘like potatoes’ was none other than H. St
4       George Gray, the site being Glastonbury in Somerset (Bulleid and Gray
5       1911/1917). But how bad was this site in fact? Gray joined Arthur Bulleid
6       in 1902 after the latter had already been excavating the site for several
7       years in the 1890s; fresh from Cranborne Chase, Gray’s contribution seems
8       to have been predominantly on the ﬁnds rather than the excavated struc-
9       tures, and indeed Bulleid’s excavation and recording appears to have been
30111   as good, if not even better than Pitt Rivers’ – scale plans were drawn with
1       most of the ﬁnds plotted on them, and full feature and ﬁnds descriptions
2       are given in the ﬁnal report (Barrett 1987: 412–13). In general, the only
3       criticisms that can be made about the excavation are the same as those
4       applicable to Pitt Rivers – namely lack of stratigraphic information, although
5       perhaps the location of ﬁnds was not always as precise as with Pitt Rivers.
6       Corroborating this picture was another site excavated by Gray between
7       1908 and 1913 – Maumbury Rings in Dorset, a multi-period site; this appears
8       to have been done almost exactly following Pitt Rivers’ methods, from
9       trenching techniques to recording (Bradley 1975: 5–6).
40111      Wheeler then is certainly wrong in describing the decades after Pitt
1       Rivers as retrograde; rather, it appears that he has exaggerated the bril-
2       liance of Pitt Rivers. Wheeler himself gained one of his ﬁrst experiences
3       of ﬁeld archaeology as a young student with Jocelyn Plunkett Bushe-Fox
44111
                                                                    Finding the past   37

1111    at the excavations of the Roman town at Wroxeter in 1913. Looking at
2       this site report, one sees that it is very similar in layout to Pitt Rivers’
3       Excavations in Cranborne Chase, with detailed discussion of the site followed
4       by ﬁnds ‘reports’. Measured plans and sections are shown and in all respects
5       it is very difﬁcult to say it is of a lower standard than Pitt Rivers’ reports.
6       What is clear is that Bushe-Fox was conscious of the importance of both
7       plan and stratigraphy and the last site report of 1914 shows three phases
8       of building development on the site (Bushe-Fox 1916; plates XXX–XXXII).
9          This is way ahead of anything Pitt Rivers did; what is signiﬁcant, though,
1011    is that Bushe-Fox’s concern for stratigraphy was partly in order to produce
1       local Romano-British pottery sequences, as he highlights the importance
2       of ‘well-stratiﬁed deposits’ in the discussions of coarse pottery (e.g. Bushe-
3111    Fox 1913: 68). It is no coincidence, then, to ﬁnd he worked with Petrie
4       in Egypt, nor that his excavations at Wroxeter were preceded by a study
5       on dating Roman pottery from excavations at the Roman fort at Corbridge
6       the previous year (Bushe-Fox 1912). It is also perhaps no coincidence that
7       Mortimer Wheeler’s ﬁrst work was on Roman pottery, a work he began
8       after the 1913 season at Wroxeter.
9          Through his experience at Wroxeter and on sites in Essex after the war,
20111   Wheeler developed his ideas on ﬁeld methodology, particularly stratigraphy,
1       and it was in Wales that these ﬁrst crystallised at the Roman fort of
2       Segontium in 1921–2 (Wheeler 1958: 60). He clearly saw the publication
3       of Segontium as a key moment, drawing attention to a section of a cellar
4       where, as he puts it, ‘the whole bones of the matter are’ (Wheeler
5       1956: 60; see Figure 2). Soon after, in 1927 in an address to the Royal
6       Society of Arts, he pronounced stratiﬁcation as the key principle of exca-
7       vation, and through a number of examples, particularly of Roman sites, he
8
9
30111
1
2
3
4
5
6
7
8
9
40111
1
2
3       Figure 2 Wheeler’s section at the Roman fort of Segontium
44111
        38   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    demonstrated how the basic geological concept can be applied on archae-
2       ological sites; natural stratiﬁcation such as the lake bed clays of Sweden
3       illustrated ‘the principle of deﬁnite chronological sequence represented by
4       the superposition of stratum over stratum in the soil. This principle lies at
5       the basis of all archaeological excavation’ (Wheeler 1927: 817). His follower
6       Kathleen Kenyon afﬁrmed the same principle: ‘The science of excavation
7       is dependent on the interpretation of the stratiﬁcation of a site, that is to
8       say the layers of soil associated with it’ (Kenyon 1953: 69).
9          However, years before, J. P. Droop in his Archaeological Excavation (1915)
1011    had preached the importance of stratigraphy and even gives an example of
1       stratigraphic interpretation rather similar to Wheeler’s in his 1927 article
2       (Droop 1915: 75–7). It is Wheeler, however, who gives the classic example
3       – which he uses later in his book, Archaeology from the Earth – of what can
4       happen if stratigraphic excavation is not followed. A buried wall will cut
5       through certain layers, be contemporary with others, and sealed by still
6       more, ‘yet the ﬁrst instinct of the inexperienced excavator when he discovers
7       a buried wall is at once to cut alongside it in order to see where it goes!
8       Thereby destroying beyond amend just that evidence which might have
9       made his discovery a real and living addition to knowledge’ (Wheeler 1927:
20111   824; also see Wheeler 1954: 73–7; and Kenyon 1953: 90). Wheeler might
1       have been thinking of Petrie when he wrote this, for in his Methods and
2       Aims in Archaeology Petrie recommends just what Wheeler abhors: ‘In the
3       case of tracing a building, trenches cut along the lines of the walls are a
4       good beginning; and then if more is wanted, the plan is clear and the rooms
5       can be emptied with foresight’ (Petrie 1904: 41).
6          The importance of stratigraphic excavation is made clear in his criticism
7       of arbitrary digging, where the ‘notion of peeling off the successive strata
8       in conformity with their proper bed-lines, and thus ensuring the accurate
9       isolation of structural phases and relevant artefacts, is not even considered’
30111   (Wheeler 1954: 53–4). Throughout Wheeler’s examples, the importance of
1       stratigraphy is usually referenced to its role in providing chronological infor-
2       mation; this is ironic given that Petrie’s concerns were practically the same.
3       How is it, though, that for Wheeler stratiﬁcation came to take on so much
4       more signiﬁcance than it did for Petrie? One may point to the fact that
5       Wheeler was working a great deal on deeply stratiﬁed sites (e.g. Roman
6       towns) – but while this may have played a part, it cannot be the whole
7       answer as Petrie too worked on such sites in Egypt. The answer must relate
8       to the changing concept of culture within which Wheeler was working; to
9       address this, it is worth examining the ﬁeld techniques of Wheeler a little
40111   more closely, along with those of his contemporaries, especially Kathleen
1       Kenyon.
2          It is quite clear from both Wheeler’s and Kenyon’s work that stratig-
3       raphy was the section; the two were inextricably linked and in this, perhaps,
44111   we can see something of the same ‘visual’ sense of an excavation as Petrie
                                                                     Finding the past   39

1111    had in terms of ﬁnds and the plan. In a similar manner to Petrie, Wheeler
2       identiﬁes three basic methods of investigating a site: trial trenching
3       (sondages), open area and substantive trenching (Wheeler 1954: 63). The
4       ﬁrst and last are rather similar in execution if not in strategy, involving
5       limited cuttings – though in the former it is to test or attempt to uncover
6       the archaeology, while in the latter it is directed at speciﬁc types of situation
7       such as across an earthwork or to link up open areas. Wheeler’s main
8       method, as Petrie’s, was the ‘open area’. This term has different connota-
9       tions, however, especially today when we often use the phrase ‘open area’
1011    to denote absence of baulks, as distinct from Wheeler’s ‘box’ system (see
1       p. 52). For Wheeler, there were many ways to excavate an open area but
2       only one satisﬁed all the necessary criteria for a proper, stratigraphic exca-
3111    vation: the box or grid method (ibid.: 64–8; also see Atkinson 1946: 41–3;
4       Kenyon 1953: 94–5).
5          The method, ﬁrst employed at Maiden Castle (Wheeler 1943), sub-
6       divided the area to be excavated into a series of squares on a grid (as Petrie
7       recommended) but leaving baulks (i.e. standing sections) along the axes of
8       the grid so that in effect the open area is divided into a series of ‘boxes’
9       within which excavation proceeds (Figure 3). In employing his method,
20111   Wheeler tended to use a control square (or, similarly, before opening an
1       area, dig a trial trench or sondage) in order to establish the nature of the
2       stratigraphy on the site. The control pit helped guide the progress of
3       the excavation for ‘stratiﬁcation must, by its nature, always be controlled
4       from the side, i.e. from the side of the control-pit, since it obviously cannot
5       be controlled prophetically from the top: vertical digging ﬁrst, horizontal digging
6       afterwards, must be the rule’ (Wheeler 1954: 66; emphasis in original). It
7       is worth reiterating that phrase, ‘stratiﬁcation must, by its nature, always be
8       controlled from the side’; stratiﬁcation is the section. This, as we shall see,
9       is not the way we view things today and the difference is quite telling.
30111      Wheeler himself does not give much information on how one goes about
1       correlating the different strata between the squares or even between sections
2       within a square except to say that it must be continually done as excavation
3       proceeds down (Wheeler 1954: 66–7). He was aware of the potential hazards
4       of a section-based stratigraphy where layers may not ‘follow through’ (ibid.).
5       His follower, Kathleen Kenyon, gave a more detailed example illustrating
6       the procedure which it is instructive to follow for what it reveals about a
7       section-based conception of stratigraphy (for a later, similar example, see
8       Alexander 1970: 71–4). In her two examples (Kenyon 1953: 130–1; ﬁgures
9       13–14), one of a prehistoric site and another Roman, she shows how one
40111   lays out the different squares in adjacent columns, the different layers in
1       each listed in rows, producing a sort of matrix (in the strict mathematical
2       sense – which is ironic given the use of this word for the modern system
3       developed by Harris; see p. 56). The layers are then placed on the same
44111   row if they either represent the same deposit or are contemporary, and

1111    can be adjusted up or down according to their place in the sequence (i.e.
2       later or earlier). Moreover, each row or a group of rows is collected under
3       a periodisation or phasing, as we would say today. The Kenyon ‘matrix’ is
4       indeed almost like a visual analogue of the section itself and stresses a
5       simpler, unilinear sequence of layering (Figure 4).
6          Throughout Kenyon’s and Wheeler’s texts there is a very clear bias
7       towards seeing stratiﬁcation chieﬂy in terms of layers, which, as Harris has
8       pointed out, is an overly geological conception of stratiﬁcation (Harris 1989:
9       12–13). Wheeler himself in his 1927 paper begins his discussion of archae-
1011    ological stratigraphy by using the geological example of lake-bed clays
1       (Wheeler 1927: 816–17). Pyddoke’s whole book Stratiﬁcation for the
2       Archaeologist (1961) focuses very much on geomorphological processes.
3111    Today the debate is still alive about the geological conception of archae-
4       ological stratigraphy, especially between Harris and proponents of
5       archaeostratigraphic classiﬁcation (Gasche and Tunca 1983; Farrand 1984;
6       Stein 1987; Harris 1991; also see Chapter 5). However, the point to note
7       here is the effect such a bias has on giving over-emphasis to the concept
8       of a layer in archaeological stratigraphy.
9          Although Kenyon (1953: 69) afﬁrmed that ‘the term stratiﬁcation may
20111   also be applied in the archaeological sense to things which are not strictly
1       layers at all, but pits, banks, trenches, in fact any disturbance in the soil’,
2       there is still a real sense that the layer provided the model for a strati-
3       graphic unit. Note how she describes such non-layers: ‘in fact any disturbance
4       in the soil’. Throughout the chapter on excavation techniques, such things
5       are repeatedly described as disturbances, that is, deﬁned in reference to
6       the layer which they break. Indeed both in her and Wheeler’s books, there
7       is a clear sense that the two basic components of a site are layers and
8       structures; tellingly, structures are never given numbers, only layers are
9       (and disturbances of layers). More signiﬁcantly interfaces and/or cuts do
30111   not receive numbers – but only solid deposits (see Kenyon’s section in
1       Figure 4).
2          In other words, even though Kenyon recognised a pit as a unit distinct
3       from a layer, the concept of what a pit was, stratigraphically, was very much
4       in terms of a layer – that is, what was recorded was the pit ﬁll, not the pit
5       cut. This is what got numbered, not the line deﬁning the extent of the
6       disturbance. Harris also quotes Kenyon in this respect, but he tends to give
7       Wheeler and Kenyon the credit for recognising the interface as a unit of
8       stratiﬁcation, when in fact this is misleading (Harris 1989: 11). Certainly
9       they recognised the importance of the interface as distinguishing between
40111   deposits, a fact which comes out forcefully in their espousal of strong lines
1       in section drawing (see p. 208); yet to say that they recognised the impor-
2       tance of the interface as a unit in its own right is incorrect. This was surely
3       Harris’s major contribution (see p. 153; Harris 1991).

        42   Critical approaches to fieldwork

44111
                                                                   Finding the past   43

1111       This idea that all units of stratigraphy are essentially layers (or deposits)
2       is still common outside archaeology in Britain and I have witnessed sheer
3       incomprehension at the idea that one might record a cut as a stratigraphic
4       event. This is clearly not necessarily a case of ‘bad archaeology’ (however
5       much we might like to think so), but must relate to something fundamental
6       in the way the archaeological record is perceived. I think it is important
7       to look at the wider milieu of Wheeler and Kenyon, especially to try see
8       in what way their perspective on excavation linked up with that of the
9       concept of culture.
1011
1
        Wheeler, Bersu and the culture-group concept in
2
        European archaeology
3111
4       Although Wheeler was undoubtedly extremely inﬂuential on British ﬁeld
5       techniques, he had a rival; Gerhard Bersu, who brought German ‘open
6       area’ methods into Britain in the later 1930s, inﬂuenced a whole genera-
7       tion of famous archaeologists such as Grahame Clark, W. P. Grimes and
8       Christopher and Jacquetta Hawkes (Evans 1989). Given Bersu’s background
9       in the ‘homeland’ of the culture-group concept and its concern for settle-
20111   ment archaeology, his focus on large, open area plans is perhaps unsurprising.
1       Indeed, settlement archaeology extended back into the nineteenth century
2       in much of mainland Europe with C. Schuchardt’s excavations and recog-
3       nition of posthole structures since the 1870s (Malina and Va šíček 1990:
4       49). Elsewhere on mainland Europe, people such as van Giffen in the
5       Netherlands and Gudmund Hatt in Denmark were, like Bersu, conducting
6       open area excavations without baulks/sections speciﬁcally to be able to
7       discern such structures (Barker 1982: 16–20). Bersu’s inaugural project was
8       the excavation of the Iron Age settlement at Little Woodbury, conducted
9       on behalf of the Prehistoric Society in 1938 and 1939 (Bersu 1938, 1940).
30111   However, Bersu’s method has perhaps gone down as more pioneering than
1       it really was; his excavation was not strictly ‘open area’, but consisted of a
2       series of parallel strip trenches which were backﬁlled with the spoil from
3       the ‘seams’ between them which were dug afterwards (Bersu 1938: 42; for
4       a contemporary critique of this method by a Wheelerian, see Atkinson
5       1946: 43; earlier critics include Droop 1915: 22).
6          As Evans points out, only in the ﬁnal plan does it look like an open
7       area excavation (Evans 1989: 443); and yet exactly the same can be said
8       about Wheeler’s excavations: when completed, the baulks of a Wheelerian
9       grid were removed to recover the whole ‘plan’ so that the ﬁnal result might
40111   look like a section-free open area. There is little difference in the plans of
1       Bersu and Wheeler, except perhaps stylistically as Bersu often published a
2       naturalistic as well as schematic plan (e.g. see Bersu 1940, ﬁgures 20 and
3       21), although it is his naturalistic sections which are usually recalled, in
44111
        44   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    opposition to Wheeler’s ‘compromise’ sections (see Chapter 6 for further
2       discussion of these points). Many of the differences between Bersu and
3       Wheeler have perhaps been exaggerated – certainly they differed in their
4       graphic representation as just mentioned, but possibly most signiﬁcantly it
5       was in their interpretation of prehistoric sites. Wheeler worked a lot on
6       Roman as well as prehistoric sites, and much of the antagonism between
7       him and Bersu was over the ‘pit-dwelling’ view of pits (Evans 1989: 444).
8       Bersu’s major inﬂuence was undoubtedly not on ﬁeld techniques in general
9       but upon the importance of recognising posthole structures in the British
1011    context and their interpretation as houses, replacing the pit-dwelling theory
1       (ibid.: 445). Even Pitt Rivers was not very good at this and missed most
2       structures in the areas he stripped (Barrett et al. 1991: 13). Yet while an
3       open area is essential for such recognition, Bersu’s method was not the
4       most conducive to it, and certainly no more so than Wheeler’s.
5          While it may be fair to say that Bersu gave more emphasis to the plan
6       than Wheeler did, it is not necessarily the case that Wheeler gave greater
7       signiﬁcance to sections. This caricature was fuelled by the followers of Bersu
8       – in their book Prehistoric Britain, the Hawkeses opposed horizontal
9       and vertical approaches, associating them with modern-sociological and
20111   traditional-historical schools of archaeology respectively, albeit paying lip
1       service to an ideal which combines both (Hawkes and Hawkes 1947: 167).
2       And yet this is exactly what Wheeler was attempting; in the statement
3       already quoted, although he afﬁrmed that the ‘vertical’ precedes the ‘hori-
4       zontal’, he still required both. Certainly Wheeler was almost on a mission
5       to raise awareness of stratigraphic relations and the importance of the
6       section, but it is also quite clear that such a method as the box system was
7       an attempt to ﬁnd the optimum compromise between section and plan views
8       of the excavation.
9          This last is quite crucial to understanding the context of Wheeler’s
30111   methods in terms of the concept of culture at the time. Wheeler’s concern
1       for stratigraphy meant that the section was raised in value, not that the
2       plan was devalued. The force of this comes out when we consider the rela-
3       tionship of these methods to the contemporary culture-group concept
4       (Barker 1982: 15; Harris 1989: 11). Petrie’s concern for chronology was
5       expressed very much as a relative phenomenon; what mattered was sequence,
6       not absolute time (see p. 31). Contrast that with Wheeler’s statement on
7       chronology:
8
9            To-day, when the traditional precision of history has nevertheless been
40111        extensively supplemented by broadly based cultural studies, it may seem
1            reactionary and perverse to reafﬁrm, as I do, at the beginning of a book
2            on archaeology in the ﬁeld that mere dates are still of primary and ulti-
3            mate and unrelenting importance. And by dates I mean not simply
44111        those nebulous phases and sequences, those date-substitutes with which
                                                                  Finding the past   45

1111        archaeologists often try to bluff us. I mean time in hard ﬁgures. I mean
2           Bradshaw.
3                                                                (Wheeler 1954: 38)
4
5       Wheeler even tells us why we need ‘time in hard ﬁgures’, and this is the
6       crucial point:
7
8           First, without an absolute chronology cultures of different regions cannot
9           be assessed: in other words, the vital causative factors of human ‘progress’
1011        cannot be authoritatively reconstructed, and may be widely misunder-
1           stood. Secondly, the ﬂuctuating tempo of human achievement – cannot
2           be estimated: the lightning ﬂash, for example, of Periclean Athens, or
3111        the glow of the slow-moving riverine civilizations.
4                                                                           (ibid.: 39)
5
6       Ignoring the speciﬁc interpretations Wheeler evokes which may sound
7       dated, the signiﬁcant aspect to absolute chronology which he highlights is
8       its status as a measure – it enables cultural variability to be commensurable
9       both in terms of change and the rate of change. The afﬁrmation of an
20111   absolute, independent chronology made by Wheeler stands in contrast to
1       Petrie. Petrie’s evolutionary views are, for all intents and purposes, iden-
2       tical to Wheeler’s, yet the two have quite contrary ways of articulating
3       them: for Wheeler, assessing the progress of civilisation necessitated an
4       independent measure of time, while for Petrie such a measure was super-
5       ﬂuous since all that was necessary was sequence. The reasons for this
6       difference are discussed in more detail in the next chapter, but the crux
7       would seem to lie with the fact that for Petrie there was only one History,
8       one Story of Progress, and the details of when this happened or at what
9       rate were merely incidental. For Wheeler, on the other hand, history was
30111   multiple, civilisation had many faces and to understand it in all its variety
1       meant that just those things which Petrie downplayed, rose to pivotal impor-
2       tance. I have argued elsewhere that the subsequent development of
3       radiocarbon and other dating techniques can be seen in this changing
4       conception of time to prehistory rather than as a simple narrative of progress
5       in archaeological knowledge (Lucas 1997).
6          The rise of the culture group, of a pluralist conception of culture, created
7       the necessity for an absolute framework within which to analyse that
8       pluralism – space and time. Cultural difference was no longer directly rela-
9       tive, but had to be compared indirectly through the medium of space–time.
40111   In order to delineate cultural horizons under such a view, time and space
1       had to be kept quite distinct, acting like a mutual check upon the other’s
2       validity. That such a model of the necessary foundation or basis upon which
3       to understand the past should also be seen in excavation techniques is
44111   perhaps not surprising. Indeed as this distinction became more important,
        46   Critical approaches to fieldwork
