6
7
8       one would expect a debate in excavation techniques over the problems of
9       reconciling horizontal and vertical control. This is perhaps just what the
40111   Wheelerian grid system attempted. The concern in excavation for both
1       horizontal and vertical control is a direct expression of the wider conception
2       of control in interpreting prehistory through the axes of space and time;
3       locating a stratigraphic layer on a site both in plan and section is a direct
44111   correlate of the need to ﬁx a culture group in prehistory in time and space,
                                                                Finding the past   47

1111    illustrated in the charts or tables of cultures seen in many textbooks, most
2       famously in Childe’s Danube in Prehistory (1929). This is not to say that a
3       layer is comparable to a culture group, although it is interesting to look at
4       one of Wheeler’s sections from his Indian campaigns (Figure 5; Wheeler
5       1954: 48; ﬁgure 9) where major discontinuities in the section (i.e. strati-
6       graphic sequence) are read as major changes in culture groups – a trait
7       more common in North America. Now is a good point at which catch up
8       with developments there and see how similar – or different – ﬁeldwork was.
9
1011
        North America: stratigraphy and culture history
1
2       The early decades of the twentieth century in North America saw the rejec-
3111    tion of cultural evolutionism and the adoption of historical particularism
4       under the lead of Franz Boas and his students (Willey and Sabloff 1993: 91).
5       In effect, this was comparable to the development of the culture-group con-
6       cept in Europe and spurred the whole development of methods to recover
7       cultural differentiation across time and space. At the turn of the century,
8       spatial, that is regional/geographical, cultural complexes were beginning to
9       be systematically articulated, particularly through the work of W. H. Holmes,
20111   who aimed to synthesise the distribution of archaeological material with
1       known ethnographic areas (Holmes 1914). However, as Meltzer says, this
2       work became obsolete almost as soon as it was published because the very
3       idea of ﬁxed, unchanging cultures was being overturned (Meltzer 1985: 258).
4       It was not so much the spatial differences which were problematic as their
5       association with an unchanging ethnographic present. Thus it was the
6       chronological aspect which gained most attention and became the dominant
7       issue for American archaeology for the ﬁrst half of the twentieth century as
8       the primary means to developing culture-historical ‘area syntheses’.
9          This emphasis is clearly borne out in the subsequent development of ﬁeld
30111   techniques, with what has been called, ‘the Stratigraphic Revolution’
1       (Willey and Sabloff 1993: 97; Browman and Givens 1996), which at ﬁrst
2       sight mirrors that in Britain under Wheeler. The methods and concerns of
3       North American archaeologists were, however, somewhat different. Their
4       primary purpose in employing stratigraphic excavation was to recover a
5       sequence of material culture assemblages which, when compared or quan-
6       tiﬁed, enabled a detailed chronological differentiation of an area. While
7       stratigraphic excavation was important in understanding the site, its main
8       aim was to prove cross-site or regionally signiﬁcant sequences. Second,
9       although it has been called ‘stratigraphic excavation’, this has not quite
40111   the same meaning as it did – or does – in Britain. While this practice in
1       North America, usually meant recognition of stratiﬁcation, excavation often
2       proceeded by arbitrary spits rather than by the physical strata. This differ-
3       ence has been described as digging by ‘metrical, as opposed to natural
44111   stratigraphy’ (Willey and Sabloff 1993: 107; also Haag 1986: 68). In Britain,
        48   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    the same difference is described as arbitrary versus stratigraphic excavation
2       (Harris 1989). Without going into which is the best terminology, the rele-
3       vant point here is that the ‘stratigraphic revolution’ in North America was
4       clearly quite different from that in Britain at the same time.
5          The key inﬂuential works on carefully controlled ‘stratigraphic’ excavation
6       were by Manuel Gamio in Mexico (1913) and Nels C. Nelson (1916) and the
7       Kidders (1917) in the Southwest. Gamio’s work came about under Franz Boas’
8       instigation and tutelage – Boas had been instrumental in setting up an Inter-
9       national School of American Archaeology and Ethnology in Mexico in 1910
1011    and Gamio was Boas’ ﬁrst doctoral student at Columbia, subsequently taking
1       up a fellowship at the School in 1911 (Browman and Givens 1996: 90). One
2       of the primary aims of the school was the establishment of a regional chronol-
3       ogy after the Berlin professor Eduard Seler (who was director in the ﬁrst year)
4       had noted the great diversity of material culture in the valley of Mexico. In
5       reporting the ﬁrst years of work by the school, Boas writes:
6
7            The question arose, how to determine the chronological sequence of
8            these remains. A survey of the wells and brick-yards in the environ-
9            ment of the City of Mexico showed that this question could be studied
20111        by observations on geological sequence of strata. In the year 1911–12
1            a careful investigation was conducted in one single excavation in a
2            brick-yard at San Miguel Amantla near Atzcapotzalco, which was
3            carried out under the supervision of the Director by Mr Manuel Gamio.
4            The result of this excavation was the deﬁnite proof that three distinct
5            civilizations were found in the Valley of Mexico, – a primitive one,
6            which is found in the bottom layers; a second one, identical with that
7            of San Juan Teotihuacán; a third one, the Aztec.
8                                                                  (Boas 1915: 385)
9
30111   While Gamio’s work initiated a spate of similar stratigraphic excavations
1       by the school under Boas’ supervision, a similar movement was occurring
2       in the Southwest under the impetus of Nelson. Nelson, who had earlier
3       excavated Californian shell mounds similar to Uhle’s but with uninspired
4       results, excavated a deep midden in the Southwest in a similar manner to
5       that of Gamio, using arbitrary units, with even more impressive results:
6
7            At that time it was noticed in a general way that different types of
8            pottery fragments prevailed at different levels but no effort was made,
9            until too late, to keep them separate. . . . A visibly stratiﬁed section of
40111        the refuse exposure showing no evidence of disturbance was selected
1            and a block of this measuring 3 by 6 feet on the horizontal and nearly
2            10 feet deep was excavated. I performed this work with my own hands
3            . . . potsherds from each separate foot of debris were kept apart. . . .
44111                                                                    (Nelson 1916)
                                                                  Finding the past   49

1111    It seems unlikely that Nelson was inﬂuenced by Uhle, especially given that
2       his tutor, Alfred Kroeber, had been very scathing of Uhle’s work (Browman
3       and Givens 1996: 82–3). Nor, despite his association with George Pepper,
4       did stratigraphic excavation seem to take hold with him from this source
5       (Reyman 1989); rather, it seems that only after spending a season in Spain
6       with famous European archaeologists did he return and adapt these strati-
7       graphic methods to the Southwest in 1914 (ibid.: 83–4). Kidder, aware of
8       both Gamio’s and Nelson’s work, was deeply complimentary, and even
9       though his own ﬁeldwork using stratigraphic methods came after them, it
1011    is unfair to paint him as an imitator. Kidder’s approach was quite different,
1       and his subsequent inﬂuence greater than that of Nelson or Gamio. Kidder’s
2       training was under the famous Egyptologist George Reisner at Harvard, who
3111    employed stratigraphic methods on his sites in the Near East and intro-
4       duced a course on ﬁeldwork in 1911 (ibid.: 87). By 1913, Kidder was already
5       thinking that stratigraphic excavation would resolve problems in sequencing
6       types, and by 1915 had started work at Pecos.
7          Kidder’s method stands out above Gamio’s and Nelson’s in that instead
8       of using arbitrary units of excavation, he followed the natural stratigraphy:
9
20111       The Pecos test pits were not divided into exactly equal cuts, as was
1           done by Mr Nelson, but were laid out in nearly equal divisions based
2           on sand, ash, or other strata which indicated actual levels of deposi-
3           tion during the formation on the mound. This was necessitated by the
4           fact that the Pecos deposits were for the most part laid down on sloping
5           or irregular surfaces, and cuts made on arbitrary chosen plane levels
6           would have resulted in the splitting or cross-cutting of strata.
7                                                    (Kidder and Kidder 1917: 340)
8
9       Ultimately, what matters is not who was ﬁrst, or who inﬂuenced whom, but
30111   that by 1920, the use of stratigraphic methods – natural or arbitrary – were
1       widespread. The actual procedures used in such stratigraphic excavation, par-
2       ticularly on initial exploratory ﬁeldwork, varied but generally two main
3       methods seem to have been employed; one was a ‘level-stripping’ method
4       whereby a trench or pit was cumulatively excavated, either by arbitrary/
5       metrical or stratigraphic/natural units, the other, almost the inverse of the
6       last, was the ‘block’ method. This involved trenching all around a square,
7       leaving a standing block or column which could then be excavated, again
8       arbitrarily or stratigraphically. This method was employed by Nelson and
9       Kidder but also subsequently by the Tennessee Valley Authority (TVA)
40111   excavation programmes which began in the 1930s (Haag 1986: 68). The
1       advantage it had over the other, was that the vertical section could actually
2       be used to guide excavation, whereas in the former, it can only serve as a post
3       facto check; in terms of ﬁxing ﬁnds to correct layers, the ‘block’ method
44111    had clear advantages. Superﬁcially, the ‘block’ method also appears to be a
        50   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    negative version of Wheeler’s ‘box’ method. It does not seem, however, as if
2       whole areas were excavated like this but rather single pits/trenches, and it
3       was more common to still excavate such blocks arbitrarily rather than by
4       layer. Nevertheless, there is a clear common conception in North America
5       and Britain of stratigraphy being understood through the section.
6          As if further mirroring the developments in Britain, ﬁeldwork in North
7       America also developed a concern for open area excavation in the 1930s,
8       particularly in the eastern states where the traces of structures survived
9       largely as postholes, etc., rather than as standing architecture as in the
1011    pueblos of the southwest or stone ruins of Central and South America.
1       Previously, plans of ‘features’ were often only recovered from reconstruc-
2       tions because of the slice method, as pioneered by Putnam and others (see
3       p. 33), but an increase in interest for such plans meant that an open area
4       stripping or peeling method became more desirable. The major impetus for
5       ﬁeldwork changes came under rescue situations with the Works Progress
6       Administration (WPA), Tennessee Valley Authority (TVA) and Civilian
7       Conservation Corps (CCC) projects in the late 1930s and 1940s, and the
8       individuals who supervised them such as William Webb and Fay Cooper-
9       Cole (Dunnell 1986b: 28). While initially under the over-arching authority
20111   of the Committee for State Archaeological Surveys (CSAS) established in
1       1920, these projects also led to the formation of the Society of American
2       Archaeologists (SAA) in 1934 (and its journal, American Antiquity) which
3       took over the functions of the CSAS when it was disbanded in 1937, unable
4       to cope with the quantity of work (Pinsky 1992: 166–7; Setzler and Strong
5       1937). The formation of the SAA has also been seen as an attempt to
6       carve out a separate identity for archaeology in the four-ﬁeld discipline of
7       anthropology pioneered by Boas and expressed though the American
8       Anthropological Association (and its journal, American Anthropologist),
9       especially as Boas appears to have had little interest in archaeology – despite
30111   helping Gamio – because of its museum associations (Pinsky 1992: 174–5).
1          These rescue projects engendered a whole new generation of archaeolo-
2       gists and stimulated the demand for academic posts outside the restricted
3       ambit of Columbia and Harvard. They also established consistent sets of
4       ﬁeld procedures, including the use of site grids and standardised recording
5       forms. While Guthe published a general statement on ﬁeld methods in 1930
6       under the National Research Council, the ultimate codiﬁcation of the
7       methods developed in the 1930s and 1940s was Heizer’s Guide to Archae-
8       ological Field Methods, ﬁrst published in 1949 and, albeit vastly modiﬁed in
9       subsequent editions, still going strong (Hester et al. 1997). As elsewhere, a
40111   distinction was made between test or exploratory excavations which uncov-
1       ered a part of the site using trenching and area excavation which involved
2       stripping a larger expanse. In either case, Heizer identiﬁed two main
3       methods: the slice or vertical face method as developed by Putnam (some-
44111
                                                                  Finding the past   51

1111    times modiﬁed by stepping the faces, a technique known as level stripping),
2       and the unit-level method where the site is divided into separate square
3       units based on a grid, with each excavated independently and stratigraph-
4       ically (Heizer 1958: 40). The latter became the main method, especially
5       for open area sites and, as with Wheeler’s box system, it deals with both
6       the horizontal and vertical planes, exhibiting the same concerns for spatial
7       and temporal control. The advantage over the slice method was through
8       the better recovery of plans, and because units were excavated separately
9       a section was always available for checking the vertical stratigraphy.
1011       As in Britain, the new methods manifested themselves on the larger
1       canvas of area syntheses, exempliﬁed most famously in Kidder’s work at
2       Pecos:
3111
4           As originally planned, the work of the Pecos expedition was to be ﬁrst
5           intensive, then extensive. The intensive phase was to consist of the
6           excavation of the pueblo, and the determination by stratigraphic
7           methods of the sequence of the pottery types found at the site. During
8           the second or extensive phase we were to use our knowledge of the
9           sequence of the pottery types to arrange in their proper chronological
20111       order all other ruins which contained those types. This work was to
1           be accomplished by a thorough archaeological reconnaissance of the
2           Rio Grande drainage.
3                                                       (Kidder 1962 [1924]: 132)
4
5       Kidder’s work at Pecos led to a major conference to formulate a classiﬁca-
6       tory scheme which in turn spawned alternatives in response, such as those
7       by Harold Gladwin and William McKern. These are discussed in greater
8       detail in a later chapter, but whatever the classiﬁcations employed, such
9       area syntheses became a major goal of archaeologists in the 1930s to 1960s
30111   and were ultimately, as Willey and Sabloff note,
1
2           an ordering of the archaeological remains of a given area in a spatio-
3           temporal framework. The essence of this ordering was the archaeological
4           chronology chart, a diagram arranged with chronological periods in
5           vertical column and geographical subdivisions across the top, so that
6           the various culture units . . . could be placed in their appropriate boxes.
7                                                     (Willey and Sabloff 1993: 128)
8
9       The ultimate synthesis and zenith of culture classiﬁcations was undoubt-
40111   edly Indians Before Columbus, by Martin, Quimby and Collier, published
1       in 1947, which produced such charts for the whole of North America,
2       appended to a long, detailed and turgid text listing the various traits of
3       each culture group.
44111
        52   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    Cultural behaviour and context (1960–present day)
2
3       Winchester and ‘techniques’ of archaeological excavation
4       in Britain
5       By the 1960s and the rise of rescue archaeology the ‘myth’ of the open area
6       excavation as pioneered by Bersu was well established; in Graham Webster’s
7       Practical Archaeology, it is rated above the grid system for most sites except
8       certain kinds (e.g., barrows, earthworks) because of its emphasis on the plan
9       (Webster 1965: 86–7). But the open area excavation applauded by Webster,
1011    as for example that employed at Yeavering by Brian Hope-Taylor or at
1       Cheddar by Philip Rahtz, was not the same as Bersu’s open area. Much of
2       the difference is simply related to the use of mechanical excavators allowing
3       large areas to be opened all at once rather than successively. They were being
4       occasionally used on sites by the 1940s, but chieﬂy in a rescue context and
5       often just to cut sections or long trenches (see Atkinson 1946: 47). Atkinson
6       suggested that their use might become more common and for stripping larger
7       areas, especially on sites which consisted of subsoil features under an already
8       disturbed ploughsoil layer (ibid.: 220). The growth of rescue archaeology in
9       the 1960s undoubtedly stimulated their use to the extent that there are few
20111   sites dug today which do not employ a mechanical excavator to take off the
1       overburden (Figure 6; see Alexander 1970: 41 and 52–3).
2          Certainly having a truly open area affected the perception and inter-
3       pretation of sites (particularly for posthole buildings; see Barker 1969; also
4       Bradley 1997: 64–8), but in a way this change in the ‘way of seeing’ had
5       already happened; the ‘true’ open area came about twenty years after its
6       alleged advantageous effect, as we have seen. Large-scale, mechanical strip-
7       ping merely conﬁrmed what was anticipated. Webster’s Practical Archaeology
8       is, for the most part, notable for the way in which it is so similar to its
9       predecessors such as Atkinson’s Field Archaeology and Wheeler’s Archaeology
30111   from the Earth. For example, the same two kinds of entities – structures and
1       layers – are recognised by Kenyon as ‘ﬁrst, man-made constructions ranging
2       from walls to hearths, rubbish-pits and post-holes, and secondly, deposits
3       or layers . . . of material laid down by man’ (Webster 1965: 59). Signiﬁcantly,
4       though, ‘disturbances’ are now deﬁned as post-depositional natural events
5       such an root action and animal burrows, while postholes etc. are grouped
6       under structures (ibid.). There are innumerable other similarities one could
7       give, but despite all the rhetoric of the Bersu ‘school’ very little changed
8       in ﬁeld methods from Wheeler’s pioneering work in the 1920s. The really
9       signiﬁcant shifts in ﬁeld techniques did not occur through open area exca-
40111   vation speciﬁcally but in methods associated with it, formulated by the
1       Winchester Research Unit in the 1960s.
2          Fundamental to Martin Biddle’s decision to set up the Winchester
3       Research Unit in 1961 ‘was a swing in emphasis away from a concept of

44111
        54   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    excavation dominated by the requirements of a particular period, notably
2       that of Roman Britain, towards the examination without period bias of a
3       city or town as an overall archaeological entity’ (Jones 1984: 17).
4       Undoubtedly the urban context of Biddle’s excavations was a causative
5       factor, but all the same the concept of an excavation stripped of ‘period
6       bias’ cannot be totally derived from the multi-period nature of most urban
7       archaeology. There is surely more to it, especially as we consider the other
8       concept that grew alongside this, that of ‘problem-oriented’ excavation (see
9       Barker 1977: 4) and indeed this comes out when we look closely at the
1011    positive developments associated with this abandonment of period bias.
1          The ﬁrst expression of these new developments was publicly aired at a
2       research seminar at the Institute of Archaeology in London at the end of
3       1967, and published two years later (Biddle and Kjølbye-Biddle 1967, 1969).
4       Three ‘new ideas’ were expounded, two of which I shall focus on here:
5       metric, co-ordinate planning and open area excavation on deeply stratiﬁed
6       sites. The big difference between stripping an open area on a greenﬁeld
7       site with little stratigraphy and a brownﬁeld or urban site is very impor-
8       tant to appreciate. Most if not all the work of Bersu’s adherents had been
9       done on the former, where little if anything was lost by opening large areas
20111   at the expense of retaining baulk sections. The same cannot be said in an
1       urban context, however; one must appreciate the boldness of the Biddles’
2       use of it in such a situation:
3
4            One of the major problems in excavation is the need to combine the
5            conﬂicting requirements of horizontal and vertical observation and
6            record. This problem is particularly acute in the excavation of large
7            areas. The normal solution, ﬁrst devised by Sir Mortimer Wheeler at
8            Maiden Castle, has been to excavate a grid of squares divided by baulks.
9            In this way the excavation of a large area can be combined with a
30111        permanent record of the vertical stratiﬁcation. . . . There are, however,
1            serious disadvantages: the trench pattern is imposed on the site, often
2            to the detriment of the pattern of structures, and once established the
3            trench pattern is ﬁxed. The deeper the squares go, the more difﬁcult
4            it becomes to view and comprehend the structures as a whole. . . . On
5            a site with very complex stratiﬁcation, correlation of layers from one
6            side of a baulk to the other may be impossible.
7                                            (Biddle and Kjølbye-Biddle 1969: 211)
8
9       In coming up with a new method to reconcile the horizontal and vertical
40111   which did not necessitate the retention of baulks, the Biddles developed a
1       process of digging by ‘phase’. Essentially this involved the layout of a grid
2       of pegs across the site enabling the recording of features by a cartesian co-
3       ordinate system (compatible with the National Grid); temporary baulks/
44111
                                                                  Finding the past   55

1111    sections are maintained but only until a certain level is reached, deﬁned
2       by a ‘major layer’, then they are removed after proper recording (Biddle
3       and Kjølbye-Biddle 1969: 212). The whole process is then repeated, the
4       grid lowered as the site proceeds down and baulks either left in the same
5       place or relocated according to the archaeology at that level. The end result
6       is then primarily a series of ‘phase’ plans and ‘cumulative’ sections.
7          As the Biddles stated, such a method ‘required great site discipline and
8       well-trained workers’ (ibid.: 213). In a way, this method signalled the start
9       of the whole ‘mechanisation’ of excavation whereby digging becomes more
1011    a technical than interpretive process, a trend which reached its culmina-
1       tion in single-context recording and professional, ﬁeld (i.e. contract)
2       archaeology. Barker summed it up when he wrote in the ﬁrst edition of
3111    Techniques of Archaeological Excavation, that the digger was a . . . ‘techni-
4       cian producing evidence from the ground regardless of its date or function’
5       (Barker 1977: 53). This is diagrammatically represented in Alexander’s hier-
6       archy of the excavation team which not only grades people according to
7       their authority but includes objects such as trowels and excavators so that
8       an impression is given of a hierarchy from human down to machine
9       (Alexander 1970: 17, ﬁgure 1). Those people closest to the bottom are
20111   most associated with the machines, most machine-like. The so-called
1       democratisation of ﬁeld archaeology which some have seen occurring since
2       the rise of rescue archaeology and the Winchester method is directly linked
3       into this ‘technicisation’ (see Chapter 1).
4          While all this chimes well with the Biddles’ focus on an excavation free
5       of ‘period bias’, it also appears that concurrently excavation was being trans-
6       formed into a process more and more removed from the interpretation of
7       the past – and yet I do not think this is quite right. Maybe the digger is
8       removed from that interpretation, but not the supervisor or director. Yet
9       changes in the manner of interpretation in the ﬁeld did occur, but we must
30111   rather see this in relation to changing conceptions of what prehistory and
1       culture have meant since the 1960s. However, before I go into this, let me
2       complete the story of the developments at Winchester, particularly the
3       development of single-context recording.
4          The advantage of the Biddles’ method over Wheeler’s is clear in so far
5       as sections are always strategically placed with respect to the archaeology,
6       both for the clarity of the section and the plan. However, as the Biddles
7       recognise, at the point at which one pauses, ‘a qualitative decision is taken
8       which may not really reﬂect an equally important phase in the history of
9       the site’ (Biddle and Kjølbye-Biddle 1969: 213). In other words, the partic-
40111   ular level at which the director decides to stop and draw a plan and change
1       the grid can only be based on his or her current understanding of the site
2       and the stop may be too early or too late. This may only be picked up in
3       the post-excavation analysis of the records. However, the issue goes deeper,
44111
        56   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    for it makes assumptions about the way a site changes – that a site will go
2       through major, site-wide shifts (phases) rather than a multi-linear trajectory,
3       with different parts of the site changing at different rates or times. This
4       problem is actually the most critical issue, and upon it hinges the change
5       from the old combined section and plan method of digging to an effectively
6       totally plan-driven method. The Biddles’ digging ‘in phase’ still relied on
7       the notion of the layer as the most signiﬁcant stratigraphic entity and conse-
8       quently the importance of the section to reading stratigraphy. In the course
9       of post-excavation work at Winchester, Edward Harris, who worked for the
1011    Research Unit between 1967 and 1971, developed a method of relating
1       stratigraphic units diagrammatically which had a spin-off for the whole
2       process of recording a site allowing just for this factor of multi-linearity.
3
4
        The Harris matrix and single-context planning
5
6       Harris had formulated a method of dealing with post-excavation problems in
7       1973 which relied on the innovations developed at Winchester. Up to the
8       use of context sheets, the basic textual ﬁeld record was the notebook which
9       was usually ordered so that each page corresponded to a square in the grid
20111   or area within the site. The square was assigned a letter and on its corres-
1       ponding page the different layers were listed and numbered (though num-
2       bering always started from zero within each square; e.g. see Kenyon 1953:
3       129–31). This basic two-tier method of a letter (square/area) and number
4       (layer) goes back at least to Petrie and is chieﬂy linked in to a means of iden-
5       tifying the provenance of ﬁnds (Petrie 1904: 51–2). With the Biddles’ use of
6       co-ordinate planning, letter and number systems were dispensed with so that
7       every ﬁnd or feature could be located by speciﬁc x–y co-ordinates as in a
8       cartesian grid (Biddle and Kjølbye-Biddle 1969: 208), and more signiﬁcantly
9       in this context, one could also then have a single running sequence of
30111   numbers of every stratigraphic unit for the whole site.
1          The basis of the Harris matrix is that any stratigraphic unit can only
2       have three possible kinds of direct relationship to another: none, earlier/later
3       than, or equivalent to (Harris 1989: 36). In practice, contexts may have
4       indirect relationships with other contexts (i.e. through a common context
5       which is earlier or later) as well as the more direct relationships. The nature
6       of this indirect relationship cannot be determined stratigraphically but
7       must be discovered by other means, which involve grouping (or phasing)
8       the matrix into broad divisions which are approximately contemporary;
9       ideally this would be done through major horizon breaks in the sequence
40111   and/or independent dating through ﬁnds association (e.g. pottery, coins).
1       Essentially this is the question of permutations of the matrix which occur
2       in all multi-linear sequences (e.g. see Harris 1984; 1989: 129–32; also
3       Dalland 1984).
44111
                                                                    Finding the past   57

1111       The idea behind the matrix derives from critical path analysis, a tech-
2       nique in quantitative geography: ‘This method [i.e. the matrix] of presenting
3       a stratigraphic sequence might be seen as a form of critical path analysis’
4       (Harris 1975: 119). Critical path analysis was formalised in the 1960s as
5       part of the same statistical and quantitative ‘revolution’ which hit most of
6       the social sciences, including archaeology. Essentially it presents in a
7       diagrammatic form the shortest route in time on a one-way path, the critical
8       path being the longest path through a network of possible routes. Inter-
9       estingly, the work Harris quotes, Quantitative Geography, suggests that critical
1011    path analysis
1
2           might also provide a new approach to studying the course of history,
3111        since certain events could not happen before the completion of others.
4           The motor car of the present could not be made before the invention
5           of the internal combustion engine using petrol. This in turn had to
6           await the invention of the sparking plug – and so on.
7                                                     (Cole and King 1968: 572)
8
9       From the Biddles’ co-ordinate planning and Harris’s work on the matrix,
20111   the idea of recording stratigraphic units on separate pro forma sheets and
1       planning them individually, produced a whole new possibility in concep-
2       tualising ﬁeldwork. The ﬁrst instances of the use of context sheets for
3       describing stratigraphic units occurred in the 1970s; single-context planning
4       was ﬁrst suggested to Harris by Lawrence Keen (Harris 1975: 121; 1989:
5       95) and developed with Patrick Ottoway on New Road site, Winchester
6       in 1975 (Harris and Ottoway 1976; Harris 1989: 95). It was developed as
7       a means to avoid reliance on multiple-feature or, more commonly, composite
8       plans – that is, plans which record all or phased features respectively, on
9       the same drawing.
30111      The combination of the matrix and single-context recording effectively
1       meant that all baulk sections could be dispensed with and everything exca-
2       vated in plan. For the ﬁrst time, stratigraphy became truly independent of the
3       section. The basic conceptual difference between Harris’s formulation of
4       stratigraphy and that based on the section is that, for the former, physical and
5       stratigraphic relationships are not conﬂated but distinct. This difference will be
6       explained below, but it was quite a momentous occasion in the history of
7       ﬁeld archaeology in Britain. Statements such as the following, in a 1974 pub-
8       lication on archaeological reports, once uncontested, swiftly became untrue:
9       ‘The whole presentation of the excavator’s stratigraphic analysis and hence
40111   his interpretation of the site has depended upon his sections, so they stand
1       as the only record of that interpretation’ (Grinsell et al. 1974: 41). Harris’s
2       methods were quickly adopted, most notably in London by the Department
3       of Urban Archaeology (DUA) who ﬁrst used them at the GPO site in 1975
44111
        58   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    to overcome the time constraints of on-site interpretation and the corre-
2       sponding need for phase and composite plans (Spence 1991b). (Schoﬁeld
3       (1977: 101), however, stated that these methods were being used as early as
4       1974.) In the following discussion, I will deal with the DUA, or MoLAS
5       (as it is now known – Museum of London Archaeology Service) system, as
6       it undoubtedly represents the most reﬁned development of the Harris–
7       Winchester method, though most other units in the country, especially urban
8       units, have adopted a form/degree of single-context recording and the matrix
9       (e.g. for York, see Pearson and Williams 1991).
1011       The primary consideration in the excavation and recording of sites using
1       this method is the stratigraphic sequence. The method is particularly useful
2       on urban sites with deep, complex stratigraphy where some external control
3       is required which does not solely depend upon on-site interpretation. The
4       basis of the approach is to divide up the site into discrete units or contexts
5       which are excavated and recorded according to their stratigraphic position:
6       ‘Any single action, whether it leaves a positive or negative record within the
7       sequence, is known as a “context”’ (Westman 1994: §1.2; emphasis in orig-
8       inal). The deﬁnition of the unit is thus in terms of its physical, that is
9       spatial, limits which are deﬁned either negatively (e.g. a cut) or positively
20111   (e.g. a deposit) and interpreted as representative of a single action or event.
1       This spatial deﬁnition means that even if what appears to be a single feature
2       such as a dump layer is bisected by a cut, and if there is no physical contact
3       extant between the two halves, then they are treated as two separate units.
4       The approach has to be consistent; the identity of the two halves can
5       always be established subsequently in the record. Each unit then has a
6       stratigraphic position – that is a position relative to other units in terms
7       of the sequence – and this is represented in terms of the Harris matrix
8       (Harris 1989).
9          The single-context recording system and the stratigraphic matrix are both
30111   extremely useful methods in understanding a particularly complex archae-
1       ological site; by viewing it as a palimpsest of discrete events (either of
2       deposition or removal), the sequence of development on site can be under-
3       stood with maximum control (Figure 7). It views the site or even a part
4       of the site as a dynamic, creative process. In a way, it represents the apoth-
5       eosis of techniques developed in the late 1960s and 1970s and best illustrates
6       the conception of ﬁeldwork as the investigation and retrieval of an archae-
7       ological record. No longer a physical site, made of dirt and sharp stones,
8       no longer the location of buried treasure, but an abstract, immaterial struc-
9       tured set of lines, numbers and text.
40111
1
        Field archaeology in North America since 1960
2
3       North American archaeologists started to encounter large-scale excavations
44111   comparable to the rescue or Cultural Resource Management work as early as

8
9
30111   the 1930s and 1940s through the Works Progress Administration and Civilian
1       Conservation Corps programmes (Dunnell 1986b: 23). It was in such situa-
2       tions that many of the innovations which were introduced in Britain in the
3       1960s had already been implemented, innovations such as standard recording
4       forms and cartesian grids (ibid.: 28). Mechanical excavators were also start-
5       ing to be used at the same time, especially by the Tennessee Valley Authority
6       and other salvage excavations, and, unlike Britain, they seem to have been
7       instrumental in opening large areas and uncovering whole settlements
8       (Wedel 1951; Haag 1986: 70). If the USA had already established many of
9       the developments which took place a little later in Britain, what innovations
40111   in ﬁeldwork did occur at this time? A look through the various editions and
1       transformations of Heizer’s original guide from 1949 up to its present incar-
2       nation shows that the same basic procedures, developed in the 1930s and
3       1940s, continue to form the core of excavation methods, in particular the
44111   ‘unit-level’ method of excavation. Most other ﬁeld manuals which started to
        60   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    appear from the 1960s onward, also cite the unit-level method as the favoured
2       technique (e.g. Joukowsky 1980; McMillon 1991).
3          The only major shifts within this scheme were the standard preference
4       by the 1960s for excavating after the natural stratigraphy wherever possible
5       rather than using arbitrary spits, and the subsequent adoption of the Harris
6       matrix by the 1980s. The continued use of a horizontal grid as a larger
7       excavation unit, however, distinguishes the US from the British approach
8       with an accompanying greater concern for sections or proﬁles. In this regard,
9       the layer remains the primary element of a site; thus features such as pits
1011    are often still described as ‘disturbances’ of the stratigraphy rather than
1       stratigraphic units in their own right (Joukowsky 1980: 150–7). McMillon
2       even gives a detailed breakdown of such phenomena under the subtitle of
3       ‘excavation problems’, where he divides them into ‘distortions’, ‘interrup-
4       tions’ and ‘disturbances’ (1991: 77–84). The importance of the section for
5       understanding the site is highlighted by Joukowsky, for it ‘can only be
6       learned by the study of exposed sections and section drawings, which are
7       scale drawings of the stratiﬁcation of the site’ (Joukowsky 1980: 153).
8          Nevertheless, while it may appear that ﬁeld techniques did not develop
9       as dramatically in the 1960s as they had in the 1920s and 1930s in North
20111   America, this is only a partial view. While it may be fair to say that exca-
1       vation methods were not radically different, more general aspects of ﬁeldwork
2       were, speciﬁcally in the area of sampling and research design. Both on the
3       larger scale of the landscape and the micro-scale of the site, sampling strate-
4       gies were undoubtedly a major innovation in ﬁeldwork around the 1960s.
5       Use of grids for ﬁeld survey, geophysical and geochemical methods of site
6       prospecting, and the routine adoption of on-site screening (i.e. sieving) and
7       ﬂotation of deposits for environmental and other remains are all major
8       developments that took off after this time (Haag 1986: 70–3; Dunnell
9       1986b: 28). The same trends also occurred in Britain and are clearly linked
30111   to the new concerns and questions of archaeology after the 1960s – site
1       distribution and regional settlement systems on the one hand, and environ-
2       mental and ecological components on the other. Both these issues were of
3       course raised in the decades before the 1960s, but it is only after this time
4       that they became routine aspects of ﬁeldwork because of changes in the
5       perception of the archaeological record.
6          Fundamental to this change was the idea that the recovery of data was
7       inevitably dependent on what one was looking for. Binford, in an impor-
8       tant paper on research design, criticised most earlier ﬁeldwork for treating
9       sites as essentially ‘mines’ for the recovery of artefacts with little regard for
40111   features or the relationship between artefacts and features (Binford 1964).
1       While this was perhaps overstating the case, his main issue was that ﬁeld-
2       work was usually too constrained by what was within the often arbitrary
3       limits of trenching or survey, and that the relationship between what was
44111   in or on the ground and what was actually recovered remained unprob-
                                                                   Finding the past   61

1111    lematised. He argued for a need to consider proper sampling strategies and
2       a thought-out research design using two basic units of sampling – the region
3       and the site – the one being covered by surface survey and the other by
4       excavation. For the former, he drew on the by now common trio of random,
5       systematic or stratiﬁed sampling procedures, while for excavation he
6       suggested a staged investigation employing methods which were already in
7       use: an initial test pit survey to identify the presence of remains, a secondary
8       block area excavation to provide a representative spatial cover, and ﬁnally
9       an open area stripping to provide the detailed contextual data (ibid.). By
1011    implementing a research design, the relevant data about sites could be prop-
1       erly recovered, with, more importantly, some control over their recovery
2       (e.g. see papers in Mueller 1975).
3111
4
        Modern field methods and ‘action archaeology’
5
6       The rise of New Archaeology around the same time as these developments
7       in ﬁeld techniques is of course no coincidence. The use of a matrix and
8       single units is reminiscent of systems theory diagrams and we have already
9       commented on its pedigree in geography’s critical path analysis. More speciﬁ-
20111   cally though is the equation made between a stratigraphic unit and an
1       event or action, for just as objects were no longer viewed as direct, norma-
2       tive representations of cultures nor were the contexts of those objects.
3       Culture came to be seen as more complex, articulated through all kinds of
4       different behavioural events/actions rather than simply a normative repos-
5       itory of traits. This complexity of culture is equally manifest in the SCR
6       system of excavation which sees a site as a complex of actions/events rather
7       than a simple sequence of cultures or cultural change as in ‘phase’ exca-
8       vation. While this thematic link to excavation methods was perhaps stronger
9       in Britain, in the USA these developments were accompanied by a group
30111   of related studies – ethnoarchaeology, experimental archaeology,
1       taphonomy, formation processes and Middle Range Theory – all of which
2       in various ways tackle the question of the relationship between the archae-
3       ological record and behaviour (see Chapter 5).
4          Willey and Sabloff have suggested that the development of ‘feature’ exca-
5       vation in the 1930s ultimately stimulated the concern for context and
6       understanding behaviour patterns in the archaeological record (Willey and
7       Sabloff 1993: 144). It certainly may have been a necessary precondition,
8       but such behavioural concerns do not really appear until much later and
9       in Britain a case can be made for clearly linking them to SCR with its
40111   focus on the ‘event’ or ‘action’ nature of archaeological deposits. For Wheeler
1       or Kenyon excavation was largely a matter of construction sequence; while
2       we have inherited this notion, our focus on the stratigraphic unit as repre-
3       sentative of an event seems a clear departure, in the sense of viewing a
44111   site as a site of actions and events. Of course Wheeler saw this too, espe-
        62   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    cially through his imaginative reconstructions, but the difference is that for
2       Wheeler the events are pulled out of the stratigraphic sequence and woven
3       into a traditional historical narrative while under SCR they form the very
4       basis of the sequence. Our concern today is understanding a structured
5       activity or set of activities, not simply a series of unique events; the site is
6       composed of this.
7          Economic and ecological dimensions of the culture group as developed
8       by such people as O. G. S. Crawford and Grahame Clark in Britain and,
9       slightly later, Julian Steward and Leslie White in the USA, can be seen
1011    as anticipating this behavioural turn. However, their work focused on
1       improving the retrieval and analysis of environmental ﬁnds, rather than
2       explicitly studying behaviour patterns through context or stratigraphic units.
3       It is only when assemblages were studied in these terms that major changes
4       were made in viewing the archaeological record – for example the studies
5       on social organisation made on Pueblo sites in the American Southwest
6       by Hill, Longacre and others and those on mortuary practices by Binford
7       and others (Binford and Binford 1968). In Britain, similar studies ultimately
8       developed into a contextual archaeology under the inﬂuence of Ian Hodder
9       (e.g. Hodder 1982b). Just as the culture group concept inﬂuenced the way
20111   sites were excavated, so processual/post-processual archaeology through its
1       concern for behaviour/action has affected the way we perceive sites today.
2          Looking back over the developments in ﬁeld archaeology since the 1880s,
3       several major changes in perception can be identiﬁed. For Pitt Rivers and
4       others of his time, the archaeological site was a repository of objects which,
5       if carefully excavated and linked to types of monuments, were instrumental
6       in the construction of evolutionary typological sequences. For Wheeler,
7       Kidder and their contemporaries, the site became a repository of an arte-
8       factual assemblage indicative of a culture group, and, if stratiﬁed, the locus
9       of critical information on the chronological changes within this assemblage.
30111   For us, the site is a repository of behavioural patterns, structured activities
1       revealed through close analysis of contextual association within or between
2       assemblages.
3          One can see how each of these views is linked to the prevailing concep-
4       tion of the past – as the evolution of culture, as the history of culture
5       groups, and as cultural behaviour (however this is viewed). These changes
6       should not, however, be seen as completely disjunctive, for many of the
7       themes from preceding periods were carried over. We still use typologies
8       and systems of classiﬁcation developed in the late nineteenth century and
9       we still use regional culture differences inherited from the earlier twentieth
40111   century. The history of ﬁeldwork is one of continuities and change and it
1       is important to see the two together. Moreover, while there is no doubt
2       that ﬁeldwork methodologies also tend to channel the process of recovery
3       in relation to theoretical perspectives, this is not necessarily a closed rela-
44111   tionship as the productive re-examination of earlier excavation archives
                                                              Finding the past   63

1111    shows (e.g. Barrett, Bradley and Green 1991; Barrett et al. 1983; Wilcox
2       1981). This issue of the openness of archives is something I return to at
3       the end of this book in terms of the concept of ‘iterability’ and the fact
4       that excavation and ﬁeldwork in general are not necessarily the unrepeat-
5       able experiments we often regard them as.

        Splitting objects

1011
1
2
3       It is very easy to forget why a site report is structured like it is – we read
4       it without really questioning the partition and order of information, indeed
5       we even expect it to look a certain way. A ceramicist may turn to the back
6       and ﬂick through, looking for ﬁgures and descriptions of pottery, knowing
7       that such reports usually occur near the end and as distinct sections of text
8       within the report. The faunal remains person, the lithics specialist, and so
9       on, may do the same. Someone interested in the site phasing may look for
20111   phase plans, or for a quick summary of the site interpretation, and will go
1       to the ﬁnal discussion. It is very predictable, very repetitive and – boring?
2       Well, perhaps, but this is not really my point. The site report as it usually
3       appears today presents a whole classiﬁcation of archaeological knowledge,
4       one which is not necessarily ‘natural’, ‘scientiﬁc’ or the best, but one which
5       has developed out of speciﬁc historical and theoretical circumstances. The
6       very partitioning and order of information presented in a site report is as
7       implicated in the history and conceptualisation of the discipline as the
8       ﬁeldwork which precedes it.
9          In this chapter, I want to explore this classiﬁcation of archaeological
30111   knowledge. I want to focus in particular on what appears to be a dual sense
1       of such classiﬁcations: on the one hand, the separation of the site’s features
2       (its pits, postholes, ditches, walls) from its ﬁnds (its potsherds, bones, seeds);
3       and on the other, the separation of different kinds of ﬁnds from each other.
4       I want to examine how archaeological objects came to be split off from
5       each other, and from their context of deposition and how these splits were
6       accompanied by a similar classiﬁcation of archaeologists – ceramicists, faunal
7       analysts, palaeobotanists, etc. Such a classiﬁcation may seem natural to us,
8       or expedient – no one can be an expert on everything they excavate; but
9       the issue here is not merely with a classiﬁcation per se, but with the partic-
40111   ular structure in use now. Why the split between features and ﬁnds, between
1       pots and ﬂint, or artefacts and ecofacts? Why do these particular axes of
2       partition operate as opposed to any other? Why and how did they develop
3       and, perhaps most importantly of all, what effect do they have on our inter-
44111   pretations of the past?
                                                                  Splitting objects   65

1111    The classification of archaeological knowledge
2
3       The development of specialisms in archaeology
4       How did object specialisms emerge? To understand this, it is important to
5       realise that such specialisms were preﬁgured in the way that objects were split
6       up and discussed in a site report, even though the excavator was usually also
7       the one to write about all the ﬁnds. For example, Pitt Rivers generally
8       discussed most of the material himself. A survey of selected reports published
9       in Britain between 1913 and 1996 shows how over the course of the century,
1011    this changed as more and more specialisms developed (Figure 8). The excep-
1       tions to this pattern are the environmental or non-artefactual remains, prin-
2
        cipally unmodiﬁed bone (both human and animal) and preserved seeds or
3111
        insects. Early studies of faunal remains from archaeological sites were con-
4
        ducted by zoologists or palaeontologists (Reitz and Wing 1999: 16). Some
5
        of the earliest work was done on the Swiss lake dwellings in the 1860s by
6
        L. Rütimeyer who differentiated wild from domesticated species and also
7
        reported on butchery marks. However, on the whole, reports on faunal
8
        remains throughout the late nineteenth and early twentieth century were
9
20111   brief and usually only taxonomic, that is, identifying the range of species pre-
1       sent. Occasionally more extended discussions can be found, but these usually
2       dealt with comparative anatomy and produced tables of bone measurements,
3       etc. The attention to such topics is not surprising as they formed the major
4       concerns of the day, and in fact the same basic topics can be seen in reports
5       on human remains, with particular emphasis given to cranial measurements
6       and the associated issues of race. Such a style of faunal reports continued
7       until the 1950s, when a major shift occurred in the interest in animal bones
8       from archaeological sites when they start to be viewed in terms of infor-
9       mation on the economy. A classic example is Grahame Clark’s Star Carr
30111   published in 1954 in which both faunal remains and wider environmental
1       data form a large part of the text (Clark 1954), while in the USA the work
2       of Gilmore was pioneering and his association with Walter Taylor cannot be
3       coincidental (Reitz and Wing 1999: 21).
4          The shift is clearly related to wider changes in the conception of the
5       archaeological record and past societies which are discussed throughout this
6       book. It is from this period that archaeologists trained in faunal remains start
7       to emerge as opposed to zoologists working on archaeological material. By
8       the 1960s, these new specialists were developing their own sets of issues and
9       problems particular to archaeology, the two major themes being domesti-
40111   cation and taphonomy. Among these specialists the German Joachim
1       Boessneck was perhaps the pioneering ﬁgure in Europe (Davis 1987). These
2       themes, especially the latter, remain central to zooarchaeology, and most fau-
3       nal reports today will not only contain basic quantiﬁed species information,
44111   but also taphonomic data such as evidence for butchery practices and bone
        66     Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111

        Maiden Castle (1943)

8
9
20111   size/fragmentation. Related to the latter was the concern to recover smaller
1       bones – either from small animals (such as ﬁsh) or through greater fragmen-
2       tation – and sampling deposits has become a major component in the control
3       for ﬁne bone retrieval (Payne 1975).
4          The study of plant remains from archaeological sites followed much the
5       same pattern; early work was conducted by botanists, especially on sites
6       with waterlogged material such as the Swiss lakes (Renfrew 1973: 1–6). As
7       with the faunal reports, these early studies were conﬁned to noting species
8       present and discussing issues pertinent to taxonomic interpretation. The
9       development of palynology and its introduction into archaeology occurred
30111   around the same time as the interest in wider economic and environmental
1       issues in the 1950s. By the 1960s, specialist palaeoethnobotanists started
2       to develop their own agenda, focusing on issues such as domestication and
3       retrieval methods. One of the problems with much of the earlier work on
4       plant remains was its reliance on chance recovery, for example the cache
5       of carbonised seeds in a pot. There was a growing realisation that most
6       sites could yield preserved plant material but it was not being recovered
7       because of the excavation methods. During the late 1960s and early 1970s
8       there emerged a range of sampling techniques which used a liquid medium
9       to separate out material and a mesh to collect it (Streuver 1968a; Jarman
40111   et al. 1971; Williams 1973; Minnis and LeBlanc 1976). Commonly the
1       process has come to be known as ﬂotation, although in general it utilises
2       a double process of sampling by density (ﬂotation) and size (sieve). Most
3       sampling today employs these techniques developed in the 1960s and 1970s
44111   with minor modiﬁcations (Jones 1991).
                                                                  Splitting objects   67

1111       Other specialists only emerged much later; as Figure 8 shows, over the
2       course of the twentieth century, the proportion of the ﬁnds which the exca-
3       vator reported on diminished as others (frequently women) began to appear
4       as specialists in various ﬁelds. For example, with ceramics, much of the
5       early work was conducted by archaeologists also associated with excavation;
6       thus the typological approach which dominated ceramic studies until the
7       mid-twentieth century is linked to people such as Pitt Rivers and Petrie
8       from Britain or Kidder and James Ford in the USA; their work on pottery
9       was integral to their excavations. It was not until the mid-twentieth century
1011    that ceramic specialists emerged,1 the most inﬂuential perhaps in the
1       English-speaking world being Anna Shepard. She brought together many
2       of the previous themes associated with pottery – chieﬂy chronology and
3111    distribution – with a greater emphasis on technological and analytical study
4       of the ceramics themselves (Shepard 1956).
5          The technological aspects of pottery and the emergence of specialists
6       studying ceramics helped to re-focus attention on issues about the nature
7       of ceramics – for example that they were not proxies for people, but objects
8       which were made and used by people. A number of important themes thus
9       arose after the 1950s, including the life-cycles of pots and the technology
20111   of their production. Informed by ethnographic work such as David’s study
1       of Fulani pottery in Africa and that of of De Boer and Lathrap in South
2       America, issues of the ceramic life-cycle became a major focus of interest,
3       expressed through the term ‘ceramic ecology’ (David 1972; De Boer and
4       Lathrap 1979; see Arnold 1985 for a major statement on this position). In
5       tandem with these studies, work on pottery fabrics also developed, to eluci-
6       date themes such as production and use, provenance and dating through a
7       variety of techniques from thin-sectioning to thermo-luminesence (see
8       Orton et al. 1993 for a summary).
9          A similar development can be traced for studies of worked stone or lithics.
30111   The very earliest studies of stone tools were linked into establishing the an-
1       tiquity of humans and they became classic type fossils for nineteenth-century
2       archaeologists such as W. H. Holmes in the USA or John Evans in Britain.
3       This chronological facet remained in the early part of the twentieth century
4       a key component of lithic studies and, again, such studies usually conducted
5       by the excavators themselves and/or nameless assistants. The rise of lithic spe-
6       cialists began to develop in the 1950s and 1960s, in particular through exper-
7       imental replication studies by archaeologists such as Don Crabtree and
8       François Bordes. These studies, while in themselves not new, were the
9       medium through which a specialism emerged (Andrefsky 1998: 3). Another
40111   element associated with the emergence of this specialism was use–wear analy-
1       sis, pioneered in the former Soviet Union by Semenov, whose work became
2       more widely available in the 1960s when it was translated (Semenov 1964).
3          Use–wear analysis helped to shift thinking away from a static view of
44111   stone tool morphology, and lithic form was increasingly seen as a product
        68   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    of its life-cycle as much as of its original design, and thus issues of stone
2       tool function became as important as any chronological index. Thus, as
3       with ceramics, the development towards lithic specialism can be closely
4       linked to a greater concern for processes of production, use and discard
5       against purely typological analysis, a change which undoubtedly relates to
6       broader shifts in archaeological thought in the 1950s and 1960s.
7          While this brief outline of the emergence of specialists has shown the
8       broad links with larger conceptual issues in archaeology, I want to narrow
9       the focus a little and trace in particular how objects became separated from
1011    the site in the ﬁrst place, whether to be studied by a specialist or not. Some
1       of the earliest archaeological reports – often in the form of narratives or
2       letters – discuss the ﬁnds in the context of their discovery; this is typically
3       the case with the Barrow excavations of the mid- and later nineteenth
4       century, where grave goods are listed and described as the graves are, and
5       ﬁgures of such objects intersperse the narrative (e.g. Greenwell 1877; also
6       see Hodder 1989b). Yet by the end of the century the separation of ﬁnds
7       from contextual or site description was in place and soon became the stan-
8       dard presentation of archaeological data and the classic form of the
9       archaeological report as we know it today. How did this come about? Pitt
20111   Rivers was one of the ﬁrst to deploy this method of presentation indeed
1       the typical sequence of discussion in his volumes on Cranbourne Chase
2       began with the site as a whole, followed by feature descriptions and ended
3       with the ﬁnds. Is there a model for this sequence – which has essentially
4       remained unchanged?
5          Hinsley, in a study of nineteenth-century graphic presentations of the
6       archaeological process, showed how there was a typical three-stage narrative
7       to such presentations, exempliﬁed by Layard’s discoveries in the Near East
8       (Hinsley 1989: 89–90). The story begins with an image of the site as a
9       place of lost grandeur (the moment of discovery), which is followed by an
30111   image of the site under investigation with the removal of structures or
1       objects from it (the process of recovery through technology), and ﬁnally
2       ends with a depiction of these structures or objects in their resting place,
3       usually the museum (the appreciation of knowledge/beauty). Hinsley argued
4       that such a presentation was guided by a desire to narrate Western ideals,
5       and it is not difﬁcult to see how this worked. However, what interests me
6       in this study is the particular sequence, speciﬁcally its ending: the object
7       as a source of ediﬁcation. Can one say that perhaps the same narrative
8       structure guided early archaeological reports, with the ﬁnds occupying pride
9       of place?
40111      Certainly, if one looks at the amount of space given to the presentation
1       of ﬁnds against the site and its features, the early reports seem very biased
2       towards the ﬁnds, although by the 1930s the balance has shifted so that
3       treatment of ﬁnds against the site are more or less equivalent. The changes
44111   to this presentation can to some extent be explained by the changes in
                                                                    Splitting objects   69

1111    ﬁeldwork practice described in the last chapter. My concern here is really
2       to focus on the history of how ﬁnds are split from their context and each
3       other; if Hinsley’s nineteenth century narrative of the archaeological process
4       helps us to understand the former, how do we understand the latter? If the
5       end point of this narrative was the object on display – either in the museum
6       or on the printed page – how were objects arranged and divided?
7          The origin and development of museums has a vast literature but the par-
8       ticular aspect which concerns me here is the relation between the ordering
9       of objects and their conceptual classiﬁcation. Ole Worm’s seventeenth-
1011    century Cabinet of Curiosities employed a classiﬁcation derived from earlier
1       works but his was the ﬁrst general treatise to cover speciﬁcally archaeological
2       and ethnographic objects (Schnapp 1993: 174). Signiﬁcantly, his division of
3111    artefacts is based on material criteria: clay, amber, stone, gold, silver, bronze,
4       iron, glass, plant, wood and animal products all form the basis of his divi-
5       sions, the only exceptions being coins and the unclassiﬁable. The material
6       basis to classiﬁcation was to become the important element in the develop-
7       ment of the Three Age System, but it is signiﬁcant that this has formed such
8       a long-term axis of classiﬁcation in Western thinking. However, Worm’s
9       classiﬁcation is interesting for another reason, in that he did not use the pre-
20111   viously common distinction between artiﬁciosa and naturalia, but rather saw
1       the two as inseparable (ibid.). These two innovations can be seen as related;
2       indeed the classiﬁcation of artefacts by natural categories is an explicit
3       demonstration of the continuity between artiﬁciosa and naturalia.
4          This material basis for the classiﬁcation of objects, best exempliﬁed in
5       the Three Age System, was more generally widespread in the treatment of
6       ﬁnds during the late nineteenth century. Pitt Rivers’ Cranborne Chase
7       volumes categorise the ﬁnds in ways no different from today – the South
8       Lodge ﬁnds, for example, are divided into bronze, iron, bone, ﬂint and pot
9       (Pitt Rivers 1898). Similarly, at Glastonbury, Bulleid arranged artefacts
30111   according to clear material categories, under headings such as objects of
1       metal, shale, wood, bone, antler, ﬂint, baked clay (Bulleid and Gray
2       1911/1917). There are exceptions, such as pottery (distinct from baked
3       clay), weaving combs or spindle whorls which, though of antler or other
4       materials, are not categorised under those general headings but separately.
5       The reason partly relates to their recognition as familiar objects, as the
6       name given to them testiﬁes – though this cannot be the whole reason.
7       Nevertheless, the same basic categorisations of ﬁnds seen in Pitt Rivers’ or
8       Bulleid’s reports have more or less remained constantly in use throughout
9       the twentieth century, as a glance through any site report will reveal. There
40111   is the same splitting of objects, either using special categories for familiar
1       ﬁnds (pottery, brooches, coins), or generic categories based on material for
2       the less familiar (objects of bronze, iron, bone, stone, antler, baked clay,
3       etc.). These divisions largely existed before the specialists who later emerged
44111   to study them.
        70   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111       The same approach is seen in the USA – an early collection by the
2       American Bureau of Ethnology of material from New Mexico and Arizona
3       is arranged by explicitly material classiﬁcations, the objects listed as articles
4       of stone, bone, clay, vegetable substance, animal substance, horn and bone
5       and wood (Stevenson 1883). However, in Fewkes’ report of ﬁeldwork in
6       Pueblo ruins, the ﬁnds appear more idiosyncratic in their grouping, although
7       in many ways the principal division is between recognisable types (e.g. pots,
8       baskets, prayer-sticks) and more generic categories (e.g. bone implements,
9       horn implements; Fewkes 1904). Similarly, in the later reports such as
1011    Webb’s and de Jarnette’s Pickwick Basin excavations, Cole’s study of Kincaid
1       or the more recent American Bottom FAI-270 investigations, to choose a
2       few at random, categorisations of objects are most often based on their
3       material (Webb and de Jarnette 1942; Cole 1951; Fortier et al. 1984).
4          Is there any signiﬁcance to this twofold division of speciﬁc types based
5       largely on perceived function or use, and generic categories based on raw
6       material? Interestingly, in Webb’s and de Jarnette’s publications of ﬁnds
7       from the Pickwick Basin, they have a section at the end on special arte-
8       facts, where they state that their distinction comes from the fact that they
9       are unusual but more signiﬁcantly characteristic of the culture, in this case
20111   the Moundville complex (Webb and de Jarnette 1942: 287). Could there
1       be a link between the cultural speciﬁcity of ﬁnds and their categorisation
2       in speciﬁc rather than generic terms? Perhaps. In fact, to give it another
3       angle, if we divide reports on the basis of the antiquity of the material recov-
4       ered and examine the ratio of the two categorisations, an interesting pattern
5       seems to emerge. The older the objects, the more likely it seems they will
6       be categorised by their generic material. This is suggested in Table 2, in
7       which the ﬁgures are taken from a selection of major site reports from
8       Britain where, proportionately, ﬁnds are more likely to be categorised gener-
9       ically on prehistoric sites than on historic (in this case Roman) ones. Now
30111   one might explain this by reference to familiarity with the object – the
1       more recent the ﬁnd, the more likelihood that it will be familiar to us
2       anyway; but I think there is more to this than meets the eye.
3
4
5       Table 2 Ratio of generic to specific finds categorisations by period in major British
6       site reports
7
        Period          Site                          Generic categories   Speciﬁc categories
8
9       Prehistoric     Durrington Walls (1971)        5                     1
40111                   Orkney (1979)                  3                     1

2       Roman           Camulodunum (1947)             8                   10

44111
                                                                   Splitting objects   71

1111       Consider Pitt Rivers’ image of the development of ethnographic weapons
2       from Australasia (Figure 9). The illustration is a visual representation of
3       his idea of evolution, from the simple to the complex, from a single orig-
4       inal form to a diversity of forms. The effect of the arrangement encourages
5       us to perceive the over-riding signiﬁcance of the centre – that this stick is
6       the origin, the prototype or universal form of all the other types.
7       Signiﬁcantly, Pitt Rivers also conceived of a museum on this plan – the
8       ‘rotunda’ was to be so organised that the visitor began at the centre moving
9       outwards into increasing differentiation, thus retracing the steps of evolu-
1011    tion (Chapman 1985). This idea comes out equally forcefully when he is
1       referring to the notion of degeneration; his examples, including Evans’s
2       study of British coins and his own of Papua New Guinean tribal masks,
3111    expresses the idea of an original artefact of which subsequent types are
4       simply degenerate copies as the original is lost and undergoes successive
5       reduction of accuracy in replication (Pitt Rivers 1906).
6          Linked very closely to this idea is that of the natural; that the lower the
7       civilisation, the greater the similarity of form to a natural form. Thus, in
8       the Australasian example, the various implements ‘show evidence of being
9       derived from natural forms such as might have been employed by man
20111   before he had learnt the art of modifying them to his uses ‘ (Pitt Rivers
1       1906: 11). Thus there is a certain afﬁnity established between the natural
2       and cultural form through an originary conception of material culture. The
3       same association between artefacts and the concept of the original and the
4       connotations of the natural which go along with it, articulated Worm’s
5       conﬂation of artiﬁciosa and naturalia and continue to inﬂuence our concep-
6       tion of the archaeological object today. That very contemporary phrase,
7       material culture, encapsulates this relationship; the archaeological object as
8       both cultural and natural.
9          For Pitt Rivers, interpretation of an artefact in evolutionary (i.e. cultural)
30111   terms is guaranteed through its origin as a natural object; cultural evolu-
1       tion or reason stands in contrast to or outside nature yet at the same time
2       nature lies at the origin of culture. A similar ambiguity is still perceptible
3       in the constitution of archaeological objects; the word we usually give to
4       an archaeological object is artefact, which means something artiﬁcially made,
5       that is, non-natural – a cultural product. A pot, an axe, even a pit or a
6       ditch is an artefact. Yet one might observe that even such objects as pots
7       are not totally cultural in so far as they are composed of natural resources
8       or materials – for example, a pot is made out of clay. This was Worm’s
9       point and underlay Pitt Rivers’ evolutionary typology. And while we may
40111   no longer trace the form of the cultural object to a natural object (e.g. a
1       club to a stick), its substance – its materiality – is fully grounded in nature.
2          Related to the question of this duality of the archaeological object, there
3       is also that of the duality of archaeological objects; for archaeologists also
44111   study natural objects or ecofacts too – pollen, seeds, shells, soils – and this
1111
2
3
4
5
6       Figure 9
7
