4                 (1620–1860)
5
6
7          The normal way to view this phenomenon is to say that the absence of
8       some attributes signals a different type – for example, a digital clock is a
9       different type of clock to an analogue one, one of the critical attributes
40111   being the presence/absence of hands. But this emphasises difference at the
1       expense of similarity; this is not to say I want to reverse this equation, but
2       rather sustain the two together. I could for example have increased the
3       sample of objects to include barometers, chronometers and watches, or
44111   restricted it to longcase clocks; in either case the character of the chart
        102   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    would in all respects be no different. There would still be no essential
2       deﬁning feature of identity but rather a stratiﬁed identity which involves
3       degrees of generality/particularity. One must not read the essential from
4       the general. This is exactly the same point we made about the biographies
5       of objects, that just as objects have no stable or originary basis through
6       materiality, neither do typologies through the categorisations we use. In
7       this way although we can draw boundaries around our analysis, we cannot
8       absolutely deﬁne them – the chart can be seen as simply a selective perspec-
9       tive dependent on the context of analysis. Moreover, a mobility of resolution
1011    is directly related to the stratiﬁed nature of the chart, and in archaeolog-
1       ical terms this is highly signiﬁcant for it suggests that we might adjust the
2       degree of dimensionality (i.e. inclusiveness of objects) to the degree of
3       chronological resolution at our disposal.
4          The point of this method is precisely to show the discontinuities within
5       a single analysis without resorting to a classiﬁcation. Classiﬁcation tends
6       to divide the analysis of an object through two contrastive and comple-
7       mentary terms, the variable (or attribute) and the value (or state) which
8       the variable possesses – for example, red being a value of the variable colour.
9       An essentialist deﬁnition of a type has to be given – even if any partic-
20111   ular deﬁnition can change, the condition of possessing an essential deﬁni-
1       tion cannot. Under classiﬁcation, attributes/variables/dimensions are the
2       immutable element of an object while the value is the mutable aspect. A
3       distinction between essential and accidental properties is presumed and as
4       a result ﬁxes the limits of an object. For example, all pottery has a fabric
5       (ﬁxed) which varies according to a number of factors such as temper, hard-
6       ness etc. (variable). What the stratiﬁcation of the clock chart demonstrates
7       on the other hand is that variables and values are not ﬁxed but relative –
8       any attribute or trait can be both a variable and a value depending on its
9       degree of generality or particularity relative to other attributes. Even the
30111   most general attribute could become a value if the horizons were altered,
1       horizons which will also affect what constitutes the object in question.
2       Thus, just as the chart can be used to show the ﬂuidity not only between
3       different types of clocks but also between clocks and other dialled instru-
4       ments, so pottery merges with bricks, tiles, ﬁgurines, etc., in sharing fabric,
5       but equally with other containers such as metal or wooden vessels which
6       share not fabric but form.
7          Questioning the parameters in such an analysis, thus challenges some
8       common ascriptions; in other words, in what sense do we ascribe a common
9       identity to the category of clock or, more generally, pottery, for example?
40111   In the case of pottery, how can we say that Neolithic pottery in Britain
1       bears any signiﬁcant relationship to Romano-British pottery? Perhaps we
2       cannot and do not, although we might argue that the two share certain
3       analogies by virtue of their material properties. But then we are back with
44111   an originary basis to classiﬁcation and specialisms. Consider the material
                                                                 Splitting objects   103

1111    artefacts we call cutlery – spoons, knives and forks have all existed as
2       distinct objects of Western material culture for a long time, especially knives
3       and spoons. But is it reasonable to draw any association between Roman
4       spoons and knives, or between these and our present cutlery set? The latter
5       developed only in the eighteenth century with changes in dining patterns,
6       and this shift is marked through the design of the object in terms of matching
7       motifs such as the rat-tail pattern etc.
8          This example shows that what we commonly describe as the same object
9       can actually have very different existences, despite the analogies. A similar
1011    but reversed situation occurs with objects of similar form in different
1       materials, for example ceramic, wooden and metal bowls. Is there a justi-
2       ﬁcation for a specialist in ceramics, woodwork and metalwork, or, to put
3111    it another way, is it reasonable that the categories of clay, wood and metal
4       are used to deﬁne such specialisms? Certainly the different histories of these
5       materials before human modiﬁcation are signiﬁcant. But what do we do
6       when faced with bone and wooden awls which look the same, or vessels
7       of the same shape but in ceramic, metal and wood? Is it still legitimate to
8       separate woodwork from bone, ceramics from metalwork? Such things are
9       not necessarily very common, and this has to do with the different prop-
20111   erties of their raw materials – but at the same time, we may often be missing
1       similarities because of this preconception.
2          More commonly, take the case of funerary pottery – it might equally be
3       wrong to study it alongside domestic pottery simply because it shares certain
4       formal similarities. This is not to argue against such comparative study, but
5       rather to highlight the relevance of prior categorisations to their study. The
6       study of funerary pottery alongside other funerary objects might be a more
7       appropriate course but, equally, it might be appropriate to study such vessels
8       alongside domestic pottery – but not because of their formal or material
9       similarity, but because the social context of their production might need
30111   to be seen against the production of domestic vessels – are they made by
1       the same potters?
2          It is through the concept of production – or rather, perhaps, reproduc-
3       tion, that the relation between classiﬁcation and the biography or life cycle
4       of an object is best articulated. Consider the problem of the European
5       Beaker, a class of late Neolithic/early Bronze Age pottery. Throughout the
6       twentieth century, there have been attempts to classify this form in Britain,
7       the most detailed being David Clarke’s which used complex statistical
8       analysis (Clarke 1970). However, neither Clarke’s attempt nor those of the
9       others have been entirely successful, and in terms of the chronological
40111   implications of their typologies none of them worked when recently tested
1       in a radiocarbon dating programme (Kinnes et al. 1991). In contrast, since
2       the 1980s, a number of contextual approaches to vessel variability have
3       revealed far more interesting patterns (Hodder 1982d; Shanks and Tilley
44111   1987b; Boast 1995). Why is it that the British Beaker (and indeed other
        104   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    prehistoric pottery) has proven intractable to typological analysis, unlike
2       pottery of later periods? The answer lies in the concept of standardisation:
3       Beakers are simply too variable. Typologies depend on the right amount of
4       variability – too little, and one hardly has a basis for any classiﬁcation at
5       all as Taylor remarked many years ago (see p. 79), but, on the other
6       hand, with too much one cannot know where one type might begin and
7       another end. The degree of variability or standardisation is a key issue
8       and it is this which links typologies into the social contexts of production
9       and reproduction.
1011       To take an opposite case, Bronze Age metalwork in Scandinavia is highly
1       standardised. It is this very aspect which made it so amenable to Montelius’
2       typology which still more or less stands today. It worked, not necessarily
3       because it was the right method but because it was the right material.
4       Sørensen has taken this point further and suggested that this very fact is
5       important in terms of understanding contexts in the reproduction of material
6       culture (Sørensen 1989, 1997). Indeed, perhaps our very concern for typolo-
7       gies derives from the fact that we live in a society where such contexts foster
8       standardisation and relative trans-contextual stability – mass-production or
9       specialised guilds which sustain closed traditions of manufacture. The fact is,
20111   a great deal of archaeological material is going to be unclassiﬁable except in
1       a broad sense of using generic identities which must change according to
2       context. Beaker typologies have never worked and they never will, because
3       the conditions under which Beakers were made are too context-speciﬁc; in
4       contrast, much of Bronze Age metalwork is trans-contextual and, in these
5       cases, a typology of the traditional sort is possible.
6          By heeding the social context of production, archaeology may be in a
7       position to reconsider categorisations which have been in place for too long
8       and the bias such systems carry towards the more numerous and typologi-
9       cally variable objects. I am not arguing for the abandonment of specialisms,
30111   nor that all the specialist divisions need to be re-structured. There are very
1       good reasons why a lithics analyst or ceramicist are useful specialists and,
2       concomitantly, why lithics and pottery are useful categories of analysis. A
3       pot specialist does not just look at how the pot was formed, or consider
4       the properties of the clay, but also examines evidence of use, residues, even
5       how a pot was broken. Similarly, a zooarchaeologist will not just identify
6       the species and sex of an animal but will look at pathologies, age of death,
7       butchery marks and other clues of post-mortem use. Objects do not just offer
8       information about the context of deposition but carry the scars of other
9       contexts, so many of which we, as archaeologists, rarely get a chance to
40111   see. Similarly, the strategies of reproduction, either of animals or pots, are
1       mediated by processes of continuity and change which often favour high
2       degrees of standardisation and at least exhibit continuity of form at some
3       level, permitting recognition of them as types. The common thread is the
44111   need to be aware of the temporality of objects.
                                                                 Splitting objects   105

1111       The issue comes to down to recognising this duality: that while all objects
2       are context-speciﬁc in that they take their meaning or signiﬁcance from
3       their context, at the same time most objects are capable of transcending
4       contexts either as individuals (biographies) or as types (design traditions).
5       Because so many of the studies on objects since the 1960s focused on the
6       issue of use, the context of objects became a primary articulation of vari-
7       ability. And yet archaeologists never encounter objects in a dynamic
8       context, that is they never witness their life cycles or biographies, but
9       usually only see one context, that of deposition. Of course production sites
1011    are excavated, and various ‘pompeii’ situations are occasionally encoun-
1       tered, but the majority of contexts in which objects are recovered refer to
2       the ﬁnal point in the life cycle. This fact may have contributed to the
3111    dominant blindness of archaeologists in regarding the ability of objects to
4       move through contexts, despite the growth of taphonomic studies. Indeed,
5       such taphonomic studies are invariably not about the life cycle but about
6       the ‘death cycle’, the post-depositional processes affecting objects.
7          Maybe now we can re-appraise Steward’s and Seltzer’s (1938) critique of
8       the way objects are split; while they were right to argue that material culture
9       ought to be seen in its cultural context, this cultural context cannot be
20111   taken to be homogeneous. One could say this was a product of the current
1       normative view of culture, even despite their call for a more functional
2       interpretation, but this is beside the point. Objects clearly move and work
3       through multiple cultural contexts and while the call for multiple classiﬁ-
4       cations might have been a response to this recognition, it really misses the
5       point. In moving through contexts, objects bring with them traces of the
6       effects earlier contexts have had on them, the most durable and impressive
7       of which is their context of production. The signiﬁcance of typology today
8       must be seen in terms of this context – that typological variability is but
9       a shorthand way of referring to the continuity and change in design tradi-
30111   tions in the context of production. Of course this needs to be linked into
1       contexts of consumption through the notion of reproduction, but, more
2       importantly, the very issue of variability and differentiation between objects
3       has to be temporalised.
4          It is precisely this temporality, therefore, which must be foregrounded so
5       that what specialists study is related to what other specialists are doing, as
6       well as being integrated with the site in general. Such integration may or
7       may not have been a component of projects in the past, but it is clear that,
8       in being studied and written up, the ﬁnds tend to become far removed from
9       their association with each other and their context, and the more this
40111   process goes in one direction the harder it seems to pull it all back together.
1       It is rare to ﬁnd a ﬁnal synthesis in a site report which does justice to the
2       various specialist studies preceding it. More integration at the point of
3       recovery is clearly a necessary direction to move in, and one which projects
44111   such as that at Çatalhöyük are moving towards (Hodder 1997). It may be
        106   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    salutary to remind ourselves that just as there are no universal classiﬁcations,
2       there are no universal specialisms – most ceramicists are also specialists in
3       the ceramics of a particular region and period, and if we can accept this
4       level of contextualisation, there is no reason not to tolerate even tighter
5       levels.

1011
1
2
3111    Gordon Childe was famous for his breadth of knowledge of prehistoric
4       Europe – indeed, he was probably one of the last archaeologists who could
5       claim to know in some detail the material culture of prehistoric Europe
6       based on ﬁrst-hand knowledge through visits to sites, museums and libraries
7       around the continent. As McNairn observes in her study of Childe’s theor-
8       etical perspectives:
9
20111       At a time when the trend was towards detailed, particularistic, research,
1           Childe’s texts such as The Dawn of European Civilisation (1925), The
2           Danube in Prehistory (1929), or Prehistoric Communities of the British Isles
3           (1940) stand out as great works of synthesis patterning the whole of
4           European prehistory.
5                                                               (McNairn 1980: 1)
6
7       Such syntheses as exist today, are often multi-authored and based on
8       secondary or tertiary published material (e.g. Champion et al. 1984). How
9       could it be otherwise? Just as in the early days of archaeology when ﬁnds
30111   specialists emerged, so did period or area specialists, both groups increasing
1       after the 1930s alongside the increasing amount of material recovered and
2       the number of archaeologists in universities.
3          At the end of the nineteenth century and into the ﬁrst decades of the
4       twentieth century, archaeologists could quite seriously consider themselves
5       students of human or world prehistory. One of the ﬁrst important texts was
6       Lubbock’s Prehistoric Times (1865) which happily crossed millennia and
7       continents. Of course there were studies which focused on speciﬁc areas –
8       Daniel Wilson’s ﬁrst major work, and a landmark in British archaeology,
9       was concerned with the prehistory of Scotland (Wilson 1851), but even
40111   he turned to more general works such as Prehistoric Man (1862). The point
1       is, that even though archaeologists might focus on a speciﬁc area, the
2       general concern was with ‘man’, and speciﬁcally the evidence which archae-
3       ology revealed of cultural or social evolution. By the 1930s, such grand
44111   schemes had been replaced in Europe and America by a more historically
        108   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    and regionally speciﬁc understanding of local culture groups as evolutionism
2       lost favour.
3          This is not to say that more cross-cultural concerns about human society
4       and prehistory were – and are – no longer addressed by archaeologists. The
5       functional and ecological–economic approaches from the 1930s to the 1950s
6       and the symbolic archaeology from the 1980s to the present all attest to a
7       continual interest in these broader issues. But these are also always histor-
8       ically and locally situated and usually researched by people who have
9       developed an area and/or period specialism in which they articulate these
1011    ideas. Indeed, it is expected of most academic archaeologists today that they
1       do have such a specialism over and above the material specialism they may
2       have (e.g. zooarchaeology). Area/period ‘ﬂoaters’ are frowned upon, decried
3       as ﬁckle and said to never know any material properly. These professional
4       divisions are no accident and are directly related to the historical devel-
5       opment of the discipline, in particular the emergence in the 1920s of the
6       culture-history or culture-group approach, which, though it no longer holds
7       sway, still affects the way we work. Yet ironically, although archaeology
8       has developed these regional or area specialisms, there has remained a strong
9       tendency for generalisation in archaeology to be of such a nature that it
20111   directly contradicts such a specialism – that generalisation is, de facto cross-
1       cultural. This is a complex issue which I will reserve for the end of this
2       chapter, but the theme of generalisation and its relation to both area/period
3       specialisms on the one hand and the interpretation of spatio-temporal vari-
4       ability on the other will be a critical undercurrent throughout.
5          In the last chapter I discussed the inﬂuence of ﬁnds categorisations and
6       concomitant specialisms in archaeological work, categorisations which
7       stretch back a long way and in many cases may be deleterious to study. In
8       this chapter I want to begin by exploring the development of culture history
9       and examine its legacy in current practice, for it reaches into issues
30111   concerned with the professional period/area specialisms and how we circum-
1       scribe the limits to these specialisms. For example we may choose to
2       specialise in the Bronze Age of Britain – but why the Bronze Age and
3       why Britain? Clearly this is a historical question of how the particular divi-
4       sions became inscribed and is partly a product of both nationalism/modern
5       political boundaries in the case of space, and older evolutionary period divi-
6       sions in the case of time. But there is an even broader question buried
7       here, about how such specialisations relate to the role of space and time
8       in archaeological explanation or interpretation and, even further, how this
9       relates to the nature of generalisation in archaeology and broader discipli-
40111   nary divisions.
1
2
3
44111
                                                       The measure of culture   109

1111    The classification of culture
2
3       The partition of culture in Europe and North America
4       In the late nineteenth century, differences in archaeological assemblages
5       were linked into a temporal difference on the assumption that culture was
6       universal and therefore developed in a similar way everywhere; any differ-
7       ence would then have to be a temporal difference. As Daniel eloquently
8       puts it: ‘That was the keynote of the old epochal idea – that man’s cultural
9       development could be represented as a single sequence, and read in a cave
1011    section, just as the geological sequence could be read in a deep cutting
1       through the sedimentary rocks’ (Daniel 1975: 244). The later nineteenth
2       century saw an increase in sub-dividing Thomsen’s Three Ages into smaller
3111
        temporal divisions which culminated with the work of such people as
4
        Mortillet and Montelius. Mortillet’s elegant chart of French prehistory of
5
        1897 sub-divides the Three Ages into periods and these are further subdi-
6
        vided into epochs (see Figure 13), while Montelius’ typological studies of
7
        the Bronze Age produced a detailed sub-division of six periods. The epoch
8
        or period thus became the lowest-level time horizon and concomitantly the
9
        lowest level of cultural difference.
20111
           Periodisation was thus integral to the evolutionary paradigm for it
1
        provided the speciﬁc methodology for turning the idea into archaeological
2
3       reality; without it, neither ordering nor – more importantly – comparison
4       was possible. Although the grand evolutionary narrative is no longer a part
5       of archaeology, its methodological expression through periodisation remains,
6       even to the extent of the names we give periods although they may be
7       often inaccurate and inappropriate. Regardless of these semantic issues,
8       however, periodisation is still a fundamental part of our conception of
9       archaeological reality; how was this transformed with the emergence of the
30111   culture-historical approach?
1          It is interesting to see how the concept of the culture group emerged
2       out of the idea of periodisation and the afﬁnities they share. Part of the
3       impetus came from archaeological ﬁeldwork which began turning up anom-
4       alies – Daniel lists several examples where a previous epochal difference
5       was being shown as in fact contemporary. Similarly the same cultural
6       sequence in Europe was difﬁcult to apply elsewhere in the world – the lack
7       of a Bronze Age in sub-Saharan Africa, for example (Daniel 1975: 239–42).
8       Concomitantly, greater emphasis was being placed on regional variation,
9       speciﬁcally to link it into an ethnic group (e.g. Celts), which was part of
40111   a wider historicising trend associated with classical archaeology on the one
1       hand and a nationalistic archaeology on the other (Daniel 1975: 242–3;
2       Trigger 1978: 80–2; Trigger 1989: 155, 161–3; Díaz-Andreu and Champion
3       1996; Graves-Brown, Jones and Gamble 1996). The culmination of all this,
44111   as is often stated, was Childe’s The Dawn of European Civilisation (1925)
        110   Critical approaches to fieldwork

                                                         The measure of culture    111

1111    where the explication of cultural difference is drawn as a spatio-temporal
2       mosaic, best exempliﬁed in a table from the slightly later book, The Danube
3       in Prehistory (1929) (see Figure 14). As Childe himself said of the charts
4       in The Dawn, they ‘present the distribution in time and space of cultures,
5       assemblages of archaeological phenomena that should reﬂect the distinctive
6       behaviour patterns of human societies’ (Childe 1925: 341).
7          Comparing an earlier epochal chart by Mortillet with Childe’s culture-
8       group version (Figures 13 and 14), the contrasting conceptions of cultural
9       difference are quite clear; and yet maybe there is also a deeper similarity
1011    which we too easily ignore. For to some extent the groundwork for a culture-
1       group concept was already laid by the concept of the period or epoch, a
2       point which Daniel makes when he says ‘the French system of “epochs”
3111    like the Acheulian, the Azilian, or the Cébénnian was, in a way, a step
4       towards the recognition of cultural groups . . .’ (Daniel 1975: 147). This is
5       wonderfully illustrated in a story Daniel relates about an altercation between
6       Mortillet and a museum curator:
7
8           De Mortillet had arranged the collections in the Saint-Germain Museum
9           according to his carefully graded subdivisions. He was infuriated because
20111       the curator of the Museum, Alexander Bertrand, changed the word Epoch
1           on the labels in every case to Type. Adrien de Mortillet characterised this
2           action as a craven compromise with the opponents of prehistory.
3                                                                       (ibid.: 125–6)
4
5       By labelling epochs as types, the curator was in effect re-interpreting them;
6       moreover there is even the suggestion that Bertrand may have regarded
7       such changes in terms of ethnic migrations as he saw the transition from
8       the Stone Age to the Bronze Age in terms of an Aryan invasion (Childe
9       1956: 27–8). Ironically, as Childe says:
30111
1           de Mortillet himself helped to show that one assemblage of type-fossils
2           could be used to deﬁne, not only a period of archaeological time in
3           France, but also a nation known to written history; the types, charac-
4           terising his ‘Marnian’ in France and Switzerland, proved in Italy to be
5           distinctive of the Gauls, known to have descended from that direction.
6                                                               (Childe 1956: 28)
7
8       We can see even further afﬁnities between the epochal and culture-group
9       conception of prehistory when we look at the way such schemes were for-
40111   malised in particular in terms of different scales or hierarchies. A hierarchi-
1       cal approach to the culture group was developed by Childe in Piecing Together
2       the Past; there he deﬁnes three levels – the culture-cycle, the culture and the
3       variant, which had clear spatial connotations (1956: 142–3). If we juxtapose
44111   Childe’s spatial and Mortillet’s temporal hierarchy of divisions, the two are
Figure 14
Childe’s chart of
Danubian culture
groups
                                                              The measure of culture   113

1111    remarkably similar (see Table 5). No direct correlation is implied in this
2       table, that is, the Bronze Age is not equatable with Childe’s culture cycle,
3       but that is not the point; it is that the way in which the archaeological con-
4       cept of culture is articulated is similar, and that given the way the epochal
5       deﬁnition of culture was scaled, such a scheme as Childe’s might have been
6       inevitable. More signiﬁcantly, at the time when the culture-group concept
7       was still relatively new, there was a great deal of overlap which is well illus-
8       trated by the confusion Childe discusses surrounding the ways in which a
9       period was conceptualised (Childe 1956: 94–9). He gives three – as a sub-
1011    division of one of the Three Ages, as an epoch, or as a culture-period – of
1       which the latter for Childe was the clearest, for it is deﬁned not in terms
2       of technology (as in the Three Ages) or cultural evolution (as with epochs)
3111    but by the culture group and its temporal limits.
4          However, despite the afﬁnities between periodisation and the construction
5       of the culture concept, there was also a point of tension. The development
6       of prehistory was deﬁned though periodisation, speciﬁcally the Three Age
7       System and material culture was primarily organised along these divisions.
8       However, when more speciﬁc culture-historical concerns developed, there
9       was an inevitable clash between these traditional periodisations and the
20111   need for independent chronology. Childe summed it up well:
1
2           Thomsen’s three ages did enable him to arrange his collection of Danish
3           relics in the right chronological order. It would have broken down had
4           it been extended to collections from Greece and Greenland. To deter-
5           mine what Danish products should be displayed as contemporary with
6           Bronze Age relics from Greece or Esquimaux Stone Age artifacts a time
7           scale quite independent of the material must be invoked.
8                                                                  (Childe 1935: 2)
9
30111   The problem was synchronicity – how to determine, for example, if the
1       Bronze Age in Britain occurred earlier, at the same time, or later than in,
2       say, southeast Europe. This was not so important in the nineteenth century
3       because it was the general evolutionary progress of European prehistory
4       through these periods that mattered, not the absolute dates of when it
5       occurred (see Chapter two). However, with the emergence of interest in
6
7           Table 5 Mortillet’s (left) and Childe’s (right)
8           schema of the partition of culture

40111       Times (Temps)              –
1           Ages (Ages)                Culture cycle
            Periods (Périodes)         Culture
2           Epochs (Époques)           Variant
3
                           Site / Assemblage
44111
        114    Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    speciﬁc culture histories, one of the major issues was that of diffusion, in par-
2       ticular of distinguishing similarities between culture groups caused by diffu-
3       sion from those which resulted from historical or independent development.
4       Periodisation merely conﬂated this problem because it was relative to that
5       which one was measuring, not absolute; it was necessary to have an inde-
6       pendent chronology.
7          Thus Childe’s innovative space-time charts of archaeological cultures
8       explicitly eschewed the use of periodisations along the time axis in favour
9       of dates, even if the exact position of the culture group or focus along the
1011    chronological axis might be imprecise and subject to change. Thus control
1       of time was a critical factor in Europe (and North America) during this
2       period of archaeology; whether it was to offset the Three Age System or
3       break down the culture-area concept (see p. 47), absolute dates were deci-
4       sive in the articulation of the culture-historical approach. Indeed, the
5       necessity for an independent chronology can be seen as the driving force
6       behind the development of absolute dating techniques, which initially
7       depended on cross-dating from historical/written sources but were eventu-
8       ally dropped in the wake of the radiocarbon revolution (Renfrew 1976).
9       This ‘revolution’, however, must not be painted as some kind of progres-
20111   sion of archaeological science but was a development driven by the particular
1       historical conditions of the 1930s and 1940s which demanded such a tech-
2       nique. One might go so far as to say the development of radiocarbon dating
3       was an inevitable consequence of these conditions (Lucas 1997).
4          Childe’s deﬁnition of culture remained a key notion in archaeology; thus
5       the British archaeologist Stuart Piggott wrote on the eve of New Archaeology:
6
7             An archaeological culture has to be deﬁned in terms of its material
8             content, but these have to be related to the dimensions of time and
9             space. By the various means of dating . . . the signiﬁcant assemblage of
30111         material traits taken to deﬁne the culture has to be given its chrono-
1             logical limits so far as possible. Its spatial bounds can be approximately
2             determined by plotting on a map all ﬁnds of the relevant archaeolog-
3             ical types which constitute the culture.
4                                                                   (Piggott 1959: 97–8)
5
6       Almost as soon as it was written, this notion of culture should have become
7       redundant; and yet it lingered. Indeed, as late as the 1960s, David Clarke
8       developed a hierarchy similar to Childe’s of technocomplex, culture group,
9       culture, subculture and site/assemblage which is much more detailed in its
40111   articulation (Clarke 1978: 366, ﬁgure 75). He himself said of this model,
1       however, that
2
3             archaeological entities do not really exist in simple hierarchical levels
44111         and that many of the most tantalising problems are false problems
                                                         The measure of culture    115

1111        arising from a model which is not entirely adequate. . . . The evidence
2           rather more closely approximates with a continuous multidimensional
3           system of elaborately networked multistate elements.
4                                                             (Clarke 1978: 413)
5
6       In other words, the partition of material culture at different spatial – or for
7       that matter temporal – scales is highly ﬂuid and need not fall into discrete
8       levels. The fact is, in the context of the New Archaeology, Clarke’s system
9       was incredibly anachronistic and out of place. While Binford and others
1011    were arguing for more behavioural and socially complex understandings of
1       the variability of material culture, any scheme which proposed a ﬁxed, over-
2       arching classiﬁcation is reminiscent of the older culture classiﬁcations of
3111    the 1930s and 1940s.
4          While such culture classiﬁcations played a role in British and more gener-
5       ally European archaeology, their development in the USA was far more
6       progressive and explicit – indeed, they are perhaps the deﬁning feature of
7       the period (Willey and Sabloff 1993: 121–5). Part of the reason for their
8       importance is clearly linked into the concern for chronology, and most of
9       the systems had explicit or implicit chronological implications. Indeed, it
20111   was precisely the fact that the earlier culture-area divisions such as those
1       of Holmes were viewed as too ahistorical (being ethnological) that more
2       complex classiﬁcations were required. Because of the nineteenth-century
3       assumption that the indigenous peoples did not really have a history, there
4       was and has always been a close link between ethnography and archae-
5       ology in the USA and the cultural boundaries and divisions which were
6       recorded for the present were largely projected into the past as stable enti-
7       ties. However, with the rise of culture history and the greater awareness
8       that changes in material culture had occurred, the stability – indeed utility
9       – of such culture areas was thrown into question. The earlier culture clas-
30111   siﬁcations were partly explicit attempts to avoid the culture-area concept
1       because of the way that genetic relations could be inferred from cultural
2       similarities – it meant that the historical connections were highlighted.
3       Unlike in Europe, North American archaeology was not seeking local
4       chronologies to avoid problems with periodisation but to break down the
5       culture-area concept. Indeed, it is ironic that while in Europe the culture-
6       group concept developed out of the emergence of ethnological concerns
7       for ethnic/tribal divisions as exempliﬁed in the Kulturkreislehre, in the USA
8       it was precisely a retreat away from this kind of association that drove
9       culture classiﬁcation.
40111      This concern can be seen in a debate that brieﬂy arose between Steward
1       and McKern, over the need for cultural taxonomies (McKern 1939, 1942;
2       Steward 1942). Steward argued that using a Direct Historical Approach
3       (i.e. an analogical interpretation of prehistoric societies using historic
44111   data) could furnish the information which cultural classiﬁcations aimed at.
        116   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    McKern argued that the Direct Historical Approach was not comparable
2       to taxonomy since it used ethnological criteria not archaeological ones. He
3       did not preclude the two working together, indeed he even encouraged it,
4       but the archaeological classiﬁcation concerned itself with similarities in
5       material culture and not with ethnological divisions, because such divisions
6       were ﬂuid across both space and time (McKern 1939: 303).
7          The ﬁrst attempt at a cultural classiﬁcation came from the Southwest,
8       particularly through the work of Kidder. Following his synthesis in his
9       Introduction to the Study of Southwestern Archaeology, published in 1924, a
1011    conference was convened at Pecos where Kidder had conducted his exca-
1       vations (see Chapter 2), in order to work out a general classiﬁcatory scheme
2       for the region (Kidder 1927). The basis of the approach was chronological
3       and a series of periods was agreed upon which deﬁned the various phases
4       of Southwestern culture. While the periodisation is still used with some
5       modiﬁcations, it had one major drawback: even within the area of research,
6       it was soon found that the periodisation was not as applicable in all regions
7       of the Southwest. This manifested itself primarily through asynchronicity
8       between regions (i.e. time lag), and furthermore the whole scheme was
9       better adapted to the northern region (where Kidder worked) than the
20111   south. Essentially, it was the same problem that European archaeologists
1       faced with their general periodisation which generated the concern for
2       absolute chronologies independent of periodisation.
3          The critique of the Pecos classiﬁcation came from Winifred and Harold
4       Gladwin in their paper A Method for the Designation of Cultures and their
5       Variations published in 1934. They argued that because of this asynchronicity
6       the Southwestern periods were better termed stages, and they developed a
7       more generalised, dendritic or tree-like scheme which, while it had genealog-
8       ical implications, was independent of chronology (Gladwin and Gladwin
9       1934). Thus at the base of the tree was the root which referred to the
30111   broadest cultural divisions in the Southwest. The root was subdivided into
1       stems, then branches and ﬁnally phases which was the smallest cultural
2       unit. It was presumed that the increasing cultural differentiation through
3       the tree system had chronological implications so that, in other words,
4       genetic relationships could be inferred through the branching system and,
5       more dubiously, all the cultures in the Southwest ultimately derived from
6       a common ancestor exempliﬁed in the root traits shared by all.
7          The system was modiﬁed by Colton in the light of the Midwestern system
8       (see below) but while the smaller subdivisions remain in use, the genetic
9       model as a whole has been dropped. Contemporary with the Gladwins’
40111   system for the Southwest was the Midwestern classiﬁcation developed by
1       a conference group in 1932 and revised by McKern a few years later (McKern
2       1939). Unlike the Gladwins’ system, this had no genetic implications and
3       was more a purely formal taxonomy based on cultural similarity, a fact
44111   which may partly have related to the lack of deeply stratiﬁed sites and the
                                                           The measure of culture   117

1111    wealth of unintegrated museum collections which existed in the Midwest
2       (Willey and Sabloff 1993: 123–4). However, more importantly, the problem
3       with genetic interpretations of the tree is that they do not allow for any
4       cross-cultural contact between phases – that is, all cultural similarities are
5       subsumed under a genetic model which cannot show diffusionary processes
6       across the splits (McKern 1939: 312). Nevertheless, it did not preclude
7       genetic interpretations and formally, it looks very similar to the Gladwin’s
8       system (see Figure 15).
9          The Midwestern taxonomic method was perhaps the most successful; it
1011    was copied for other areas and also used to modify the Gladwin system. At
1       the bottom of such systems were the sites, or more speciﬁcally (given multi-
2       period sites), the component (i.e. the assemblage). It is this which provided
3111    the basic material on which cultural similarities were compared, much as
4       they did in Europe (cf. Table 5, p. 113). As the theoretical ground shifted
5       in the 1950s, however, such taxonomies began to decline and the only
6       terms to remain in use were the smallest and largest (as well as the compo-
7       nent), with foci simply arranged chronologically and geographically,
8       enfolded within a larger pattern (Rouse 1972: 76; also see Rouse 1955).
9       However, it was the smallest, the focus (and its relation to the component)
20111   which remained the primary unit. Culture historical syntheses reached their
1       zenith with Indians before Columbus (1947) by Martin, Quimby and Collier,
2       which was essentially a descriptive list of traits deﬁning culture areas and
3       regions for the whole of North America, with accompanying chronology
4       charts at the back. Although not explicitly adopting the terminology, the
5       text and charts exhibit a clear minimalist classiﬁcation based on the focus
6       which they termed the ‘culture’, which was located in a spatio-temporal
7       framework (Martin, Quimby and Collier 1947, ﬁgure 122).
8
9
30111
1
2
3
4
5
6
7
8
9
40111
1
2
3
44111   Figure 15 North American culture classification schemes
        118   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111       The shift towards a more minimalist culture classiﬁcation (as used in
2       Europe) was theoretically marked in a paper by Gordon Willey and Philip
3       Phillips (published in two parts in 1953 and 1955), and later expanded
4       into a book, Method and Theory in American Archaeology (Willey and Phillips
5       1958). The problem with the taxonomic classiﬁcations remained the
6       inherent pull of a genetic interpretation to cultural similarity because of
7       its root structure, and so cultural similarities tended to be predominantly
8       viewed – and explained – in genealogical or genetic terms. As the 1950s
9       witnessed an increasing concern for functional interpretations, the need to
1011    actually examine the relationships between cultures (i.e. phases or foci)
1       rather than simply classify that relationship became more important. Thus
2       Willey and Phillips constructed a scheme which employed only the most
3       basic elements of the taxonomic systems, the component and the focus
4       (what they called the phase), which were cross-cut by two axes, the tradition
5       and the horizon (Willey and Phillips 1958: 41). Adopting Kroeber’s concept
6       of the horizon style as a dissemination or diffusion of traits over a large
7       area for a delimited period, they offset this with Willey’s concept of tradi-
8       tion which designated the long-term persistence of traits within the same
9       area. The two concepts of horizon and tradition provided what they saw
20111   as an integrative approach to providing area syntheses, yielding a balanced
1       model between genetic and diffusionary relationships between cultures.
2          In a response to the ﬁrst part of Willey and Phillips’ original paper, Rouse
3       re-afﬁrmed the central importance of the component and focus/phase and
4       elegantly argued for the importance of a staged approach using solely these
5       units and their correlation across time and space (Rouse 1955). He found
6       the concepts of tradition and horizon inadequate and argued for the need
7       to correlate foci/phases in a more systematic way, promoting a three-step
8       process: descriptive (i.e. formal or typological), distributional (i.e. through
9       physical contiguity, either spatially or temporally) and ﬁnally genetic (i.e.
30111   through diffusion or temporal continuity). The critical point was that no
1       classiﬁcatory scheme could encompass these possibilities within a single
2       model since both distributional and genetic correlations could be highly
3       variable (Rouse 1955: 720). None the less, while there were many inno-
4       vations in Rouse’s and Willey and Phillips’ scheme, both reinforced the
5       basic principle of culture classiﬁcation, if not taxonomically, then mini-
6       mally through the preservation of the focus and its determination in space
7       and time. Essentially, this is what was occurring in Europe, where the culture
8       group was the equivalent term to the focus/phase (Rouse 1955: 713). Indeed,
9       despite the rise and fall of taxonomic systems in the USA, in their after-
40111   math the same basic approach to cultural division as seen in Europe
1       remained intact, with the culture group forming the basis of understanding
2       variability in the archaeological record.
3          How inﬂuential are these cultural classiﬁcations today? While few would
44111   openly adopt the taxonomic or hierarchical method, the basic idea of a
                                                         The measure of culture    119

1111    cultural area or region deﬁned by similarities in constituent assemblages
2       remains a dominant, if not always explicit concept in archaeology. We still
3       draw general temporal and spatial limits around our data, which are often
4       based on the same culture divisions created earlier in the twentieth century,
5       whether it is broad periods such as the Three Age System or more particular
6       ones such as Beaker or the Hopewell. The word ‘culture’ frequently still
7       stands as a shorthand or generic term for the circumscription of assemblages
8       of material culture in space and time. To this extent, the culture classiﬁ-
9       cations of the 1920s-1950s still inﬂuence the way we do archaeology today,
1011    including the fact that as archaeologists we usually specialise in a partic-
1       ular area and period which was deﬁned by these classiﬁcations. At this
2       level, the historical reasons for contemporary practice are perhaps quite
3111    clear and to some degree unsurprising. Most archaeologists base their inter-
4       pretive research within the framework of culture historical divisions; much
5       of archaeology is taught at an undergraduate level on a region and period
6       basis; and if an archaeologist were to work in a new or unknown area, there
7       is little doubt that one of the ﬁrst tasks of ﬁeldwork would be to construct
8       a local chronology or type series. There remains a strong sense in which
9       time-space parameters of material culture form the basis to any interpre-
20111   tive study. What validity though, if any, do such classiﬁcations have today?
1
2
        Ethnicity and the critique of the culture concept
3
4       To help understand this question, it is important to consider further how the
5       concept of culture has been articulated in archaeology over the past century
6       or so (e.g. Kroeber and Kluckhohn 1952; Trigger 1978; Watson 1995). It was
7       only by the eighteenth century that the term ‘culture’, which derived from
8       Latin and was associated with agriculture (i.e. cultivation), took on its mean-
9       ing of human enlightenment of progress (Trigger 1978: 75; Díaz-Andreu
30111   1996: 51–2). Very soon afterwards, both in Spain and Germany, it also came
1       to be used more speciﬁcally for the customs or beliefs of a particular nation
2       or people, by writers such as Herder or de Masdeu (Díaz-Andreu 1996). Its
3       use continued into the nineteenth century where, in Germany in particular,
4       it became associated with a growing ﬁeld of culture historical studies
5       (Kulturgeschichte) associated with Gustav Klemm. In contrast, in Britain and
6       France the word ‘civilisation’ was used instead – typically by E. B. Tylor –
7       although towards the end of the nineteenth century the word ‘culture’ was
8       slowly adopted and eventually replaced ‘civilisation’ by the middle of the
9       next century. Thus Tylor’s classic deﬁnition: ‘Culture or Civilisation, taken
40111   in its widest ethnographic sense, is that complex whole which includes
1       knowledge, belief, arts, morals, law, custom and other capabilities and habits
2       acquired by man as a member of society’ (Tylor 1871: 1).
3          Throughout the nineteenth century, cultural differences also had racial
44111   associations, although this classiﬁcation usually had an inﬂuence on a wider
        120    Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    sphere between the West and the Rest. Nevertheless, archaeological studies
2       of the later nineteenth century and early twentieth often linked racial types
3       (based on skulls) with particular cultural periods. In terms of material culture
4       and artefacts, the association of particular types with this partitive concept
5       of culture was preﬁgured in earlier post-Renaissance attempts to link histor-
6       ically attested people such as the Saxons and Celts with material remains
7       although under quite different epistemological conditions (Hides 1996).
8       This only developed more systematically in the later nineteenth century
9       where ethnic groups were linked to material culture by researchers such as
1011    Montelius or Rudolf Virchow. The classic expression of this approach was
1       however by Gustav Kossinna in Die Herkunft der Germanen (The Origin of
2       the Germans) published in 1911. In this work he promoted the notion
3       of culture as deﬁnable archaeologically through spatial and temporal conti-
4       nuity of a number of traits and made identiﬁcations of major ethnic groups
5       such as Germans, Slavs and Celts, as well as more particular tribes (Jones
6       1997: 16). Both Kossinna and later Oswald Menghin were key inﬂuences
7       on Gordon Childe, who is regarded as the pivotal ﬁgure in the establish-
8       ment of the culture-historical approach in archaeology although, as Sian
9       Jones remarks, many of his contemporaries such as Crawford were using
20111   the term in similar ways (Crawford 1921; Jones 1997: 16–17). While Childe
1       ﬁrst used the term ‘archaeological culture’ in The Danube in Prehistory (1929),
2       it was in his classic paper ‘Changing methods and aims in prehistory’ (1935)
3       that he deﬁned culture more explicitly:
4
5             The culture is not an a priori category elaborated in the studies of
6             philosophers and then imposed from outside upon working archaeolo-
7             gists. Cultures are observed facts. The ﬁeld-worker does ﬁnd speciﬁc
8             types of tools, weapons, and ornaments repeatedly associated together
9             in graves and habitations of one kind and contrasted with the artifacts
30111         found in graves and settlements of another kind. The interpretation of
1             the observed phenomenon is supplied by ethnography. The traits of a
2             culture are thus presented together to the archaeologists because they
3             are the creations of a single people, adjustments to its environment
4             approved by its collective experience; they thus express the individuality
5             of a human group united by common social traditions.
6                                                                     (Childe 1935: 3)
7
8       Childe’s understanding of culture underwent various changes in his lifetime.
9       In particular he grew more and more cautious over identifying the archaeo-
40111   logical culture with any linguistic, ethnographic or political unit (e.g. Childe
1       1956: 132–3; see McNairn 1980: 46–73). However, the interpretation of the
2       archaeological culture as a bounded unit of material traits and equivalent in
3       some way to a bounded social unit remained, and it is a basic understanding
44111   which persisted until its critique in the 1960s and 1970s (Jones 1997: 24).
                                                        The measure of culture    121

1111       In the USA, the association between the archaeological culture and an
2       ethnographic conception of culture was even stronger due to the close links
3       between ethnography and archaeology and the predominance of the culture-
4       area approach (see p. 47). In a classic review paper by Dixon entitled ‘Some
5       aspects of North American archaeology’ published in 1913, he describes
6       archaeology as ‘prehistoric ethnology and ethnography – the incomplete and
7       wasted records of cultures which, often in vain, we try to reconstruct
8       and afﬁliate with their historic descendants’ (Dixon 1913: 558). For Dixon,
9       such an approach characterised a scientiﬁc and systematic archaeology,
1011    distinct from mere antiquarianism (ibid.: 565–6). Dixon’s call was later to
1       crystallise into what became known as the direct historical approach, ﬁrst
2       coined by W. R. Wedel (1938); it was a method which involved working
3111    backwards from the ethnographic to the archaeological record to establish
4       continuities. On its own, it had severe limitations, not least its presumption
5       of the ahistorical nature of ethnographic groupings and lack of concern for
6       chronology. However, despite some differences, it was retained as a useful
7       tool in the reconstruction of culture history in conjunction with the taxo-
8       nomic methods (McKern 1942). With the development of culture classiﬁ-
9       cation and taxonomies in the late 1920s and 1930s, the ethnographic link
20111   with archaeological cultures was increasingly downplayed although a broad
1       association with ‘a people’ was tacitly assumed (e.g. Colton 1942). As late
2       as 1972, Rouse was still arguing that his cultural complexes were effectively
3       the assemblages associated with a cultural group (Rouse 1972: 87).
4          The implicit understanding of culture in archaeology until the 1960s was
5       thus one of a bounded, homogeneous entity which ‘more or less’ corre-
6       sponded with a comparable social unit – a people, an ethnic group and, in
7       some cases, a race. It was this normative concept which came under ﬁre
8       in the New Archaeology. However, an important distinction needs to be
9       made between two uses of the word ‘culture’, a distinction made by Taylor
30111   in 1948 between a holistic and partitive conception (Taylor 1948: ch. 4).
1       The holistic sense applied to culture as an attribute of humans and human
2       society – it was a generic term which distinguished humans from other
3       animals. The partitive sense was the one I have been discussing above –
4       it is a particular manifestation of the generic sense among a group of people.
5       The distinction, though made by Taylor, existed tacitly before – archaeol-
6       ogists such as Childe sometimes talked about culture in a more holistic
7       sense, particularly in distinguishing the biological and racial aspects of
8       humans (McNairn 1980: 52–3). More generally, it is a distinction simply
9       between the diversity of humans and their common humanity.
40111      Most critiques of the culture concept since the 1960s – both the proces-
1       sual reaction to traditional culture-historical archaeology and the post-
2       processual reaction to the New Archaeology – have focused on the former,
3       holistic sense of the term. Processual archaeologists discuss culture as an
44111   adaptive or functional system, while post-processualists view it as symbolic
        122    Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    or meaningfully constituted for example, and so on. The focus of this debate
2       has largely been at this generic level of the culture concept since the 1960s
3       (e.g. see Watson 1995). In contrast – and as a consequence – discussion
4       of the partitive sense of the term was largely backgrounded. The kinds of
5       critiques that did emerge about the partitive meaning of culture were mostly
6       about the polythetic nature of cultures and their ‘fuzziness ‘, a point which
7       however went back to Childe (Childe 1956: 33–4; cf. Clarke 1968: 35–7,
8       263–6). Colin Renfrew took this argument to an extreme in 1977 when
9       he argued that the notion of a culture group was a ﬁction. His paper ‘Space,
1011    time and polity’ accentuated the problem of the ﬂuidity of the culture group:
1
2             For it is easy to show how spatial distributions, equivalent to the tradi-
3             tional cultural entities, can be generated by the archaeologist out of a
4             continuum of change. If uniformities and similarities in artefact assem-
5             blages are viewed as the result of interactions between individuals, and
6             if such interactions decrease in intensity uniformly with distance, each
7             point will be most like its close neighbours. Consider the point P lying
8             in a uniform plain, with its neighbours fairly regularly spaced around
9             it. Similarity in terms of trait C decreases with distance from point P.
20111         At the same time the variables A and B vary uniformly across the plain
1             with distance along the axes x and y. If the excavator ﬁrst digs at P
2             and recovers its assemblage, he will subsequently learn that adjacent
3             points have a broadly similar assemblage, which he will call ‘the P
4             culture’. Gradually its boundaries will be set up by further research,
5             with the criterion that only those assemblages which attain a given
6             threshold level of similarity with the ﬁnds from P qualify for inclusion.
7             So a ‘culture’ is born, centring on P, the type site, whose bounds are
8             entirely arbitrary, depending solely on the threshold level of similarity
9             and the initial, fortuitous choice of P as the point of reference.
30111                                                            (Renfrew 1977: 94–5)
1
2       Renfrew’s argument is in many ways the same as that given by Ford years
3       earlier regarding the question of whether types are real (Ford 1954; also
4       see Chapter 3). Yet as Hodder pointed out and Renfrew admitted, the
5       notion that ‘culture’ is a homogeneous continuum may be the case, but not
6       necessarily. Such a situation is only true if the association between the
7       groups is random; analysis can distinguish if the discontinuity is random or
8       not and at what scale it operates (Hodder and Orton 1978; Hodder 1982:
9       6–7). Indeed, in his paper Renfrew does not really overturn the notion
40111   of bounded entities in the past but rather reformulates them in terms of
1       neo-evolutionary social organisation and the archaeological correlates
2       of such entities. More importantly, though, just as processualists never really
3       addressed the concept of style in artefacts, so they never really grappled
44111   with the problem of identity or ethnicity in culture.
                                                         The measure of culture    123

1111       Thus, despite the shift from a normative to a functional (and later
2       symbolic) conception of culture, the issue of cultural identity was largely
3       pushed to one side in the wake of the New Archaeology (Jones 1997: 27).
4       While there were severe critiques of the normativism implicit in this concept
5       of culture, there were few attempts to address the nature of speciﬁc cultural
6       identity or ethnicity (but see p. 138). In fact, in many ways the use of
7       culture classiﬁcations, as I have already mentioned, continued – and
8       continues in practice with little thought for what this might mean (ibid.).
9       Part of this relates to the fact that such classiﬁcations are often considered
1011    an empirical rather than interpretive question – yet, as this chapter is
1       attempting to show, such a distinction is highly ﬂawed. However, another
2       part is related to the fact that ethnicity remained implicitly regarded as a
3111    normative concept and, given the processual turn, attention naturally turned
4       to more dynamic and functional interpretations of spatial and temporal
5       variability. In the next section, I want to examine these other dimensions
6       and look at how variability has been interpreted across space and time since
7       the 1960s. At the end of the chapter, I will return to the theme of iden-
8       tity and, in particular, raise issues about the consequences for an archaeology
9       which abandons a partitive concept of culture – at least as a privileged axis
20111   of differentiation.
1
2
        The interpretation of spatio-temporal variability
3
4
        Settlement and spatial archaeologies
5
6       The demise of the normative view of culture was already presaged in the
7       late 1940s with the call for more functionalistic interpretations of culture
8       such as those of Steward and Setzler (1938), a trend in the USA summarised
9       in a paper by Bennett (1943). However, Taylor’s ‘A study of archaeology’
30111   and its promotion of a conjunctive approach was the ﬁrst in-depth critique
1       of culture classiﬁcation – or the ‘comparative approach’ as he termed it.
2       He called for the study of the relations within cultures as well as between
3       them: ‘This attitude, the conjunctive approach, considers a site to be a
4       discrete entity with career and cultural expressions of its own. It is no
5       longer just one more unit in a spatial and temporal range of comparative
6       units’ (Taylor 1948: 96).
7          Taylor’s conjunctive approach was important in highlighting the impor-
8       tance of a site as a locus of activity, rather than just a manifestation of a
9       culture group, and it was this critical recognition which was pivotal in
40111   changing the way spatio-temporal differences in assemblages were viewed.
1       Two kinds of approaches to a more site-oriented archaeology developed in
2       the second half of the twentieth century, both in Britain and the USA,
3       one coming from a more ecological or economic context, the other being
44111   more social or political in orientation (Trigger 1968). Such approaches in
        124   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    the USA took their direct inﬂuence from Steward, whose work was ecolog-
2       ically oriented, rather than from Taylor who, despite addressing a wide
3       range of fundamental issues, for a number of reasons never had the impact
4       he deserved (Watson 1995: 685–6). Julian Steward’s Theory of Culture
5       Change was, with Willey and Phillips’ Method and Theory, a major theo-
6       retical text of the 1950s. Published in 1955, it explicitly distinguished
7       between a scientiﬁc, generalising approach and a historical, particularising
8       approach, boldly favouring the former (Steward 1955: 3). Outlining his
9       famous concepts of multi-linear evolution and cultural ecology, of partic-
1011    ular relevance here that he uses of different levels of socio-cultural
1       integration (e.g. the family, the community, the nation); moreover, he crit-
2       icised the ‘culture-area’ concept of cultural classiﬁcations, arguing that it
3       had serious limitations when trying to analyse process or function.
4          While Steward’s work was perhaps the most important of its kind in the
5       1950s, subsequent ecological and environmental approaches were perhaps
6       more inﬂuenced by Leslie White, especially through his student Betty
7       Meggers. Meggers adopted a quite explicit environmental determinism along
8       with White’s more rigid unilinear evolutionism, both of which she outlined
9       as the basis for a new synthesis in archaeology in the volume New
20111   Interpretations of Aboriginal American Culture History, published in the same
1       year as Steward’s book (Meggers 1955; also see Meggers 1954). This volume
2       is interesting in that many of the papers read like traditional culture history
3       with merely a nod in the direction of environmental and evolutionary
4       factors, and Meggers’ claim for ‘new interpretations’ seems rather hollow,
5       her own work excepted. It was in Britain and Europe that many of the
6       more explicit models in an ecological approach were developed.
7       Topographical approaches to archaeology had a long pedigree in Britain
8       (see Chapter one) and between the 1920s and 1950s the work of Cyril
9       Fox, O. G. S. Crawford and especially Grahame Clark all helped to estab-
30111   lish the importance of seeing an archaeological site in its environmental
1       context.
2          Grahame Clark’s ﬁrst general book Archaeology and Society published in
3       1939, argued from a functionalist standpoint but in direct opposition to
4       Childe’s Marxism. Clark asserted that the primary function of any society
5       was survival and that therefore ecological constraints acted on all societies.
6       The importance of understanding the environment and its effect on societies
7       was therefore basic to archaeology. Indeed he claimed, as did others of the
8       period, that the economic aspects to a society were easier to understand in
9       archaeological contexts than those of social organisation or belief because of
40111   the material nature of archaeological data. Clark’s ideas received their
1       applied expression through his work on the mesolithic site of Star Carr and
2       his general synthesis Prehistoric Europe: The Economic Basis, both published
3       in the 1950s (Clark 1952, 1954). The latter explicitly related broad economic
44111   systems to ecological zones, drawing on the concept of ecosystem.
                                                        The measure of culture    125

1111       The direct descendant of Clark’s approach came in the 1970s with the
2       work of the Cambridge-based palaeoeconomic school. Using geographical
3       models, Eric Higgs and C. Vita-Finzi developed the concept of site-
4       catchment analysis which made far more explicit the ideas of the previous
5       generation on the importance of environmental context (Vita-Finzi and
6       Higgs 1970). By circumscribing an area around a site, the available resources
7       and access to those resources could be mapped out and associated with the
8       subsistence of the site. Through two books edited by Higgs, the palaeo-
9       economic approach was developed further and focused on the relationship
1011    between population and resources, although it is the model of site-
1       catchment that has remained the enduring contribution (Higgs 1972, 1975).
2          The other strand of settlement archaeology, while not excluding envi-
3111    ronmental factors, focused much more on the social organisation of
4       settlements, examining settlements in relation to each other rather than
5       just to their environment. In the USA, Willey and Phillips, although heavily
6       inﬂuenced by Steward, went beyond his ecological approach to consider
7       this broader relationship. In their Method and Theory in American Archaeology
8       published in 1958 they similarly describe different levels of integration,
9       although clearly from a more archaeological perspective, deﬁning three types
20111   of archaeological data – the ﬁrst being the formal or material type (i.e. the
1       component and focus), the second two being spatial and temporal. These
2       latter two are manifest at different scales, primarily deﬁned by the spatial:
3       thus the site lies at the lowest level, then locality, region and area, with
4       temporal sequences based on these spatial ones (i.e. local sequence and
5       regional sequence; Willey and Phillips 1958: 17–27). The idea that differ-
6       ences between assemblages might relate to functional relations between
7       sites rather than simply demarcate cultural entities gained practical appli-
8       cation in Willey’s project in the Virú valley, Peru, published a few years
9       earlier. Encouraged by Steward but going beyond his cultural ecology, Willey
30111   looked at the different kinds of sites in the Virú valley (such as cemeteries,
1       settlements, temples) in terms of changing patterns of social and political
2       organisation (Willey 1953). Willey’s study stimulated a spate of similar
3       surveys such as Robert Adams’ research in the Near East. In 1968 a volume
4       called Settlement Archaeology was published in which Willey looked back
5       and interestingly appraised such studies as indicative of new approaches but
6       not a new archaeology (Willey 1968: 208; Chang 1968).
7          Nevertheless, the volume contained some interesting debates, particu-
8       larly between Chang and Rouse over the primacy of settlement as a unit
9       of analysis, which did not impress Willey. While Rouse argued for the
40111   primacy of the component-focus on the basis of a traditional epistemology
1       which moved from description to interpretation, Chang saw the archaeo-
2       logical process as one of continual interpretation focused on typological
3       variation within and between settlements (Rouse 1968; Chang 1968). The
44111   difference was more than procedural as Willey seemed to think, but rather
        126   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    went to the heart of the distinction between cultural, classiﬁcatory and
2       processual approaches to settlement: for Rouse, the culture group remained
3       more basic, while for Chang what was important was the settlement, or
4       the site where people lived. This point Chang had made in a paper published
5       ten years earlier, drawing on ethnographic information for the relation
6       between social organisation and settlement patterns. It was perhaps ten
7       years too early (Chang 1958).
8          The settlement approach only really became signiﬁcant under the New
9       Archaeology where it was more fully developed in papers by Binford and
1011    Streuver (Binford 1964; Streuver 1968b). Both authors discussed the issue
1       in terms of research design and the rise of sampling procedures, highlighting
2       the signiﬁcance of site location and distribution. A similar focus on such
3       issues also developed in Britain (e.g. Cherry and Shennan 1978). However,
4       perhaps the best practical example of the time was the volume edited by
5       Kent Flannery, The Early Mesoamerican Village, published in 1976, which
6       employed the same idea of different levels of socio-cultural integration as
7       espoused by Willey and Phillips. Flannery uses a very detailed spatial hier-
8       archy starting with the smallest unit, the feature, through activity area,
9       household, village, and so on up to regional and inter-regional network
20111   units. While he cites Streuver as an inﬂuence, he was also drawing on
1       human geography, particularly Peter Haggett’s locational analysis (Flannery
2       1976: 8). It was Gregory Johnson, however, who most explicitly used
3       geographical approaches in the USA, partly inﬂuenced by the develop-
4       ments in Britain (Johnson 1977).
5          In Britain, a similar change was occurring which was much more heavily
6       inﬂuenced by human geography – indeed the theoretical developments in
7       archaeology and geography often mirrored each other (Earle and Preucel
8       1987; Wagstaff 1987; Tilley 1994: 7). Moreover, in Britain there was a pre-
9       vious history of using geographical and topographical frameworks to analyse
30111   the archaeological record, especially through the work of O. G. S. Crawford
1       who moved from geography into archaeology. His major book Man and his
2       Past, published in 1921, is pervaded by his geographical background, as was
3       his early study of the relation of Bronze Age settlement to topography and
4       geology (Crawford 1912; 1921: 80–2). Crawford even went so far as to say
5       that ‘it is probable that most of the advances in archaeological knowledge
6       will be made by means of geographical studies’ (Crawford 1921: 132).
7       However, while Crawford was using geographical approaches, this remained
8       within a culture-group approach and the most his and other comparable
9       studies ever achieved was to correlate cultural divisions with geographical
40111   ones. It was not until the 1970s under the inﬂuence of David Clarke that
1       more dynamic approaches were taken, characterised by the sub-ﬁeld of spatial
2       archaeology.
3          David Clarke drew heavily on contemporary ideas in geography, particu-
44111   larly the work of Haggett on locational analysis to which Flannery was also
                                                          The measure of culture     127

1111    drawn; Clarke even named his book Models in Archaeology after one of
2       Haggett’s. It was this work by Clarke, published in 1972, which created the
3       focus for spatial archaeologies from which a number of other works followed,
4       such as Clarke’s Spatial Archaeology (1977), Ian Hodder and Clive Orton’s
5       Spatial Analysis in Archaeology (1976) and Hodder’s The Spatial Organization of
6       Culture (1978). It was Hodder’s co-authored book which provided the heavy-
7       duty methodological grounding for such studies, drawing on current quanti-
8       tative techniques in geography such as point pattern analysis, regression
9       analysis and spatial association. There Hodder and Orton emphasised the
1011    importance of quantitative approaches (as opposed to the usual ‘dots on maps’
1       which most archaeologists employed), as well as the related need to under-
2       stand the processes which lead to the spatial patterning observed in the
3111    archaeological record (Hodder and Orton 1976: 8). It clearly represented a
4       need to go beyond many of the more simplistic invasionist/diffusionist inter-
5       pretations of distribution patterns which characterised the earlier culture-
6       group model of prehistory (e.g. Renfrew 1969), while also being part of the
7       scientiﬁc discourse which marked so much of the New Archaeology. Such
8       approaches continued into the 1980s and after, as models such as central-
9       place theory and rank-size distributions were used to analyse the spatial organ-
20111   isation of settlement structures, especially of more complex societies.
1          Spatial analysis within sites was comparatively under-developed until the
2       1980s. One of the earliest attempts was Grahame Clark’s excavation of
3       the mesolithic site at Star Carr where he looked at artefact patterning to
4       interpret the nature of the site (Clark 1954). However, it was only in the
5       1960s/1970s that such studies became more common and were informed
6       by a more explicit methodological approach. Two broad kinds of analysis
7       can be identiﬁed; one which studies a point pattern distribution of an arte-
8       fact (or the co-occurrence of more than one artefact), and the other which
9       uses broader deposit or feature association with an artefact or artefacts. The
30111   former was pioneered in particular by Leroi-Gourhan and his colleagues at
1       the Upper Palaeolithic site of Pincevent, France, inaugurating a new era
2       in intra-site spatial analysis (Leroi-Gourhan and Brézillon 1966). This has
3       subsequently been developed through the use of quantitative techniques
4       and ethnoarchaeological studies, particularly by Robert Whallon and others,
5       initially as part of the attempt to resolve the function–style debate of Lewis
6       Binford and François Bordes (Whallon 1973; Kroll and Price 1991b: 2).
7       The 1980s saw a proliferation of such intra-site spatial analyses which has
8       continued and developed many of the major themes of the 1970s (e.g. see
9       the papers in Hietala 1984, Kent 1987 and Kroll and Price 1991a).
40111      One of the problems faced by mainly the broader, regional approaches was
1       the heterogeneity of space – that is, the real space of mountains, rivers, coast-
2       lines – of topography. All the more analytical studies developed in the 1970s
3       on proximity or distance either between sites and a centre (such as regression
44111   analysis and trend surface analysis) or between all sites in a given area (such
        128   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    as nearest neighbour analysis and the construction of Thiessen polygons),
2       faced this problem. Hodder and Orton’s study of Oxfordshire Roman pottery,
3       for example, showed how an apparently random distribution of sites made
4       sense when two fall-off curves were separated according to whether sites could
5       be reached by water or not (Hodder and Orton 1976). Such awareness of the
