5       as if Pitt Rivers for example belonged to an alien discipline, yet can archae-
6       ological historiography paint a simple picture of progress in methods and
7       techniques without risk of ridicule?
8          Usually the issue is sidestepped in favour of generalising statements such
9       that studying a discipline’s history puts it in perspective, helps to clarify why
30111   we do things in this way and this of course is undeniable (e.g. Meltzer 1989:
1       12). Sometimes the implication is bolder, that the history of archaeology is
2       a history of improvement, although even here it is not directly stated but
3       suggested. Whether it is too obvious to state or too provocative, though, is
4       it adequate? Surely we must demand that looking at the history of archaeol-
5       ogy itself requires some justiﬁcation? Meltzer discusses the view of Glyn
6       Daniel, perhaps the ﬁrst historian of archaeology in English, who regarded a
7       historiographic perspective as useful in so far as it increased our knowledge
8       of the discipline – the more we know, the less we are likely to repeat the
9       errors of the past (Daniel 1981; Meltzer 1989). This view sounds just like
40111   the schoolbook view of history in general and is of course naive in the
1       extreme. Meltzer’s own view is more general and is referred to at the start of
2       this paragraph: ‘the best way to understand why we do what we do is to unfold
3       the beliefs that have structured, and continue to structure, our work’ (Meltzer
44111   1989: 12). Why this is important is not as explicitly addressed but Meltzer
        196    Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    does appear to believe that progress is better served by being able to tran-
2       scend one’s own history through self-knowledge (ibid.: 19).
3          If we turn to Bruce Trigger who is one of the major living writers in
4       English of the history of the discipline, he too asks what a history of the
5       discipline serves:
6
7             A ﬁnal question is whether a historical study can measure progress in
8             interpretation of archaeological data. Are steady advances being made
9             towards a more objective and comprehensive understanding of archae-
1011          ological ﬁndings, as many archaeologists assume? Or is the interpretation
1             of such data largely a matter of fashion and the accomplishments of a
2             later period not necessarily more comprehensive or objective than those
3             of an earlier one?
4                                                                  (Trigger 1989: 25)
5
6       Trigger’s answer is essentially for the former, and his reason also serves to
7       explain why studying history is so important. Because he sees the problem
8       as one of recognising social bias in interpretation, only history can offer
9       the perspective which disentangles enduring truth from the historically/
20111   socially contingent; even if it cannot erase all bias, ‘it almost certainly
1       increases the chances of gaining more rounded insights into what has
2       happened in the past’ (Trigger 1989: 4).
3          Trigger essentially states more explicitly what Meltzer alluded to, and in
4       the end such histories validate current practice in terms of scientiﬁc progress;
5       regarding this, Hinsley makes an important point:
6
7             most [histories] still look inward for a logic of progress in data gathering
8             and in theoretical modelling. However one modiﬁes such history – intro-
9             ducing notions of paradigmatic structures, admitting occasional wrong
30111         turns or even the exclusive nature of the scientiﬁc community under
1             conditions of ‘normal science’ – it still remains essentially Whig history,
2             an Enlightenment legacy that assumes what it purports to illustrate: an
3             upward trajectory toward more accurate, cumulative knowledge.
4                                                                     (Hinsley 1989: 80)
5
6       If presenting the history of the discipline has become almost synonymous
7       with justifying the status quo (but cf. Taylor 1948 and more recently, Kehoe
8       1998), theory has taken almost the inverse role, standing for the critique
9       of contemporary practice. Major theoretical texts of the past twenty to
40111   thirty years have typically looked at the broad spectrum of archaeological
1       work as it is done today and showed ways in which it is lacking and ways
2       in which it can be developed. The epithet ‘new’ has commonly been linked
3       to changes in archaeological perspective, especially in the USA – the new
44111   stratigraphic methods developed in the second decade of the twentieth
                                                                 Eventful contexts    197

1111    century announced a ‘New Archaeology’ in a paper of that title by Clark
2       Wissler in 1917, while a collection of papers which drew on evolutionary
3       and environmental themes espoused by Steward or White were published
4       in 1955 as New Interpretations of Aboriginal American Culture History
5       (Anthropological Society of Washington). All this before Binford and his
6       students proclaimed their New Archaeology.
7          However such critique and ‘newness’ can only go so far without under-
8       mining the very existence of archaeology. As Wylie says, remarking on this
9       recurrent phenomenon of ‘new archaeologies’, each time a break is urged
1011    with the past, there has been an equally vociferous opposition and support
1       for traditional approaches (Wylie 1993: 20). Thus today it has been argued
2       the New Archaeology is not really ‘new’ at all. Kent Flannery was an early
3111    detractor (Flannery 1973, 1982), but the French archaeologist Paul Courbin
4       has written what is probably the most extensive and scathing critique,
5       arguing that there is very little in it which is in fact new:
6
7           In reality, the real progress made in the past three decades has not
8           been in theory, as the New Archaeology would have us believe: it has
9           been in techniques, whether it be contributions of the ‘sciences’ to
20111       archaeology or the help of statistics, and especially computer studies.
1           These advances are very real. But they are completely independent of
2           the New Archaeology. . . .
3                                                             (Courbin 1988: 159)
4
5       Courbin’s critiques may be too extreme, but they represent the same funda-
6       mental view of history and theory in the end, simply stressing continuity
7       over change. Since, for all the critique theory offers, it still needs to preserve
8       the integrity of the discipline or else it becomes self-defeating, I doubt any
9       theoretical archaeologist, however radical, would deny all links with the
30111   past of their discipline. Theoretical archaeology is never simply pursued as
1       critique but also as productive development; in questioning current ways
2       of interpreting the past, alternative ways are offered in the process. The
3       same dual aspect to theory is brought out in the relatively new ﬁeld of
4       metaarchaeology, a term coined by the philosopher Lester Embree in the USA,
5       and which he deﬁnes as a ‘critical reﬂection upon the science of archae-
6       ology’ (Embree 1992a: 3). Much of his work has looked speciﬁcally at the
7       sub-discipline of theoretical archaeology, which he sees as a rather loose
8       term to cover broadly any kind of reﬂexive or critical archaeology, some of
9       which may reﬂect on archaeology as a whole (therefore falling under
40111   what he calls metaarchaeology), some of which is more methodological/
1       problem oriented (therefore falling under what he calls substantive research)
2       (Embree 1989: 68).
3          Embree raises a number of signiﬁcant questions which metaarchaeology
44111   can address, including the status of archaeology as a science, and its relation
        198   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    to society, to the historiography of archaeology and to philosophy (Embree
2       1992a, ff.41). However, much of his emphasis lies on how (theoretical)
3       archaeology is done rather than on its value. In his analysis of Gordon
4       Willey’s work on the Virú valley in Peru, for example, he separates out the
5       question of whether Willey’s work was an improvement or not, from
6       the question of whether or not it effected a change in how archaeology
7       was done, and only gives the former question a few sentences at the end
8       of the paper (Embree 1992b: 191). Tellingly, he says that the question can
9       also be historiographic, and indeed the very framing of it in terms of
1011    ‘improvement’ merely conﬁrms that he sees the value of theoretical archae-
1       ology ultimately in progressive terms – progress being deﬁned by ‘a new
2       mode of evidencing’, that is, new ways of collecting, analysing and synthe-
3       sising data. Quite how this is assessed though is left rather vague.
4          From a bold perspective, theory/critique is part of the same process of
5       progression presented in history; without it, there would be no advances,
6       no developments – no history in effect. In this view, history and theory
7       are interlinked in the grand narrative of scientiﬁc progress. An alternative
8       view might simply highlight the importance of change, with the critical
9       term not being theory or history but practice; practice lies between them,
20111   supported by history, undermined by theory; but in keeping them apart, it
1       also enables them to complement each other through an ongoing her-
2       meneutic. Histories of archaeology do not have to afﬁrm any progress, or
3       even any disciplinary identity – especially as history and theory are inte-
4       grated through practice. I hope that this book has demonstrated this. But
5       whatever archaeology is, it is not static. The very processes of archaeolog-
6       ical interpretation which seem to represent a ‘new consensus’ (e.g. Preucel
7       1991; Wylie 1992), namely the interdependency of interpretation and data,
8       must also affect the shape of the discipline as well as the shape of our repre-
9       sentations of the past. The power of this dialectic is reﬂexive as well as
30111   dynamic – its effects rebound back on the discipline, how it is constituted,
1       as well as articulating its focus, its subject matter.
2          Another way of characterising this is to say that archaeology is not just
3       a mental or theoretical exercise – it is a practice as well. Hodder discusses
4       this in terms of the concept of praxis, a term used to denote the socially
5       and historically situated nature of archaeology – it is not passively contem-
6       plative but active and creative (Hodder 1992: 3; also see Hodder 1984).
7       The notion that archaeology is a social practice has been a recurrent theme,
8       especially but not exclusively, of post-processualism (e.g. Shanks and Tilley
9       1987a; Gero 1995). But I think there is still something missing from this
40111   equation – something which archaeology itself is all about: material culture.
1       Recall Taylor’s three orders of phenomena – ideas, behaviour and material
2       culture. I argued that one of the major innovations of post-processual theory
3       was how it integrated these previously disjunctive categories into a whole
44111   – each constituted the other. The stress on praxis as the conjunction of
                                                             Eventful contexts   199

1111    theory and practice could be seen as incomplete in the light of post-
2       processualists’ own formulation of their subject matter. If material culture
3       is so important and active, equally constituting as constituted by beliefs/
4       theory and behaviour/practice, then we need to include this in our concep-
5       tion of archaeology. Archaeology is not just contemplative, nor is it just a
6       social practice, it is also a material praxis.
7

2
        Conclusion
3       Material archaeologies
4
5
6
7
8
9
1011
1
2
3       In reﬂecting on what has been written so far, I would like to try and reit-
4       erate the directions in which this book has travelled. I began by establishing
5       the theme as an investigation into several key historical and conceptual
6       issues in archaeological practice. The development of practice was studied
7       initially through the changes in ﬁeldwork, and three major conceptual
8       trends were identiﬁed: a focus on ﬁnds or artefacts, a focus on assemblages
9       of ﬁnds and ﬁnally the concern for the social or behavioural context of
20111   ﬁnds/assemblages. It was argued that these foci are not necessarily exclu-
1       sive to any one particular point in the history of archaeology but rather
2       that, as foci, they provided a major orientation on the nature of the archae-
3       ological record and one which was linked in to the ways in which
4       archaeological ﬁeldwork was conducted. These foci in turn became the basis
5       for discussion in subsequent chapters – on ﬁnds analysis, culture classiﬁca-
6       tion and middle range theory. From the discussion of these foci, both from
7       a historical and conceptual perspective, it was seen that they exhibited a
8       practical dimension in the form of disciplinary specialisations. In the case
9       of ﬁnds analysis, such issues were tied back into ﬁnds specialisms; similarly,
30111   in the case of culture classiﬁcation, the issue of area/period specialisms was
1       questioned; and ﬁnally for middle range theory, the status of archaeology
2       itself as a ‘specialised’ discipline distinct from others such as anthropology
3       or history was highlighted.
4          In this book, therefore, I hope I have raised what I think are relevant
5       issues about the nature of archaeology, issues which have clear consequences
6       for the way we do archaeology both theoretically and practically. They
7       affect not just our interpretations of the past but the structure of our disci-
8       pline. In many ways, this book is a study of archaeology which embraces
9       the hermeneutic discussed at the end of the last chapter between theory
40111   and practice and applies it to the way archaeology itself functions – to
1       the archaeological process. In conclusion, I would like, unfortunately all
2       too brieﬂy, to take up the issue left hanging at the end of the last chapter
3       – the relation between this hermeneutic of theory and practice, and
44111   materiality.
                                               Conclusion: material archaeologies    201

1111       In the summer of 1998 I was excavating a Viking period farmstead called
2       Hofstaðir in northeast Iceland, of which the main turf longhouse had been
3       excavated previously in 1908 by a Danish archaeologist called Daniel Bruun.
4       It took several people several days to remove the backﬁll of Bruun’s trench
5       by hand, using spades and shovels, the weather was variable and there were
6       blackﬂies in abundance, crawling into every open part of the face – eyes,
7       nose, ears and mouth. At the beginning, the edge of the earlier trench cut
8       was not always very clear and it took some time before we realised how
9       the trench had been cut. As we moved down, we also had to be careful
1011    to stop at the level of the earlier cut since we knew that the excavator
1       had stopped at the ﬂoor but that he might have cut through it too and its
2       survival could be patchy. Another thing we were looking for was traces of
3111    a wall which the earlier excavator said was missing. As we cleaned up the
4       trench, we found indeed that the ﬂoors were patchy and that the exca-
5       vator had gone too deep, and that there was in fact a wall, a very clear
6       wall. The excavator had cut quite a way into it, as on other walls but had
7       still missed it, and yet by all accounts he was good for his day. There was
8       much speculation on why he excavated the way he did and why he did
9       not see the things we could . . .
20111      In re-excavating the 1908 trench at Hofstaðir, the questions were always
1       in terms of what Bruun or his workmen were doing, or why they had dug
2       like this. When I compare this with my thoughts on, for example, another
3       feature on the site such as a silo pit, they are similar but there is also a
4       difference. Throughout the process of excavation of the silo pit, the same
5       questions recurred but were always being posed in term of what the pit was
6       doing, how the edges were behaving. And this is my more typical reaction
7       to archaeological features, to see them as purely material objects which
8       generate problems of interpretation – they have no face, no person, they
9       are anonymous. One can easily see why this happens. With most archae-
30111   ology we have no personal associations with features, and even if we can
1       link some historical person with a site, it is unlikely we can actually join
2       that person with any particular feature, let alone its production. But this
3       difference is misleading for it is not really about attaching a face or person
4       to a particular feature – it is about attaching an intention. This means
5       understanding the feature in terms of some purpose; understanding what
6       the edges were doing with the pit was all part of my understanding of it –
7       in the ﬁrst instance as a pit dug by a person deliberately; and in the second,
8       as a pit dug for a particular purpose, in this case hay storage. If I had started
9       to see it as a different kind of pit, I might have had different expectations
40111   of what the edges would do.
1          This story provides a way into an alternative view of the relationship
2       between history, theory and practice. Excavating Bruun’s trench and the
3       interpretive process involved is in a way like using archaeological methods
44111   to understand the history of archaeology. Encountering the trenches of
        202   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    earlier excavators is not uncommon, especially on famous sites, and one
2       may learn things about the previous excavator and his or her record which
3       could not be gleaned from any written sources. An Icelandic friend, and
4       the person who initiated the project on the Viking farm mentioned in the
5       story, once told me that he wanted to do just this (Adolf Fridriksson, pers.
6       comm.). What could it mean to turn our methods back on to ourselves? I
7       ﬁnd this a tantalising possibility – it offers a reﬂexivity which is not simply
8       a questioning of why and how we do things, but a questioning that employs
9       the very methods we seek to interrogate. Putting it like this is interesting
1011    because it brings out the material nature of archaeological practice – it not
1       only studies material culture but uses and produces material culture in this
2       process. From this perspective, archaeology and its object begin to merge
3       (Figure 20).
4          Excavating an excavation might seem like a special case, and in most
5       cases archaeology and its object remain distinct. Are they though? What
6       if we see excavation as part of the archaeology of the site – and not just
7       any earlier excavation, but the one happening now. Thus as we uncover
8       the site we also add to it. The dominant ethos sees excavation as destruc-
9       tion, the ‘unrepeatable experiment’ at Barker once put it (Barker 1982:
20111   11–12). Now, it may be that we remove features, but this does not neces-
1       sarily have to be viewed as a subtractive process but rather as a transformative
2       one. An excavation trench is part of the history or archaeology of the site,
3       as much as anything else there. History does not stop – it is ongoing, and
4       when we come to a site we should not see ourselves as coming to some-
5       thing halted but as being part of something continuous. It is a little too
6       obvious that we write ourselves out of the sequence of events on a site,
7       that the top of a stratigraphic matrix is not our trenches and excavations
8       but the topsoil. It is very telling of our perception of the site as something
9       separate from us and our actions, pulling ourselves out of its history. To
30111   this extent, we are perhaps not being as true to the linearity of time we
1       portray, either in the past or in the development of archaeology.
2          But excavation is more than just another part of the history of a site –
3       it is special in the sense that it is reﬂexive, it reiterates everything that has
4       left a trace on the site. Inasmuch as excavating an excavation can be called
5       repetitive, all archaeological practice could be ascribed the same character;
6       it is an encounter which has taken place before. A friend once expressed
7       it this way – that every time we excavate a ditch or pit on a site, we are
8       in fact re-excavating it, we are repeating an act performed maybe thousands
9       of years ago, in exactly the same place (M. Knight, pers. comm.). This
40111   event is so commonplace in archaeology, yet it is an experience I had never
1       before heard expressed in this way and it makes the physical process of
2       excavation extremely signiﬁcant. It is very common, on the other hand, to
3       hear how holding an artefact that has not seen the light of day for thousands
44111   of years brings the past alive, is maybe even the closest one can get in
1111

6
7
8

1011
1

3111
4

20111
1
2       (a)
3
4
5

30111
1

3
44111   Figure 20 Excavation at a Viking farmstead in Iceland in 1908 (a) and 1995 (b)
        204   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    experiencing the past. But the mere holding of something, the touch and
2       the gaze it involves surely does not come anywhere close to the act of re-
3       excavation, an act involving the whole body, a productive act, not simply
4       a contemplative one.
5          Of course this perception of excavation only applies in a very restricted
6       sense since we are not removing the same kind of soil when excavating
7       the ditch, nor are we repeating the events of its ﬁlling up which may not
8       even be the result of human but of natural processes. But in a way, the
9       relevance here is not in faithful reproduction or duplication but in repetition;
1011    it is through repetition that a sense of difference is generated. Excavating
1       Bruun’s trench was also an act of repetition, yet while he was trying to
2       understand the edges of walls and ﬂoors, we were trying to understand his
3       attempt at understanding. What is this, if not an archaeology of archae-
4       ology? Or just archaeology for that matter? In the rest of this chapter I
5       want to explore ways in which archaeology is a material practice, or rather,
6       shall we say, a materialising practice. In so doing, this is perhaps less of a
7       conclusion, a closure, as an opening, a looking forward. By materialising,
8       I mean the ways in which we produce or create physical products in the
9       course of doing archaeology, two of which dominate – texts and images.
20111   Thus I will not be discussing the management of museums or heritage sites
1       here, which although they are certainly material practices, do not in them-
2       selves routinely create new objects. They are not materialising, although
3       they are linked to materialising practices such as the production of display
4       boards and captions, guidebooks, etc. It is this general ﬁeld of textual and
5       graphic production that I want to examine.
6
7
        Writing archaeology
8
9       The theme of archaeology as a textual practice has of course received much
30111   attention recently, especially in the wake of a similar turn in anthropology
1       (Clifford and Marcus 1986; Baker and Thomas 1990; Tilley 1993; Pluciennik
2       1999). Hodder in particular has questioned narrative styles in site reports
3       and more general syntheses (Hodder 1989b, 1993a). To paraphrase Marcus,
4       textualisation is at the heart of archaeology. How does this manifest itself?
5       Right at the beginning, if we can use such a term, we textualise archae-
6       ology in ﬁeld notebooks and context sheets. I discussed in the introduction
7       the ways in which context sheets affect practice through a system of checks,
8       controls and standardisation. The adoption of sheets, particularly in
9       replacing the ﬁeld notebook (as in Britain) where there were no prompts
40111   but just blank pages, is very telling of how perceptions of textualisation
1       have changed, particularly as this shift occurred alongside the New
2       Archaeology. Not that writing in notebooks was, or is, necessarily a free
3       or undisciplined affair – students learn what should and should not be
44111   placed in notebooks.
                                               Conclusion: material archaeologies    205

1111       Hodder’s study of the narrative form of site reports takes us into another
2       realm of textual production (Hodder 1989b). He shows how early reports
3       of excavations were often in the form of letters, and only later gradually
4       shifted to articles, many of which were read at some meeting or other. The
5       use of the ﬁrst person and the construction of the narrative around the act
6       of discovery was a predominant feature again, until by the end of the nine-
7       teenth century and the beginning of the twentieth, a form more familiar
8       to us emerged: writing in the passive voice, descriptive rather than narra-
9       tive. Hodder links these changes to broader shifts in the way science is
1011    conducted and authority established, away from individuals to institutions.
1       Indeed it is not hard to see how the form of textualisation in archaeology
2       relates to wider historical trends, especially in the shift from letters to
3111    articles (also see Hamilton 1999 for a similar, more recent critique). To
4       some extent, Hodder’s reservations against the dryness of modern site reports
5       have received a new twist, as journals become less and less willing to publish
6       site reports at all, unless they are framed within a suitably academic, research
7       agenda (C. Evans, pers. comm.).
8          Turning ﬁnally to more general works, in light of Hodder’s comments
9       we can interestingly compare his most inﬂuential theoretical text, Reading
20111   the Past, with David Clarke’s Analytical Archaeology in terms of textual style.
1       Clarke’s is a massive text, the second edition cut down to over 500 pages
2       with justiﬁed text, highly structured chapters and numbered sections
3       reﬂecting the philosophy he is putting forward: a very systematic book. By
4       contrast, Hodder has written a fairly brief work of under 200 pages with
5       unjustiﬁed text, each chapter treating an almost separate theme, themes
6       which are, nevertheless, interwoven by various connecting ideas. The differ-
7       ences in the two styles are clearly linked to their authors’ theoretical outlook,
8       their perspectives clearly affecting the mode of textualisation.
9          The three examples of textualisation – ﬁeldnotes, site reports and general
30111   works – all show how the process of textual production is mediated by and
1       inﬂuences the way we perceive archaeology. Moreover, just as we can think
2       about the rhetoric or poetics of texts, it is equally important to think of
3       them as material products – as books or digital displays. Indeed, the wide-
4       spread adoption of computers and word-processing software has undoubtedly
5       altered the way we write – the ease with which we can self-edit our texts
6       on computers compared with written or typed manuscripts may have affected
7       the whole process of textual production. Perhaps of equal interest, however,
8       is to consider the form of a text – its structure in terms of layout and typog-
9       raphy. We can all instantly tell the difference between a serious academic
40111   text and a coffee-table book for lay consumption – format size, text to
1       image ratio, layout of the text and images and so on. Would a site report
2       look more interesting if it was in the style of the fashion magazine Vogue
3       I wonder? More importantly, perhaps, what would thinking about using this
44111   format do to the way we write the site report?
        206    Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    Imaging archaeology
2       If textual production pervades all areas of the archaeological process, the
3       same is true of image production through drawings and photographs. Just
4       as forms of textual production have altered, so archaeological illustration
5       has seen some interesting changes over the past century (Piggott 1965). In
6       1828 E. T. Artis excavated a series of Roman sites near Peterborough in
7       eastern England, which he published as a series of plates (Artis 1828). It
8       is an interesting example because it reveals something of how archaeolog-
9       ical sites were perceived in the early nineteenth century – as part of a
1011    Historical/Romantic landscape. It is worth quoting Piggott at length on this
1       issue, because he reinforces this observation:
2
3             One cannot say that any discernible archaeological style of draughts-
4             manship came into being in the eighteenth, or really the nineteenth
5             century, and one would be surprised if it had. . . . Not unnaturally, the
6             delineation of antiquities, sites or architecture conformed to the conven-
7             tions of non-specialised draughtsmanship at large. Perspective views of
8             ﬁeld monuments – ‘prospects’, the equivalent of modern photographs
9             – fell naturally within the developing traditions of the topographical
20111         artists of the day, and indeed the depiction of antiquities played an
1             important part with almost all the early landscape painters. . . . Plans
2             were drawn and engraved according to the prescriptions of estate
3             surveyors and cartographers; small antiquities were illustrated as if they
4             were butterﬂies or petrifactions or prodigies of nature which might well
5             have accompanied them in the cabinet of curiosities of an ingenious
6             gentlemen.
7                                                                    (Piggott 1965: 171)
8
9       However, such a series of ‘prospects’, is not what Petrie or Pitt Rivers would
30111   have had in mind. Their concern was more abstract – to represent an
1       object, not a view. It is the visual equivalent of Hodder’s example of the
2       passive voice – while the view retains a sense of a viewer, the plan and
3       section erase it.
4          This even spills over into the ﬁnds; earlier depictions were usually per-
5       spective ﬁgures, often painted, much in the style of curios as Piggott remarks.
6       One might also venture that such depictions could have been inﬂuenced
7       by contemporary retail catalogues of goods (see Figure 21). However, with
8       Pitt Rivers there is great concern for scale and accuracy and each sherd is
9       accompanied by its section, which anticipated present-day illustrations; here
40111   the standard half-section of pottery so familiar today derives from
1       Dragendorff’s famous typology of Samian (1895), which became widely
2       adopted by the early part of the twentieth century (Piggott 1965: 175). Such
3       representations have, however, come to be contested recently – for example,
44111
                                               Conclusion: material archaeologies   207

1111

3111

30111
1
2

4
5
6
7       Figure 21 Late nineteenth-century advertisement for gas brackets
8
9
40111   many of the conventions derive from nineteenth- and early twentieth-
1       century reproduction technology (e.g. engraving, lithography), while the
2       scope of contemporary computer-aided design is only just beginning to be
3       exploited (Hughes 1999).
44111
        208   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111       With the establishment of scale drawings, new concerns emerged over
2       what was the best way to draw the site. The most famous debate about graph-
3       ics in British ﬁeld archaeology was over ﬁeld drawings and between those
4       who favoured a ‘naturalistic’ approach after Bersu or a more schematic one
5       after Wheeler (Piggott 1965: 175–6; Bradley 1997: 68–70). While Bersu’s
6       sections (and plans) attempted to show a pit ﬁll ‘as it was’, Wheeler used
7       more stylistic conventions, most importantly a hard or sharp line to distin-
8       guish different layers and cuts (Figure 22). In general, Wheeler’s approach
9       has dominated because of the way British ﬁeld archaeology adopted the idea
1011    of discrete stratigraphic units in its excavation procedure. Again, the current
1       use of computers in combination with total-stations changes the whole way
2       the graphic record on site is made – potentially, one does not need to draw
3       any plans at all as the whole process is automated and can be linked in to
4       ﬁnd spots, etc. (e.g. Biswell et al. 1995). However, such a method, while
5       increasing speed and accuracy, loses in its inability to deal with ambiguity –
6       it is the graphic record reduced to a series of lines and dots.
7          Other forms of imaging include mathematical ﬁgures – graphs, charts and
8       tables. As Clark and Stafford (1982) showed with respect to North American
9       archaeology, simple descriptive quantiﬁcation is as old as the discipline.
20111   The same undoubtedly applies to British archaeology – basic tabular presen-
1       tation of data can be seen in many studies from the later nineteenth century
2       onwards (e.g. Greenwell’s British Barrows, published in 1877, or Pitt Rivers’
3       Relic Tables), and it is still a commonly used representation of quantitative
4       data. The use of charts or graphs is more recent – in an interesting study
5       of graphic representation of seriation between the 1920s and 1960s it was
6       shown how there was a gradual shift from the use of tables to charts, but
7       more signiﬁcantly, perhaps, many of the early charts were effectively intu-
8       itive depictions of the data rather than accurate representations (Lyman et
9       al. 1998). Indeed, good graphs are far more persuasive rhetorical devices
30111   than tables, which are now frequently conﬁned to appendices or micro-
1       ﬁche. As the 1960s ushered in a new wave of more complex, inferential
2       statistics based on probability theory and testing, a whole new range of
3       graphic images was introduced into archaeological texts to aid arguments
4       – indeed, one might say that in many instances they are an essential compo-
5       nent of any data analysis.
6          A common assumption of much of the imaging discussed here – partic-
7       ularly drawings and photography – is the importance of objectivity. While
8       their selective nature is recognised, there is still a strong sense that accuracy
9       and precision are critical – the use of scales and proper measuring equipment
40111   is an important component in the materialisation of such images. The
1       objectivity of these kinds of images is accentuated when they are juxtaposed
2       with artistic reconstructions. In archaeology, the question of reconstruc-
3       tions of past life has recently received considerable attention, particularly
44111
                                          Conclusion: material archaeologies   209

1111
2
3
4
5
6
7
8
9
1011
1
2
3111
4
5
6
7
8
9
20111
1
2
3
4
5
6
7
8
9
30111
1
2
3
4
5
6
7
8
9
40111
1
2
3
44111   Figure 22 Wheeler’s and Bersu’s section drawings
        210    Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    in the realm of early hominids and how gender stereotypes are represented
2       (Moser 1992; Moser and Gamble 1997; Wiber 1998). While this issue is
3       about the political and social nature of such images, it also raises the ques-
4       tion of their objectivity. Alongside these critiques lie internal debates about
5       how to reconstruct an image without going too far beyond the evidence
6       (e.g. James 1997). A recent textbook on archaeological illustration recom-
7       mends that the artist use the archaeological evidence to the full, and exploit
8       devices such as obstacles of perspective which can cover up any gaps in
9       our knowledge (Adkins and Adkins 1989: 145–7). Thus although their
1011    status as artistic impressions is recognised, there is still an important ethic
1       of constraint. However, the history of models illustrates how such recon-
2       structions can shift in meaning.
3          The use of models today is generally as museum pieces showing the site
4       as it might have looked in the past, a kind of three-dimensional version
5       of artistic impressions. However, it was not always like this (Evans n.d.).
6       Pitt Rivers’ use of models for example was quite different. He employed a
7       carpenter to render many of his sites in this form; these models were not
8       simply artistic recreations, but a part of his actual excavation archive, a
9       record of the archaeology in three dimensions instead of the usual two. It
20111   cannot be too far-fetched to suggest that he perceived these as being the
1       best representation he had of the sites he excavated. From the 1930s and
2       1940s full-scale models or re-creations of structures were also considered an
3       important part of understanding the archaeology, but not as a record so
4       much as an experiment revealing information on the constructional prop-
5       erties of, say, roundhouses, allowing for guided interpretation on things such
6       as the pitch of a roof. This kind of work too has more or less gone out of
7       vogue, and models today have a status similar to that of artistic impressions.
8          This tension over objectivity in imaging is evocatively illustrated in
9       Cook’s voyage to the Paciﬁc:
30111
1             [C]onsidering that such expeditions left Europe each time with an
2             anticipation of confronting the unknown, one can readily imagine
3             the difﬁculties inherent in having to decide, in advance, what one’s
4             criteria might be for representing something of which, at present,
5             one has no experience – that is, for apprehending the exotic. When
6             Joseph Banks was charged by the Royal Society to ﬁnd illustrators
7             for Cook’s ﬁrst voyage to the Paciﬁc in 1768, he resolved the
8             problem as both the scientist and the gentleman that he was. He
9             would have one illustrator, Sidney Parkinson, provide faithful
40111         copies of plants and animals, and another artist, Alexander Buchan,
1             provide him pictures of savages and scenery that would delight his
2             friends back home.
3                                                                 (Napier 1992: 9)
44111
                                              Conclusion: material archaeologies   211

1111    Archaeology reproduces this dilemma, not just between the site plan and
2       the artist’s reconstruction, but even within these modes. The same tension
3       is played up by Shanks in a recent paper concerning photography (Shanks
4       1997). Shanks argues for viewing photography as photowork, in which it
5       mediates between its role as a record and as a creation through techniques
6       of collage and montage (Shanks 1997: 83–4). I ﬁnd his discussion here
7       extremely useful when thinking about the nature of imaging, in that through
8       the selectivity of the frame and the juxtaposition of fragments, photography
9       makes a new reality, one which does not refer outside of itself as suggested
1011    in the record or the artistic impression. It is particularly in the idea that
1       photoworks create their own reality that they circumvent the whole tension
2       of objectivity in representation, and it seems to me that they do this because
3111    they are viewed as materialising practices, not simply as passive represen-
4       tations. A good example is the use of photography with computer-designed
5       images to make ‘reconstructions’ – much like the technology used in ﬁlms
6       to juxtapose actors with computer-generated dinosaurs (Lamb 1999).
7
8
        Materialising archaeology
9
20111   Despite the production of texts and images having been discussed sepa-
1       rately, they have always been used together in archaeology. However, the
2       relationship between the two has changed, just as each mode of material-
3       isation has changed. In Chapter 2, we discussed the esteem in which Pitt
4       Rivers was held by later archaeologists (especially Wheeler) for his recording.
5       What is really interesting about his record, especially the four volumes on
6       Cranbourne Chase, is that they are all arranged around the plates. All the
7       text is (albeit often extended) annotations to the ﬁgures, and this includes
8       not just the ﬁnds but also the site features. It is as if, in his conception,
9       the site was best represented by drawings and only secondarily by text. This
30111   is supported by a saying attributed to him: ‘Describe your illustrations, do
1       not illustrate your descriptions’ (Piggott 1965: 174). This is almost the
2       inverse of today’s approach which is heavily textually dominated. Is Pitt
3       Rivers unusual, though? Not really, for Petrie felt the same and indeed
4       actually spelt it out: ‘the text is to show the meaning and relation of the
5       facts already expressed by form’ (Petrie 1904: 115). For Petrie, it was
6       the lack of ﬁgures in older archaeological work that was their chief failing
7       – Greenwell’s British Barrows, for example, he felt could be reduced almost
8       entirely to a series of plans – it was too text-laden (ibid.: 114).
9          The visual record was thus perhaps seen as the most important part of the
40111   archaeological record in the latter part of the nineteenth century, as that
1       which most accurately represents it. To further support this, one might cite
2       Petrie’s effusive recommendation on the use of photography and, perhaps
3       even more tellingly, Pitt Rivers’ use of scale models. Despite the preference
44111
        212   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    for seeing the visual record as the best way of representing the archaeology,
2       Petrie’s ideal of a site report consisting solely of ﬁgures was never realised.
3       Instead, by the 1920s and 1930s the standard site report was established and
4       has changed little up to the present except in detail. In effect, a balance of
5       interlinked graphic and textual representation is offered. However, with the
6       advent of computer-aided publication, this balance may be about to change.
7       The very boundaries between text and image can be broken down, through
8       the use of annotated and ‘cartoon’ style graphics in conventional publica-
9       tions (e.g. Hamilton 1996), but even more so in the ﬁeld of hypermedia. This
1011    will certainly have consequences for the way archaeology is presented and,
1       ultimately perhaps, feed back into the way it is practised – certainly the use
2       of hypertext can be linked to non-linear types of narrative for example
3       (Hodder 1999).
4           I want to close with a reminder as to what is perhaps the most basic
5       materialising practice in archaeology – the creation of the archaeological
6       record. By this, I am saying nothing more than that we actually have to
7       go out and actively ﬁnd our data – primarily by digging, but also through
8       surface, aerial and geophysical survey. What perhaps distinguishes archae-
9       ology from other disciplines such as history, anthropology or even other
20111   material culture studies, is that it involves an operation of materialisation.
1       In all these other disciplines, the material or data is already there – only
2       in archaeology is there a performance of presencing, materialising the data.1
3       Beyond this, of course, all disciplines create their data in the qualiﬁed sense
4       of data being theory-dependent, but this other aspect, of materialisation, is
5       something rarely raised. I am not so sure it is unique to archaeology, but
6       I will leave this for the moment; instead, I want to ask what relationship
7       this mode of materialisation in archaeology has to those of textualisation
8       and imaging already discussed.
9           To answer this, I will use as an example a formulation of the archaeo-
30111   logical record made in Britain in the 1970s, which employed a hierarchy
1       of different levels distilling a process of representation (see Table 7; Frere
2       1975). First there is the Level I archive, the site itself, which exists inde-
3       pendently of and prior to any investigation. Beyond Level I lie the various
4       representations we make of that site: the primary archive, analyses and
5       ﬁnally publication. Critically, the ﬂow is reversible in most cases except
6       usually between Levels I and II – we can go back to the primary archive
7       or the analyses, but we can never re-excavate. Fundamentally, the
8       reversibility of the lowest levels justiﬁes the irreversibility of the upper, that
9       is, excavation. That the hierarchy permits iteration, repeatability, is a funda-
40111   mental axiom of scientiﬁc ‘ﬁeldwork’, and in a way this barrier between
1       Level I and the others validates the whole schema, because if it was not
2       there, there would be no stopping point or point of rest. This point is Level
3       I, archaeological reality, Archaeology Degree Zero.
44111
                                                     Conclusion: material archaeologies          213

1111    Table 7 Levels of archaeological data as proposed by the Frere and Cunliffe report
        (Frere, 1975)
2
3       Level Site description                            Loose material
4
5       I     The site itself and general notes, old      Excavated finds
              letters, previous accounts, etc.
6
7       II    Site notebooks, recording forms,            Finds records, X-rays, photographs,
8             drawings, sound-recorded tapes              negatives, colour transparencies
9       III   Full illustration and description of        Classified finds – lists and finds –
1011          all structural relationships                drawings, all analyses
1       IV    Synthesis of descriptions with              Selected finds and specialist reports
2             supporting data                             relevant to synthesis
3111
4          But consider a geophysical survey. It might show perhaps an enclosure
5       ditch, without us ever having seen or felt it. The enclosure exists only
6       in the greyscale plot and we can read it precisely because we have seen
7       this kind of representation a thousand times before on site plans of
8       excavated features. How does the geophysical plot differ from the measured
9       plan? Both are representations – in the former of something which
20111   has never been seen and in the latter of something which can never be
1       seen again. What is the difference? With excavation, we might argue that
2       the site is real, was actually there, and we can claim to make a distinction
3       between the record we make of it and the site itself – but with the
4       geophysical survey, the site remains a virtual site, the record and the site
5       itself are no longer distinct. How could you argue that the site exists
6       independently of your record in a geophysical survey, when the only proof
7       you have of its existence is that survey? This is an interesting paradox
8       but it perhaps also confuses another issue – our use of material culture in
9       materialising the past. The resistivity meter is an ‘artiﬁcial eye’; it
30111   sees where we cannot. I think the important point here is that the
1       proof of a geophysical survey would come through repetition – either through
2       comparison with another survey or with an excavation plan. In this it
3       highlights an important point which in fact goes against the whole ethos
4       of this model: that the very validity of archaeological interpretation is
5       not based on the independence of an archaeological reality (Level I, the
6       site or objects themselves), but on the iterability of the representations
7       we give it.
8          For example, even in those cases where old sites can be re-excavated,
9       it is the comparison of plans that assesses the validity of the ﬁrst
40111   interpretation – we can never compare their perception directly with our
1       own, only the materialisations of that perception. It is these materialisa-
2       tions – of texts and images – which guarantee the validity of archaeological
3       interpretation, not the site itself. These materialising practices are not simply
44111
        214   Critical approaches to fieldwork

1111    representations but materialising strategies for enabling the archaeological
2       record to be subject to repeated investigation. They create the possibility
3       of iterability. And, like any materialisation, they have a certain indepen-
4       dence, a certain life of their own – indeed this is a cornerstone of
5       post-structuralist theory in relation to texts. This is why archaeologists can
6       re-examine old sites and come up with new interpretations, as in the exam-
7       ples given in the last chapter of Glastonbury or Snaketown.
8          Image and textual materialisation enable comparison, but, more signiﬁ-
9       cantly, repeatable comparison. The records we make in archaeology are our
1011    answer to the scientiﬁc experiment – it is a way of re-excavating a site, re-
1       analysing an object over and over again. This is why we call excavation
2       destruction – we have to, otherwise the record we make is of no value.
3       Without these materialising practices, there would be no archaeology –
4       they allow its discourse to develop. Indeed, the archaeologists’ facts are
5       primarily not sites or artefacts, but the textual and graphic materialisations
6       which stand in for them – from ﬁeldnotes to site reports, from drawings to
7       photographs (see Duke 1991). In this way, the concept of materiality and
8       material culture is introduced into the previously closed dualism of theory
9       and practice. It is this materiality which actually enables the hermeneutic
20111   to function as an iterable process.
